THE RED PAGE.
Newer Australian Books.
Stephen Kyrle, by Mrs. T. R. Andrews (Melb.,
Geo* Robertson). A remarkable ebullition in
lxxvii chapters and 383 solid pages, on the infan-
tile plane—Victoria sent to the Diclcens via
Harriet Beecher Stowe. The title-hero “stood
some six feet four inches in his socks, and he was
as strong, muscular, and supple as a Roman
gladiator. His head, held erect in a way that
suggested strength and courage rather than pride,
was shapely, and large in proportion to his great
height." He lands in Melbourne in 1852, goes to
Spring Creek diggings, fails, succeeds, drinks,
gets into gaol, gets out, and is finally (in quite
modern times) saved from a “life” sentence at
Pentridge by the remarkable talent as a sculptor
which his gold-digging and bush-ranging career
did not in the least prepare us for. For the body
of Stephen Kyrle two women strive—a dark bad
angel, who gets him for a long time ; and a
fair good angel (with singular mawkish affinities to
Little Nell), who gets him when he comes out of
Pentridge. The book fishes everything reasonable
and unreasonable into its net, and is full of good
morals and strange episodes (two chapters are de-
voted entirely to the death and funeral of the
good little girl’s good little dog). So ’tis odd if
the “ Wild Colonial Boy ” audience does not read
Stephen Kyrle with satisfaction.
The Wisdom of Esau, by R. L. Outhwaite and
C. H. Chomley (Melb., E. W. Cole). Life on a
Vic. mallee farm, with all the incidents, and a
fair plot to make them more palatable. Not an
inspired book; but a piece of solid, conscientious
workmanship, with interest enough to be read,
and value in its illumination of an aspect of Aus-
tralian life. A body of similar books would form
a very acceptable second rank in our Coming
Literature.
The Wonder-Child, by Ethel Turner (Lond.,
The Religious Tract Society). Another of this
friendly writer’s stories of children gambolling
against a fainter background of adults. The local
musical phenomenon is worked in ; also the Afri-
can war and the Bush Contingent. Mrs. Cur-
lewis’s talent and style enable her to make read-
able story-copy from the common paragraphs in
our newspapers. Perhaps her treatment grows a
trifle mechanical ; but her interest is maintained
as formerly.
The Warrigals ’ Well, by Donald Macdonald and
J. F. Edgar (Lond., Ward, Lock and Co.), bears
on the cover the name of Donald Macdonald only
—which seems decidedly unfair to lately-deceased
Edgar. It is unfair also to the public, who may
purchase the book under the impression that it
was written by Macdonald solely. This is pro-
bably the impression sought for ; since Macdonald
has won some reputation by his account of
the Ladysmith siege, and Edgar is com-
paratively unknown. Edgar gets his due
credit on the title-page and in a preface;
but Macdonald should see that he gets it every-
where where authorship is announced. These
little devices are familiar to the less scrupulous
class of publishers ; but reputable writers should
not countenance them. And Edgar is dead.
Not, unfortunately, that The Warrigals 1 Well
(the alliterative title has nothing to do with the
story) brings credit either to Edgar or to Mac-
donald. J. F. Elgar was a bright jour-
nalist ; Donald Macdonald is a serious jour-
nalist ; and their book does not rise above the
plane of journalism. The story tells how the
agents of a London syndicate discovered a gold
mine in the Northern Territory; but it is so
overlaid with extrinsic incident that it loses
all narrative interest. Instead of a breathless
recital of adventure, we have a hotchpotch
of descriptions of fauna and flora, a gold-
robbery from a mail steamer, encounters with
blacks, a badly-managed cipher episode, and a
great deal of dull intrigue. Almost throughout,
the book is heavily written ; and the characters
are shadows who never become really alive. The
gold-mine is found on paper ; but it is a lead-mine
that is offered to the public in print. The idea
and the materials are good; but they needed
better hands to write the story.
Love and Longitude, by R. Scot Skirving (Syd-
ney, Angus and Robertson), is another example of
that restless energy, that formidable enterprise,
which carry the Scottish race to the front of
the World’s Work. It represents the leisure in-
cursions into literature of an Edinburgh medico
well known in Sydney. For the rest it is a moral
and informing representation of what passes with
sober-minded people for romance, and, unjess for
the gratification of the author, there is no great
reason why it should have been printed. The
appendix on navigation is verra humourous.
When Winds Awake, by W. M. Whitney
(Sydney, Wm. Brooks & Co.) is a collection of
commonplace verses without literary title to per-
petuation. D. H. Souter contributes five decora-
tive illustrations, of which “Pegasus” is less
stiff than the ugly others. The effort to print the
booklet attractively deserves praise, though the
success is doubtful.
Recent Reading.
Several of “McGv’s” “Men Who Write The
Bulletin ” are now travelling Australia; and the
other day the police were so impressed by one of
them that they walked into the office and de-
manded assurance of his good faith. On being
assured that the Man was really “Titus Salt”
and “Silas Snell” and all the other local heroes
rolled into one, and did really (as he seems to-
have claimed) write the leaders and the verses
and the wild-cat column, the police representative
borrowed the third edition of Ellis’s The Criminal
and departed. Having brought The Criminal
back like an honest police person, and being
invited to contribute a commentary, the Force
affirmed that the official habit of silence had so
grown upon it that it could not open its lips
even when it tried ; but, generally not-speaking,
it thought that if Ellis relied less upon books and
more on the living model he would get his subject
in better perspective. And there may be some-
thing in that. Nevertheless, as a handbook to
the science of Crime, Ellis’s book is the best
available by the ordinary English reader; and
this new edition is considerably enlarged. The
cardinal deductions remain unchanged by re-
vision—
The only education that can avail to prevent crime in
any substantial degree must be education in the true
sense, an education that is as much physical and moral
as intellectual, an education that enables him who has it
to play a fair part in social life. . . We seem to be
approaching a point at which it will become obvious that
every citizen must be educated to perform some useful
social function. . .
All education must include provision for the detection
;*nd special treatment of abnormal children. We cannot
"catch our criminals too young. . .
The making of children is the highest of all human
functions, and that which carries the most widespread
and incalculable consequences. It is well to remember
that every falling away from health, every new strain and
stress, in man or woman, ma.v lay an additional burden
on a man or woman vet unborn, and perhaps wreck a life
or a succession of lives.
Ellis asks particularly/after Joe Brass’s (Svrlney)
Confessions of a Thief, parts ii and lit. Can any-
one say if these were ever published ? or did not
Joe s romantic work (not literally to be relied on
say the authorities) exhaust itself with the first
part ?
Frederic Harrison’s views on the proclamation
of martial law in Cape Colony— •
Speaking as a lawyer, as one who had had occasion to
study constitutional law for thirty years, he advisedly laid
down three propositions, all of which were, he said, sus-
tained by the highest of English Judges, from Coke to
Lord Chief Justice Cockburn.
T re i ~ l ‘ That if a ".V British subject
r?,L U tt° death not in war, not as a spy, and not in the
course of actual military operations, but put to death
after a mock trial for treason, or for some such civil
offence, every man who ordered, aided, or abetted in his
death, was liable to be tried by a jury on a charge of
murder. He was thus liable, even after years had
elapsed, and although an Act of Indemnity might be
passed in general terms.
2. That unless any man charged with such death could
satisfy a jury of his countrymen that the act was a bona-
nae military necessity for the carrying out of some direct
military operation, and was not a mere act of revenge of
terrorism, or of usurpation of civil law, the accused,
whether soldier or civilian, general or governor, was un-
doubtedly guilty of murder.
_3. That if any mock court-martial arrogated to itself
the prerogative of civil government and pretended to
pass sentences of penal servitude, such proceedings were
not only null and void, but were in themselves crimes,
and every man—soldier or civilian, officer of "justice or
police-vyho attempted to give effect to such sentences,
who unlawfully arrested, tried, or imprisoned such
prisoners upon such civil charges, was himself liable to
criminal justice.
We could not, said Mr. Harrison, play fast and loose
with English law. If we did, it vanished. Defy the prin-
ciples of liberty under the law, and there would soon be no
principles left to defy. What was done in Cape Colony
to-day might be done in Ireland to-morrow, and in Ena-
land the day after.
Where was anarchy going to stop when once proclaimed
°f. British soil ? If law were to be abolished in South
Africa by a single Minister in his orchid house while his
colleagues were playing golf or amusing themselves at
Monte Carlo, why might it not be abolished the next day
in Australia or m Canada by some Minister, or even in
Ireland, Scotland, or England ?
He would ask on what was the Throne, the House of
Lords, or Parliament, or civil government going to be
based hereafter if the most venerable conditions of British
freedom were to be trampled upon because our soldiers
could not get the better of 50,000 Dutch farmers ?
Mill’s “Liberty,” and Descartes’ “Method”
and “Metaphysical Meditations,” are new volumes
m the Scott library at Is. 6d. Mill seems ever-
green ; and he is commended to the notice of the
Commonwealth Cabinet. The interest of Des-
cartes is nowadays chiefly personal and historical:
science has passed him.
Writes William Archer—
Let us suppose Mr. Maldonado called upon to justify in
cold blood his little ebullition of temper [in Pinero’s new
play, when he calls England “the suburb of the uni-
verse ”]—what would he say ? His case, I fancy, would
run something like this : “ What is the essence of suburb-
anism ? Is it not imperturbable self-satisfaction, inacces-
sibility to ideas, social cliqueishness and ineffi-
ciency (outside of a narrow routine) in practical affairs
amateurishness in art, inertia in politics, soullessness in
religion, and, in general, a sheepish, mechanical sub-
servience to the dictates of Mrs. Grundy? What
is suburbanism, if not this? And what, if not this
is the English character? You plume yourselves’
with John of Gaunt, upon your insularity, and
yet you cannot endure to be told that you are insular
Because you live in an island you think (and it has
hitherto been true, in a sense) that you can afford to be
second-rate in everything ; and oh ! how second-rate you
contrive to be ! You are second-rate in statesmanship
generalship, scholarship ; second-rate in science, in art’
in literature, in commerce, in engineering. Even in the
sports to which you pay a mad devotion you are being
forced to take a second place. What can be more
dismally suburban than your Royal Academy, unless
it be your popular theatres, with their garish vulgarity
and their sloppy sentimentality ? When by chance a
really fine thing is done among you, your moral paladins
are shocked and your aesthetic pundits set themselves to
carp, and belittle, and pick holes, in the truest spirit of
suburbanism. Morally, aesthetically, intellectually, your
national note is self-complacent inefficiency. Again I
declare that England is the suburb of the universe ; and
if you try to drown my remark by blaring out ‘’Rule
Britannia,’ I add that you thereby prove my case.”
Maurice Hewlett’s New Canterbury Tales show
some loss of power and charm as compared with
earlier books— Little Novels of Italy, for example.
The trick of style is less well justified, and the
author’s vitality throbs less keenly. The Prioress’
tale of a Christian child done (almost) to death in
Jewry gives a few pages to modify this judgment;
but the others, though deserving, have not the
high desert we have known. Occasionally a
gallant line comes—
... a moonfaced, sidling, cuddling, snoozleing, snug-
gling, coaxing, adoring, mothering, greensick little slip
of delicacy, fifteen years old, and no more, by Cock and
the Prophet Jonah . . . (133)
Note a rare possessive—
... a head of brown hair softer and sunnier than anv’s
of his own family. ... (268)
The sixth edition of the Orient-Pacific Line
Guide, a handsome 400-page volume with many
maps and illustrations, merits every commenda-
tion. It is well written, admirably printed, and
of absorbing interest to tourists.
Another new book is published for Louis Becke
(pandering to the B.P. with his tongue in his
cheek) — By Rock and Pool. Becke’s work nowa-
days is comparatively poor—acceptable journalism
unworthy of perpetuation. Few can write so
much as he, and continue to write well. Here is
a Becke bibliography for correction—

Mutineer (with Walter Jeffery), Sydney and London,

Pioneers of Australia (with Walter Jefferv), London

And this, they say, is the way American poli-
ticians make their speeches :
Three weeks before the late President McKinlev left
Canton for Buffalo Exhibition he and Mr. Cortelyou" were
sitting in the study, the President smoking and the
Secretary at his desk reading papers, and neither of them
speaking. Suddenly the President took his cigar from
his lips and said :
Expositions are the timekeepers of progress.
Mr. Cortelyou wrote down the epigram, but said
The President smoked a few minutes, and then remarked
apropos of nothing at all: ’
Amity is better than animosity.
The Secretary made another note, and Mr. McKinlev
lapsed into his thoughtful mood. Suddenly he again
broke the silence with : *
Reciprocity is better than retaliation.
The President finished his cigar, went upstairs, and
returned to find on his table on a large sheet of paper
the three phrases, one at the top, one in the middle and
the third lower down, type-written. Mr. McKinley ’read
the epigrams, and turning to Mr. Cortelyou, said, with a
smile, “ We have begun the Buffalo speech, I see!”
Away from home we hear the news—
The Australians have more slang phrases than the
Americans (says the Baltimore Sun). Their most common
ejaculation is “My word ! ” Among other slang phrases
are the words “ screw ” for salary or income, “narked”
•J a , nsTy > “ cush ” for comfortable, and “putting on
side ” for putting on airs. If a man is assaulted by high-
waymen and robbed he is “ stuck up,” and if he has no
money whatever it is common to say he “ has not a
bean.” “ Good iron ”is an expression of incredulity at a
preposterous story. People ask you to “hang up your
horse ” instead of hitching it. If a person fails “he has
gone bung,” and if he is well off “he is pretty well in.”
Americans use the expressions “on the jump” or “on
the go”; the Australian says he is “on the wallaby.”
An Australian dude is a “ tofi,” a tramp is a “ swapman ”
or a. “sundowner.” Luggage is always called “swag,”
and the common word for food is “ tucker.”
Asquith s sarcastic reference to Lond. Spectator's
blunder over the Rhodes cheque incident—
A respectable weekly newspaper of blameless antece-
dents and growing infirmities was selected to be at once
its vehicle and its victim. It was a piquant variation upon
those stories about cocks and bulls and other eccentric
members of the animal creation with which that journal
was in the habit of regaling its unsophisticated readers.
This must make the ghost of Richard Hutton
walk uneasily.

The Inky Way.
Proposed Q. legislation in regard to Sunday
papers penalises the publishing, distributing, selling, or cir-
culating of any newspaper on Sunday first offence, fine
£10; second offence, fine £lOO ; third offence, fine £2OO and
the plant becomes forfeitable. An exception is made in
favour of a Sunday special giving* “ pressing news of great
public importance,” but no paper can publish more than
three such Sunday specials in one year. In Westralia,
legislation to silence the two existing Sunday sheets is
promised. In Sydney, it has been pertinently asked why
the citizen who buys Sunday papers cannot get a glass of
milk or an orange for his children. The spasm of reac-
tionary legislation and reactionary sentiment which is
crossing Australia just now, is worthy of the Middle Ages.
And now that Sunday trams run in Edinburgh, the
crusade against the Sunday newspaper is reallv too
stupid.
Lismore has now a daily paper—the Evening
Post. There are several country towns in N.S.W.
with dailies, usually anaemic little sheets. At
Bathurst, whose adult male population can he
judged from the fact that it returns only one member to
the State Assembly, there are three daily newspapers.
Broken Hill, with three or four times the male popula-
tion, has only one daily, and it is able to be a creditable
production. In the old days, before the concentration of
interest in the N.S.W. capital, there were several pro-
vincial papers which rivalled chose of Sydney in import-
ance. Under Federation energetic country newspapers
ought to regain their old influence—to the profit of the
community. But the curse of the country press is the
extreme competition. So soon as a paper begins to pro-
vide a bare living for its proprietor-editor-manager,
another one springs up to cut into the business. The
country newspapers, if they studied their own interests,
would concentrate. With one paper where there are now
two or three there would be a living in provincial press-
work.
Max O’Rell has settled down to join the edi-
torial staff of Paris Figaro.
Journalist Galbraith, Thames (M.L.), has in-
vented a shorthand system, and claims verbatim pro-
ficiency in 12 months. It is the dream of many a news-
paper man to invent a new shorthand system, but so far
Isaac Pitman’s position is not seriously threatened.
The West Australian, the conservative journal
of Groperdom, claims \to have first enlightened the
Westralians in 1833, and is junior only to the Sydney
Herald, amongst Australian dailies. The W.A. recently,
with much speechifying and drinking, started a new three-
decker Foster machine. Lady Lawley, wife of the Gov.,
set the new machine in motion and named it King
Edward VII!
Francis Lawley, a distinguished journalist who
died in London last November, was at one time appointed
Governor of N.S.W. Originally private secretary to
Gladstone, Lawley lost heavily in Stock Exchange specu-
lations, and, to give him a chance to recoup his losses,
Gladstone offered him the Governorship of N.S.W. But
the appointment caused a rancorous debate in the
Commons, and was cancelled. Lawley then took to
journalism, and was Times correspondent during the
American Civil War.
-S'. M. Herald has sent a special commissioner
(Kirby, a former sub-editor) to inquire into the Kanaka
traffic in Queensland.
“Vulcan”: Re Mahbub Ali’s “When I was
fifteen I had shot my man and begot my man!” S.M.
Herald printed neither “that” nor much more of the
kind. Such bits are as the spice to Kipling’s curry—
S.M.H. dished up hash. I confess to having spent a
humoursome half-hour in comparing the expurgated
S.M.H. version with the real Kim.
Steve O’Brien, a Bulletin contributor and lately
sub. of Brisb. Worker, has gone to edit the famous Eagle
at Charters Towers. Part of the history of the Eagle is
written in blood and beer, not without blasphemy.
Various lively scribes have had a finger in the paper’s
destiny ; and a former Bulletin poet, the six-named Von
Kotze, proudly recites his share. But Won Kotze was a
mild editor comparatively.
Of The Golden Fleece, a new Sydney wool-trade
review, F. J. Broomfield makes quite a creditable maga-
zine-graced with resplendent allusions to all the Arts
and many of the Sciences.

A LETTER FROM A MINING ENGINEE
“Ontario,” Nicholson Street,
Burwood, N.S.W.,
March 8,1900.
Gentlemen,—
“On the Ist Sept., 1898, I became over-heated,
then suddenly exposed to a piercing cold wind, I
caught a chill, the result being that I was deprived
of robust health, and for about seven months my
life was despaired of. During this time I had been
through the hands of eight medical men in private
and public hospitals. The majority of them held
out no hope, some of them declaring that I had
kidney, liver, heart and lung troubles. In the last
Hospital, my medical attendant said 1 had Bright s
Disease of the kidneys, that he could do nothing
for me, and that I had better be taken home, as I
could not recover. Rather a dark prospect this,
considering that, in addition to the troubles
enumerated above, I had dropsy to a fearful extent.
I had been tapped in the lower part of the abdomen
five times, several gallons of water being drawn off,
but all to no good purpose. In a few days after
being tapped, I swelled up in my legs, abdomen,
and stomach, larger than before. The dropsy
began to make its appearance in my hands and face.
My body swelled to that degree that I sickened to
look at myself, and many times wished I could die.
Friends who visited me sympathised and bid me,
as they believed, a last farewell. But an angel of
mercy came in the shape of Warner’s Safe Cure.
A lady friend strongly recommended me to give up
hospitals, doctors and medicines, and take only
Warner’s Safe Cure. On the 3rd April, 1899, I
began to take Warner’s Safe Cure. A.s soon as I
began to take this medicine my appetite began to
return. At the end of four weeks, when I had
drank four bottles of Safe Cure, the water began to
come away. I reduced in circumference around
the abdomen from 54.jf inches to 41| inches, and my
legs reduced in proportion. I felt like a new man.
My spirits returned. I began to get regular sleep,
still reducing in size until not a vestige of lung,
kidney, liver or dropsy trouble could be seen or
felt. Tam now in the best of health, and am firmly
convinced that Warner’s Safe Cure has, in God’s
hand, been the means of restoring my lost health
and strength. I would gladly write, talk, or smg
the praises of Warner’s Safe Cure, but will con-
elude by stating that I shall be only too happy to
answer any questions, either by letter or otherwise.
I feel I owe a duty to the world at large m making
known the almost miraculous cure effected in my
case by Warner’s Safe Cure. I also feel a deep
debt of gratitude to the discoverer of Warner s Safe
Cure, and trust this humble, unsolicited testimonial
may be made public for the benefit of suffering
humanity. I am well-known all over the Australian
Colonies, and my old mining contemporaries will
rejoice to learn of my restoration to health.
S. E. RICHARDS,
Mining Engineer.
Messrs. H. H. Warner & Co., Ltd.,
Melbourne, Vic.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A. Drover’s Dream Realised.
With the saddle for my pillow, and the dewy grass
my bed,
Not a roof to form a shelter but the starry sky
o’erhead:
I lay in peaceful slumber near the campfire’s ruddy
gleam,
And while I bathed in gentle sleep, there came to
me a dream.
In my vision there before me stretched the
Steeplechasing track,
The noisy Saddling Paddock, and the Grandstand
at my back ;
And I heard the “bookies” laying on Priora six
to four —
It was even money Playboy, twelve to one against
Galore.
I was riding Jimmy Gov’nor (that’s the name was
on my brain) —
Jetty black and shone like satin, he was of the
“ Outlaw” strain ;
I could hear the bell a-ringing, warning starters
to prepare
For the Christmas Hunting Contest, and there
was no time to spare.
The conditions of the contest were as follow after
this —
Each lady chose a rider, for his fee he charged a
kiss ;
The prize, a golden bracelet, was around the wrist
to twine
Of the lucky girl whose champion would be first
to cross the line.
There’s a maiden young and pretty with a head of
flaxen curls,
Her shapely lips are ruby-red, her'teeth are set
like pearls,
Her eyes are dew-wet violets, as pretty as can
be—
To ride and win the golden band she somehow
fancied me !
So I mounted Jimmy Gov’nor, and I rode him
past the stand ;
There my lady beckoned to me with her gingham
in her hand ;
When I rode across she whispered, “Ride the
best race of your life !
For if you win the bracelet, Jack, you’ll also win
a wife.”
Now her promise made me anxious, and my spirits
rose and flew,
And it seemed to raise the spirits of old Jimmy
Gov’nor, too.
For I felt the old horse tremble as we faced the
barrier start, ,
And his mighty heart was throbbing- like the
throbbing of my heart.
The flag went down, we started ! In my dream I
felt the force
Of her promise, that was urging me to send the
old black horse :
You bet I did, I sent him like a wild and sweep-
ing flood,
Till his sides were gored and blistered, and my
spurs were red with blood.
His jumping it was splendid, and he made the
pace so fast,
That before two miles were finished there were
only two could last ;
One was the veteran Fairfield : he galloped like
a toft ;
At my girth the Streak was racing, and I could
not shake him off.
In the straight I leaned and whispered, to the
grand old horse I spoke,
“If you win, I win a fortune, if you lose the race
I’m broke ;
’Tis not for their golden sovereigns that you face
this cruel strife,
But you’re racing, lad, to win for me a lovely little
wife! ”
And he seemed to understand me, for he tossed
the white foam back,
And he made a desperate effort down that
phantom racing track,
And he left the others standing—how romantic all
did seem —
He won the race and I a wife • • . alas, twas but
a dream !
When I wakened from my slumbers, and my
dream was realised,
I was utterly disgusted, disagreeably surprised ;
From my infancy to manhood a somnambulist I’ve
been,
The greatest sleeping prowler I, that ever you have
seen.
I had wandered in my slumbers, and was riding
on a log
That I straddled Toddy Sloan style, with my back
up like a frog ;
And my heels were bruised and blackened, just
from spurring blasted wood—
The way my thews and sinews ached can quite be
understood.
The maid for whom I’d ridden was a nasty, baldy
calf
That stood nibbling at my laces ; I declare it made
me laugh !
I had dreamed my lady kissed me— twas the calf
that licked my face—
As I rode that fallen gum-tree in that “waking-
sleeping ” race!
And now, when round the camp at night we tell
of dreams we’ve had,
Of dreams of love, of dreams of hate, of dreama
both good and bad,
Yeu’ll hear my mates laugh loud and long, when
through the smoky fog,
I tell them of that blessed race upon that cursed
log!
JOHNNY PINTPOT.

A witness at Collingwood (Melb.) Court,
t’other day, confidently gave “Galway” as his
religion. Come to think of it, there are many
people of the Galway and Belfast creeds.
The number of those who describe them-
selves as “atheists” and “agnostics” in Maon-
land has largely decreased, according to the cen-
sus; hut, on the other hand, those who “decline
to state religion” have increased by over .3000
since last count.
One charity home, near Sydney, seems
to he a most hopeless fire risk, and, in the event of
an outbreak, the loss of life would be tragic.
When the head man’s attention was directed to
this he is said to have replied “ that he trusted in
God.” The authorities should be responsible for
the safety of all these places. People who, without
taking all necessary human precautions, ‘trust
in God,” are as dangerous as lunatics.
“R.A.K.” : What an ideal breeding-place
for cancer, consumption, etc., is a little, badly-
ventilated chapel. I knew a woman who, after a
year’s illness, has just died a terrible death from
cancer; and it turns out that, for at least two
years, she had sat twice every Sunday, at chapel,
next to another woman affected with this terrible
disease, and had drunk from the same communion-
cup. Yet, when separate chalices are suggested,
the parsons either smile contemptuously or screech
with holy horror. “ Religion, ” dirt, and disease
always did have a good deal in common !

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 14, 1901.
The Tariff and the Labor Party.
Very slowly and wearily the tariff debate
potters along in the Federal Parliament.
The little knot of obstructive Foreign-traders
talk for hours on almost every item, and on
two occasions, by way of variety, they
blathered for 12 hours on no item at all—
merely on the proposal that the House
should adjourn. Every attempt on the part
of the Government to get a small item
through with less than three or four hours
conversation is denounced by the Foreign-
trade press as an attempt to stifle free dis-
cussion, and to shove the tariff along by
“brute force”; and the Sydney morning
papers, which have for many years demanded
and enjoyed 50 per cent. Protection under
the N.S.W. postal law, urge that the sense-
less stonewalling policy should be carried
out to the bitter end in the name of “ com-
mercial freedom.” And in between times
the same press denounces the Federal
Government for not making better progress
with public business. When the tariff tortoise
has at last crawled through* the House
of Representatives—which will probably be
some time next year—it has to get through
the Senate, where it will presumably meet
with the same difficulties and make about
the same pace. And already the obstructive
party is complaining about the protracted
character of the session and similar griev-
imces.
mm**
The spectacle of William M‘Millan lead-
ing the fight against a tariff which tends to
establish Australian industries is an interest-
ing feature of the case. M‘Millan is not
an Australian. He represents a great
absentee syndicate of English importers, and
to this syndicate, quite naturally, the estab-
lishment of Australian manufacturing indus-
tries, which would enrich Australia and
supply employment and wages to the Aus-
tralian people, and damage the M‘Arthur
importing business, is utterly abhorrent.
The importer wants to see Australia rich
enough to buy his foreign goods, but if it is
a choice between Australia being so poor that
it can only buy a moderate amount of
his goods, or so rich and self-dependent
that it won’t want any of them, his interest
certainly lies in leaving the country poor.
And, allied with the representatives of the
absentee importing interest, are certain
foolish Labor politicians who yearn to let in
free the products of nigger and Chinese labor
in the name of White Australia. These
individuals —mostly from New South Wales
—propose to cut down the tariff by
£1,000,000 or more per annum, and if they
succeed they will create such a scarcity of
money that the abolition of the Old Age
Pension system will very probably follow, as
the shortest way out of the difficulty. The
N.S.W. Labor party voted for the payment
of these pensions with an enthusiasm
which was beautiful to contemplate. The
enthusiasm with which its representatives in
the Federal Parliament vote against putting
up the money to pay them isn’t at all
beautiful to contemplate. A sad and foolish
tendency to hope that things financial will
come right of themselves is characteristic of
the Labor party, and of many other parties.
* m * *
If the Foreign-trade contingent in the
House of Representatives, which is mostly a
New South Wales contingent, has its way
the effect promises to be startling. The
Federal tariff has, so far, yielded more
revenue than was expected of it, and even
with all this extra revenue the Cornstalk
financial situation is about as bad as it can
be. The deficit of 30th November, 1900,
was £44,277- At 30th November, 1901, it
was £437,776. But for the wholesale
annexation as revenue of the rents of the
unpaid-for properties in the resumed area of
Sydney, the shortage would be already
approaching £600,000. The exact situation
is as follows :
Five months Five months
to November, to November,
1900. 1901.
Revenue .. £4,134,588 £4,159,715
Expenditure.. 4,178,865 4,597,491
Deficit .. £44,277 £437,776
And the loan expenditure has been increas-
ing just about as gaily as the deficit. This
is the account to date :
Five months Five months
to to
November, November,
1900. 1901.
Ordinary Loan Expenditure ..£993,838 £1,225,816
Extra, for purchase of Resumed
Properties Nil 291,133
£993,838 £1,516,949
• * * »
Last financial year N.S.W. enjoyed, in-
directly, the benefit of the Federal tariff just
as it does now ; there was such a remarkable
rush to import heavily with a view to getting
ahead of the tariff that in June alone the
customs collections at Sydney increased by
over £107,000. Yet last year ended with a
deficit, according to the Government Statis-
tician, of £334,880. This year things pro-
mise to be worse, and among the States
which issue progress reports of their expendi-
ture N.S.W. is, just now, almost the most em-
barrassed. Yet the war-expenditure has been
comparatively small, and no money has
been fooled away on ducal celebrations and
similar ornaments. Most of the extra expen-
diture has gone for purposes of which the
Labor party cordially approves. Old Age Pen-
sions have been the largest item. There have
been additions to the road vote to make the
ways easier for the man on the land ; reduc-
tions in railway freights for the same pur-
pose ; increased wages to many Government
employees, mostly those engaged in manual
labor ; and more education expenditure.
Also there is a big bill to pay for interest on
last year’s loans—carried through by the
Labor vote. Likewise, there is ahead an
extra expenditure of about £120,000 a year
by reason of the new loan raised to pay for
the Sydney resumptions, and an almost
equal annual expenditure will shortly be
incurred through the granting of the eight-
hour day to railway and tramway employees.
All these are matters which were cordially
approved of by the State Labor party in
N.S.W., but it made no attempt to provide
the money to pay for them. And the
N.S.W. Federal Labor party has mostly
allied itself with the representative of the
rich absentee class to fight against any
serious attempt to provide the money.
* * * *
And, curiously enough, the N.S.W.
Federal Labor party has, to a great extent,
allied itself with the bebaubled agent of the
rich absentee importing co. to prevent work
being found for the class it represents. At
almost every item of the tariff the voice of the
M‘Billion has been raised, with the soured
and querulous complaint: “This duty is
prohibitory and will stop importation.” The
duty which stops importation may be bad for
the bloated absentee importing firm, but it
must create local manufactures and employ-
ment for the class which sends the Labor
party into Parliament. Yet, a large section
of the Labor party votes consistently with
the M‘Billion to prevent imports being
stopped, and to keep its own constituents
out of work. In this matter it shows itself
the simple and child-like dupe of the
M‘BiLLiONclass. It demands such high wages
and such short hours for the Australian
worker that his product must be turned out
at greater cost than that of most foreign
workers—so much greater that even the cost
of importation isn’t sufficient to make up the
difference. Therefore, the importer, when
he sees a new Australian industry starting
under Freetrade conditions, can always cut
down his prices until he kills it dead. The
same Labor men refuse to make good the
difference by import duties, and thereby
seek to kill off Australian industries for the
benefit of the M‘Billion order. That
representative of an absentee class, which
makes from 10 to per cent, dividends
out of the Australian people, leads his Labor
following by its foolish nose to denounce as
an enemy and a monopolist and an extor-
tioner the local manufacturer, who seldom
makes over 8 per cent. The spectacle of a
considerable section of the Labor Party voting
against providing the money for the Old Age
Pensions, against providing the means by
which the State can give its railway
employees an eight-hour day, and against in-
creased employment for the workers, also
voting for the absentee importer with his
huge profits and for the free influx of cheap
labor goods and nigger and Chinese products,
is a solemn one.
• • • •
Even the excuse that the new duties are a
heavy burden on the masses has already
almost fallen through. When the Federal
tariff came into force there was a sudden and
very considerable increase of prices. In
many cases the importer added on two or
three times the duty—an act of extortion
which, apparently, only further endeared him
to a certain class of alleged democrats. But
already the bottom has dropped out of the
movement, and prices are getting back to
their old level. In many cases they are not
only getting back there, but have actually
arrived. The chief loser is the importer—
the rich English and German absentee, to
whom a large section of the N.S. W. Labor
party appears to have sold its soul and its
judgment and its other mental faculties. To
the foreign importers’ representatives it
matters very little if the Old Age Pension
scheme falls through, if the concessions
granted to the railway workers have to be
rescinded, or if the State treasury is practi-
cally bankrupt. It is much better for them
that wages should be low and a large section
of the people unemployed, than that wages
should be high and the people employed in
industries which will make the importer
wholly superfluous. The Bulletin doesn’t
accuse the importing class of taking this
brutally selfish attitude without good
authority. The leader of the great fight
against a national tariff and a national policy
himself acknowledged, almost in so many
words, that he fights against the tariff with
the full knowledge that he is fighting to
keep down local industries—mainly N.S.W.
industries—which would supply work and a
decent weekly wage to the community which
is foolish enough to send him into Parlia-
ment. Consider this item (Sydney Tele-
graph report, December 3) :
Sir William M'Millan : “ Victorians were making a
mistake in agitating for high duties, AS THEY WOULD
RESULT IN NEW SOUTH WALES BECOMING A
LARGE MANUFACTURING STATE and securing the
trade from their Victorian rivals.”
* * * #
That surely is a sufficiently broad intimation
that the M ‘Billion, who votes consistently
against the high duties which would make
N.S.W. “a large manufacturing State,”
doesn’t want it to become such—that he is
the direct enemy of N.S.W. industries. It
is also a sufficiently broad intimation
that the Labor member who votes with the
M‘Billion party is voting to keep down
Australian industries and to prevent Aus-
tralians getting employment,, all for the
benefit of the rich absentee trader. And
the people who send the Labor members
into Parliament want new industries that
they may have employment. They can’t
always live on building unproductive public
works paid for out of loans, and buying
M‘Billion’s imported calicoes with the
proceeds, and repairing at Mort’s Dock
the ships that brought the goods
that were paid for with borrowed money.
If N.S.W. isn’t weary of these servile
representatives who vote, at the bidding
of the wealthy foreign importer, against the
policy which he himself admits will make
N.S.W. a large manufacturing State, then it
is a patient country, and takes much ill-usage
in a Christian spirit. Surely that State
didn’t elect its Labor representatives that
they might run on little errands for the
wealthiest crowd of monopolists with which
it is afflicted.
Insolvent Friendly Societies.
Australia is a country which doesn’t take its
financial legislation seriously. It occasionally
passes an Act to put something right by the plain
and obvious process of making the parties con-
cerned pay up the shortage. But in nine cases
out of ten the parties concerned don’t pay up any-
thing, and the Act is either allowed to remain a
dead letter, or is repealed, or suspended. In this
manner Premier Lyne gained great kudos by
passing an Act providing that N.S.W. was to
begin next year to pay £400,000 per annum off its
deficit, and when next year came the Act was
postponed, and now the postponed amount is to
be reduced to £250,000, and further reductions and
postponements will probably follow. Something
similar has happened to the Friendly Societies
Act of 1899. The Friendly Societies of all the
States bar Maoriland are certainly in a dubious
position. Also, except in Maoriland, their condi-
tion is growing worse. Between 1892 and 1896
the general tendency was towards improvement;
since then the funds per member have been
diminishing at a rate which makes it almost
certain, in some States at all events, that insol-
vency is ahead —and in certain cases not very far
ahead —unless the rates are increased. The cause
of the trouble is simply that the amounts charged
to the members are not large enough in proportion
to the benefits granted—which is the simple and
sufficient explanation of the trouble in almost
every similar matter.
* * * *
The downward tendency of the Friendly
Societies in each State is thus set forth in
Coghlan’s “ Seven Colonies ” :
Funds per member.
1896. 1898. Decrease.
Westralia .. ..£l2 3 5 .. £7 15 11 .. £4 7 6
Victoria .. .. 14 6 5.. 13 4 8.. 1 1 9
Tasmania .. .. 811 0.. 805.. 010 7
1896. 1899.
Queensland .. 718 6.. 712 8.. 0 510
1896. 1898.
N.S. Wales .. 818 2.. 814 2.. 0 4 0
1895.
South Australia .. 11 2 9.. Unknown ..
1896. 1897. Increase.
Maoriland .. .. 19 4 6.. 19 10 0.. 0 5 6
In every case, there has been some increase of
membership, but, except in Maoriland, it has gone
along with a decrease of financial solidity,
wherever the figures for comparison are available.
New South Wales is certainly not in the worst
position as regards funds, though it is much worse
than Maoriland, Victoria, or South Australia.
And it isn’t falling astern nearly so fast as
its neighbors. All the same, it was officially
announced that, in 1899, hardly any of the N.S. W.
Friendly Societies were solvent, and, in one or two
cases, the deficit ran to over 10s. in the £. The
average deficit was over Bs. in the £, which is a
truly awful state of affairs, and the whole shortage
was over £1,000,000. The Societies showed no
inclination to help themselves in the matter; the
Australian reluctance to pay up and the tired
Australian tendency to let it slide supervened in
every, or almost every, case.
* * * *
So in 1899 Parliament took a hand with a view
to protecting those new members who were con-
stantly joining what were practically insolvent
institutions —as a rule, without any knowledge of
their insolvency. It passed an Act giving the
societies two years in which to adopt such in-
creased rates as would give them a chance to get
back, in course of years, to a solvent basis, the
penalty being that, in case of non-compliance, they
would cease to be registered as Friendly Societies.
The Societies made no protest at the time, and
the Act passed amid a general impression that a
troublesome subject was at last disposed of. It
didn’t call for any great sacrifice or any violent
effort, but merely demanded that a beginning
should be made towards putting affairs right.
And, almost unanimously, the Friendly Societies
just went on letting things slide, and paid no
attention whatever. The time came in sight
when they must either begin to put their house in
order or else wind up, and instead of making a
move towards getting on to a sound basis, they
hollered for a suspension of the Act, so that they
could jog peacefully along their old insolvent
road for a while longer. And the See Govern-
ment promptly gave way and compromised. Its
new measure dropped the unpleasant question of
solvency, and dropped it m a rather clumsy
fashion, the main provisions of the Bill being
these
(1) New members were to pay rates declared by any
actuary of five years standing to be sufficient, and their
funds were to be kept separate, and be liable to pay only
the new members’ claims.
(2) The old members might pay their old, insufficient
contributions into their own insufficient fund, provided
that they didn’t reduce these contributions and make
them still more insufficient. Their own claims must be
met out of their own fund. (This suited the aged “ old
members ” all right, as they would, in the natural course
of events, die first, and the old fund might last long
enough to pay their, claims. The younger among the
“old members” would probably find the fund defunct
when their claims began to fall in.)
(3) On this arrangement each society might be regis-
tered till next valuation And then, if the position
of the old members’ fund had improved ever so slightly
—if the deficit had been reduced from 12s. in the £
to 11s. 9d. in the £ ; or if there was no improvement,
but if, even at this late hour, the old members agreed to
increase their contributions to a figure which any actuary
of five years’ standing held to be sufficient, then the
society might be again registered and allowed to go on
indefinitely.
(4) And if neither of these things happened, then the
society might either holler for a further suspension of the
law, or else be wound up—the new members who had
paid sufficient contributions being thus, in a measure,
sacrificed to the selfishness of the old members who
hadn’t.
• • * •
This seems about as far as concession could
reasonably go. But even this didn’t satisfy the
old members. What they wanted was that new
members should pay an increased rate ; that the
old members should still pay at the old low rate ;
and that instead of the new members’ fund being
kept separate their extra payments should help to
make good the old members’ deficiency. They
wanted separate accounts but only one
fund, and the See Government gave way
without any demur. Furthermore, there is
to be no State standard of solvency after
all. Any scale of contribution is to be held to be
sufficient, if any actuary of five years’ standing
can be persuaded to certify that, in his
opinion, it is sufficient! This opens a fine
large field of usefulness for the actuary who
will certify that the smallest premium rate is
large enough. The much-amended new measure,
as passed by the Assembly, doesn’t demand that
any Friendly Society shall put itself on a solvent
basis at any time—not even 25 or 150 years hence.
Apparently even the institution whose funds show
a deficiency of 12s. 7d. in the £ as regards the old
members may be re-registered if it reduces that
shortage to 12s. 3d. in the £, and after that may
go on indefinitely. And if its position has become
worse instead of better, it may still be re-registered
and go on indefinitely if its rates are certified as
sufficient by an actuary who has been practising
his profession for five years. And here there
is great scope for an actuary who is far gone in
beer and doesn’t mind much what he certifies.
Taking one thing with another there is so much
lamentable shirking of the difficulty that the whole
matter might almost as well have been left alone.
A great deal of time has been wasted, first in
enacting that the societies must put themselves
on a sound basis, and, secondly, in enacting that
they may remain on the old unsound basis if they
want to.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Coast Hospital Again.
The eternal N.S.W. Coast Hospital scandal
cropped up again, last month, before a Par-
liamentary Committee. Presumably, it will
never be at rest till the meddlesome and
offensive person Glynn (who made such an
amount of trouble over a trifling matter of
feeding convalescent typhoid patients with fish,
which was sworn to be putrid; vermicelli,
solemnly declared to be so full of weevils that
the insects formed a scum when it was put in
the soup; and vegetables stated on oath to be
slimy and fermented), is prosecuted and gaoled
for bringing about disclosures which have seri-
ously disturbed the Public Service Board, and
cast discredit on the Board of Health. What
right has any miserable probationer-storekeeper


to cause all this trouble and disgrace to a public
body ? A meddlesome Parliamentary Committee
is now inquiring into the matter, and causing
more unpleasantness by dragging to light such
unsatisfactory and unpleasant evidence as the
following:—
George Edward Brodie, Chief Inspector of Accounts,
swore: “ I saw a minute from Dr. Thompson . . .
censuring the storekeeper (Glynn) for having carelessly
condemned fish and other food . . . a statement was
made by Mr. Kidman that the fish was not bad, and he
sent along certain samples . . . but the whole procedure
was worth nothing, because the fish taken from the
Coast Hospital was not sealed up, and Kidman could have
got fish from anywhere, and could have supplied the
samples.”
Fred. O. Glynn swore : “ The contract said * fresh
fish ; ’ but the only fish I was supplied with by Arthur
Kidman was fish of an old, stale character, that had been
on ice and partly thawed . . . three parts of it would
have to be condemned. ... I sent the goods back two
or three times. About a week after I was there Mr.
Willman seemed particularly strong on my receiving it.
I had it referred to the medical superintendent. He
immediately caused it to be sent back. . . . Butter has
been absolutely rancid. I took portions of the butter to
the chief cook and the matron, and took their opinion
before I submitted it to the doctor. They always
coincided with me, but in 19 cases out of 20 it was the
other way with Mr. Willman —he always wanted to say
it was not too good, but that he did not think it was bad
enough to reject. The flour was analysed by the Govt.
Analyst, and it was proved to be unfit for human
consumption. Even the soap which was sent to
the laundry was green and had to be dried. . . .
These supplies were sent out repeatedly. It was
a constant thing every day, as delivery books
will show. We sent in samples to the Board of Health of
the fish and vegetables. Daily reports were sent in as to
the character, quality and condition of the material. So
far as I could see, it had absolutely no effect at all. . . .
When I went to Kidman’s store, I found that there were
three old stores in a back lane, in a dirty, ill-lighted con-
dition. All the vegetables were on a cement or concrete
floor. The place was very dirty. Two horses were
stabled in front of the doorthe droppings and urine lay
in front of the floor. ... I remember bacon and hams
being rejected because they were maggotty. ... I heard
Willman telling Kidman (through telephone) that he
could not do anything, that he would have to report him,
but was trying to do the best he could for him.
Then one 0. A. Simms took up the tale. Mr.
Simms was lately secretary to the Board of
Health; but, after the exposure, the P.S.B. shifted
him, without explanation, to be a common clerk
in the Mines Department. Presumably, the
Board’s heart was too full for speech. Under
oath, Mr, Simms told a pitiful tale:—
I cannot tell you . . . how often these goods came in,
but they came in frequently, and Dr. Violette’s descrip-
tion of them was nearly always the same—that they were
unfit for food. . . . Every day a short memorandum
came in dealing with two or three lines of food. The
practice had been in existence for several years, and they
came in with unfailing regularity. So much so that
they bore no special importance . . . but Dr. Violette
sent in several long reports. Certainly, in the majority of
cases, the provisions which were submitted were quite
passable, such as in my opinion should not have been con-
demned —notably fish. ... In some cases these samples
of condemned fish were brought in by Kidman himself.
... We had no guarantee that the fish he brought was
the fish rejected beyond his statement and his offer to
make a statutory declaration that it was so. ... I sub-
mitted all specific letters from Dr. Violette recommend-
ing punishment of the contractor to Dr. Thompson. ... I
was against punishing the man, and I told Dr.
Thompson so, and he concurred in most instances,
having in mind the samples of food that were quite
good, and such as might reasonably have been
accepted. I considered his (Dr. Violette’s) judgment
was defective. Dr. Thompson, when visiting the
hospital, did not go through the wards and make inquiries
of the patients. He simply visited the offices. . . I have
not the slightest doubt, speaking from the knowledge I
gained at the Public Service Board inquiry, that Kidman
did supply bad provisions from time to time. But to say
that he systematically supplied putrid food every day is
to assume an absolutely untenable position. I do not
believe it—not from my knowledge of the man, but
because I think it incredible that a man would do such a
thing. I cannot see his object in it. . . We practically
took little notice of these daily memoranda, we were so
accustomed to them by usage.
What a streak of humor runs through that evi-
dence ! Almost every day for years the officers
in charge of the Coast Hospital complained that
the contractor was supplying the patients with
bad food, and there was no counter evidence save
that the contractor occasionally brought in or sent
in samples which he said were the ones rejected.
And all that time the wretched officers of the
Board of Health just let things “ rip.” The com-
plaints were so frequent that they became accus-
tomed to them. The only time anybody woke up
was when officer Glynn took matters so seriously
that the scandal began to be troublesome. Then
Glynn lost his billet, and it wasn’t till he had
been out of work for many months, and The
Bulletin had drawn attention to his case at
great length, that he was reluctantly supplied with
another job—at a reduced salary.
The Butter-Box Superstition.
The demand that timber should be admitted free
into the Commonwealth with an especial refer-
ence to Maoriland pine, said to be absolutely
essential for the manufacture of butter-boxes, has
evoked a very reasonable protest from Queens-
land. The Queenslanders point out that in their
own butter-trade they use their own pine, which
is quite as good and almost as abundant as that of
Maoriland. The same timber grows plentifully in
the northern parts of N.S.W., but as these two
States do very little in the butter export trade,
the value of the local pine for this purpose is not
much known. Moreover, the industry m Queens-
land has long' been hampered by want of
roads, and N.S.W. makes importing an article
of faith and neglects local products on prin-
ciple, because its fetish and its _ one test
of prosperty is “trade,” and when it imports
timber from Maoriland that is trade, while using
the local article isn’t. So the energetic Maori-
landers, who are spreading their industries
everywhere under their splendid system of Pro-
tection, finding that Victoria had a large butter
export trade and no pine forests of its own, seized
the opportunity, and an impression has arisen
that Australia is wholly dependent on Maoriland
for the casing wherein to pack the product of the
cow. The impression grew so strong that the
Maorilanders even conceived the idea of squashing
the Commonwealth and bringing it to a humbler
frame of mind by putting an export duty on pine,
the idea being that Australia would thereby be
reduced to exporting its butter wrapped up in an
old newspaper. What the Queenslanders desire
to point out is that the saw-milling industry is
almost the largest factory industry in their State,
coming a very close second to the sugar-
refining business; that, allowing for the hands
employed in timber-getting and conveyance
it employs more white men than any other manu-
facturing industry, sugar not excepted ; that the
business is an important one, and deserving of
encouragement in all the States ; that the Com-
monwealth possesses timber suited for almost
every purpose,-and, if reasonable care is taken to
replant the forests as they are cut out, it will
always possess it in reasonable abundance.
Therefore they ask the Commonwealth to protect
the whole industry, to abolish the proposed
exemptions, and not to grovel to any extraneous
State by reason of any imaginary dependence on
outside supplies for butter-boxes or other sub-
stances. And their case seems good enough to be
worth consideration.
N.S.W. Western Lands Problem.
The Bill of Minister Crick for the smoothing of
the Western lands difficulty looks like a reason-
ably good attempt to deal with a very bad
problem. No amount of legislation will make
some parts of the West fit for a decent white
man’s habitation. But as the white man has gone
there, the matter now on hand is to alleviate his
case as far as possible. It is proposed to place the
whole West under the control of one Board, which
will assess rentals. Leases may be extended for
as much as 42 years, with power reserved for the
State to resume if, by some apparently impossible
contingency, any of the land should be wanted
for closer settlement. There is no compulsion
upon the State to renew leases. Where the
squatter is hopelessly mortgaged and is merely
being driven by his creditors to seek a re-
newal in order that he may spend 42
years in working off a dead horse, and
sweating himself and his employees, and
taking the last possible blade of grass off the
State’s property, the Minister has power to re-
quire, as a condition of renewal, that sufficient of
the debt shall be written off to give the State’s
tenant a fair show. This is strictly logical. The
financial institutions lent money on the old lease
which is expiring, and they got the security they
stipulated for. The State isn’t bound to make
them a present of a new lease, especially as its
object is to grow men in the West, if possible—■
not mortgages. The lessees are to be bound to
keep down rabbits, and in some parts this will
probably be their chief duty. There are places in
the West which might almost as well be aban-
doned but for the necessity of keeping a watchful
eye on the weary, endless rabbit. In theory there
is much to be said against Crick’s Bill, but
theories that are good in many other places are of
little account out on the road to Tibooburra.
Long leases and large areas and great land
monopolies are all objectionable, but if a man
wants a long lease of hell, or a large area of hell,
or desires to peg out a big monopoly on the floor-
space of the Bottomless Pit, it seems almost as
well to let him have it if he will pay anything for
the privilege. Some parts of the West are less
objectionable than others, but, after all, it is mainly
a question of the difference between the bottom
shelf of Gehenna and the shelf above. So long
as there are great areas like the A.A. Co.’s
property and the Bibbenluke estate to be resumed
and cut up in the comparatively well-watered
East, no man of any feeling wants to see the
agriculturist beguiled out into the Never Never
Land, to grow hopeless and loony and uncivi-
lised among its plains and horrors. And the
agriculturist himself appears to be of much the
same way of thinking. The men who really know
the West seem to be unanimously agreed that
anybody can have their share of it for 42 years on
very moderate terms indeed.
The Complication of Colors.
Part of the price paid by Australia for its ad-
mission of the colored inferior races is the compli-
cation of the problem of dealing with its own
aborigines. So far as the mass of the people are
concerned, there is now a general desire to deal
kindly, and as justly as possible, with the primi-
tive inhabitants of the land, however badly the
intention may be carried out. But good inten-
tions are badly frustrated in those places where,
in addition to the aboriginal colored man, there is
a Kanaka and Asiatic population. Thus in
Northern Queensland —the most color-stained
patch of the continent —a genuinely humane at-
tempt to do some measure of justice to the
aborigines is being rendered almost nugatory by
the presence of other colored races. The report
for 1900 of Dr. Roth, Protector of the Northern
aborigines, makes tin's clear. He says :
I look upon the opium-habit as a cancerous sore, slowly
but surely eating its way into the bosom of the com-
munity—a danger fatal alike to the black and to the
white. ... But I regret to state that the supply of
opium to aboriginals continues apparently with unabated
vigor in spite of the vigilance of the authorities and the
increasing severity of the fines inflicted. . . . The police
have to cope with the ever-increasing smartness of the
Asiatics (the main suppliers).
Then follows a list of convictions of opium-sellers,
the convicts being named :
Charley Ah Lam, Ah Sue, Ah Way, Ah Chong, Ah
Yet, Ah Sam, Ah Young, Mow Quay, Ah Hoey, Ah Way,
Ah Wong, Charlie Ah Tie, Ah Long, Ye Yek (Ah Sing),
Ah Yua, Wah Lu, Aii Luey, Ah Sing, Ah Sam, Mollimon,
Sam Yin, Ah Yen, Jimmy Ah Kong, Jimmy (Aii Sam), Ah
Ki, Ah Quey, Ah Sam, Ju See You, Lum Foo.
Which list seems to indicate that Asiatics are
more than the main suppliers—that they are the
only suppliers of this deadly drug to the abori-
gines. Other evils arising from the contact of the
two colored races are exposed by Dr. Roth when
he laments
The formation of Chinese camps, which are anything
but conducive to the health and morals of the European
residents, and certain destruction in the near future of
the once robust native population, by their supplying
opium and other abominations among them. One of the
most repulsive cases of leprosy that I have seen came
from these camps.
Vice and vice-disease statistics collated by the
Protector help to show the terrible moral and
social consequences of the admixture of Kanakas,
Chinese, Malays, and aborigines in the far North.
There is no room for doubt that with an exclu-
sively-white intruding population, the N.Q. native
might be given a show : the presence of the
Kanaka, the Hindu, the Chinese 'and the Malay
makes his doom certain. The fact should suggest
some wholesome reflections to the kind Christian
clergyman who wishes to bring the extraneous
nigger to our shores for his “ moral elevation.”
O
Insolvent Municipalities and Municipali-
ties that are going Insolvent.
Municipal Government in New South Wales is
an amusing burlesque. Take, as an example, the
case of Camperdown—one of the 41 or 42 or 43 fly-
speck municipalities in and around Sydney. A
year or two ago it was a place with good pros-
pects and the worst possible intentions. It had a
population of 7200, and only 14 miles of streets to
keep in order, and its rates, even when it began
to go definitely to the bad, remained at Is. 4d. in
the £ (including 4d. for street lighting). And this
is its record in recent years :
Total
Income. Expenditure. Deficit. Debt.
1897 .. .. £2,868 .. £3,431 .. £563 .. £22,892
1898 .. .. 3,151 .. 3,461 .. 310 .. 23,432
1899 .. .. 3,048 .. 3,656 .. 608 .. 24,007
In these three years, the miserable spot got £550
of financial assistance from the State—an absurd
thing in the case of a thickly-peopled city muni-
cipality—else its situation would have been much
worse. It collected its income so feebly that, in
1899, about eight months’ rates were outstanding.
Of its debt £15,500 carried 6 per cent, interest,
and about £6OOO was a bank overdraft at 8 per
cent. No attempt was ever made to raise enough
money to make ends meet. In 1899 the general
rate of Is. in the £ produced £1946, and interest
on the municipality’s debts absorbed £1498. When
its principal loan fell due it doesn’t appear that
any serious attempt was made at renewal—it was
just allowed to fall. So the creditors put in a re-
ceiver who sold the ridiculous town hall for what
it would fetch, and who now collects the rates
on behalf of the creditors, and is a mixture of a
bailiff and an indignant Asiatic despot. As his
business is solely to look after the creditors he
naturally put a stop to street repairs and improve-
ments, they being matters which cost money.
Consequently, the alleged Mayor reports that
in this thickly-peopled city municipality the main
street has worn into holes “large enough to bury
a horse in,” and traffic has practically left it. It
was proposed to close a number of streets as being
no longer safe, but it was found that the law
didn’t permit of this, so it was resolved to
humbly approach the bailiff, or man in posses-
sion, with a request that he would in his great-
ness and magnanimity, allow £l7 10s. to be
spent in “ballast to fill up the larger holes.”
Nobody proposes to levy enough rates to meet all
legitimate outgoings and something over to reduce
the debt, and to negotiate with the creditors
for a renewal on that basis—the weary ten-
dency to let-her-slide is against any such energetic
measures. And the bulk of the innumerable
municipalities of Sydney are wandering more or
less definitely towards exactly the same position
which Camperdown occupies. Out of the 41
councils enumerated by CoGHLAN.as helping to
misgovern the N.S.W. capital and its suburbs, 26
had a deficit in 1897 ; 22 m 1898; and 26 in 1899.
Seven had a deficit each year, and 20 had a deficit
two years out of three. The whole crowd went
£153,440 to the bad in three years. Truly, it will
be a great day when Parliament gives the people
a chance to abolish the whole 41 absurdities by a
popular vote, and makes a Greater Sydney, .with
a sane municipal constitution, and no holes in its
streets large enough to bury a horse or a tram-
car in.
4
The recent letter of N.S.W. Labor-leader
M ‘Go WEN on “Federal Finance” was probably
the most unutterably silly thing any Australian
has got off his chest this year. After carefully
enumerating the anticipated deficits of five of the
States under the Federal tariff, and carefully
omitting the huge anticipated deficit of N.S.W.,
the puerile James went on thus: —
The last alternative is assistance from the Common-
wealth. As all the States, except N.S.W., are necessi-
tous—that is to say, according to the Treasurer’s state-
ment and recent statistics, they will not be able to make
ends meet—the only source from which assistance can be
drawn to help them is N.S.W. This brings me to my
contention during the Federal elections— viz., that the
financial provisions of the Commonwealth Constitution
would bring ruin to the States unless N.S.W. foots the
bill.
Think of any sane man talking glibly about
N.S.W. (which, by its own Treasurer’s calcula-
tions, appears likely to have the biggest shortage
save one in the six States, which has the biggest
accumulated deficit, and whose last year’s deficit
was the biggest in the Commonwealth save
Queensland’s) footing anybody else’s bill !
N.S.W. has just the same difficulty about
footing its own bill as its neighbors’ have,
but its politicians seem specially addicted to this
kind of silliness. George Reid, who never could
keep his own accounts square, also talked loftily
about his willingness to “finance” the other
federated States. Of course, neither he nor
M‘Gowen ever really dreamed of paying a copper
on behalf of any other State. It is merely a large,
offensive attitude they have got into. And the
apex of the whole gorgeous foolishness is reached
in M‘Gowen’s suggestion that it is the financial
provisions of the Commonwealth Constitution
which are causing this scarcity of money. Seeing
that N.S.W. has had 14 deficits in the last 21
years; Queensland, 11; S.A. and Westralia,
10 each ; and Tasmania and Victoria, nine each ;
and that the Commonwealth, taken as a whole,
has had eight deficits in the last eleven years, it
is somewhat rough to blame the new Federal
Constitution because the usual thing seems likely
to happen again.
4
N.S.W. Acting-Justice Pring went far out of his
way to “horse-shoe” the two prisoners he lately
sentenced to death. To one he said :
The offence you have committed under such circum
stances as have been described is one that for awful
atrocity simply baffles the power of language to describe.
The penalty for it is death, and the Legislature has wisely
thought that in the country, where little girls go about
unprotected, frequently three or four miles to school, it
is necessary to protect them by such a penalty for such
an offence as yours. It is intolerable that filthy, disgust-
ing brutes like you should lay in wait for children and
treat them as you did in this case. Your request will be
submitted to the authorities. I have no power to com-
ply with it. His Honor then passed sentence of death,
with much, &c.
To another he remarked :
This crime of yours is one of the worst it is possible to
commit. There is nothing which indicates a greater
degree of cruelty than this attempt to poison. It evinces
such a horrible, cruel deliberation that it is almost im-
possible to understand that a human being could be guilty
of it. In your case it is rendered all the more terrible
because you have attempted this crime upon one whom
you were bound by every tie to save from trouble.
Why in the name, of the majesty of the law are
Judges permitted to go on in such a fashion ? Do
such Judges talk for mere advt. of their own im-
portance, or to prejudice any chance the con-
demned man may have with the Executive?
Either notion is unworthy—the one a horrid
pandering to notoriety, the other a contempt of
the final Court of Appeal. Judges would serve
better their own dignity and the ends of justice
by reserving their moralisings for the breakfast-
table, and on the bench keeping their judicial
duty strictly in view.

The truly remarkable O’Sullivan is to the fore
with a project for a Sydney City railway “exten-
sion to cost £1,155,000. It isn’t to be a main
railway—only an extension. Trains coming into
Sydney will still stop at Redfern, and the weary
passenger (who may have come from Brisbane or
Melbourne or Bourke, and who wants to get
really into the city) will still have to struggle out
of his train, and curse O’Sullivan on the plat-
form, and make his way with his luggage into
another train to take him the last mile of his
journey. The City railway, which should be about
the most profitable in the whole system, is actually
estimated, under these ridiculous circumstances,
as not likely to pay working expenses, let alone
interest. And no wonder. If it was a mile or so
added on to the present run a penny or 2d. could
be added to the fares for the extra distance, but
when once the public is thrown out of its train it
would just as soon take a cab in the old way as
scramble into a new train to have a separate
penn’orth. The receipts of the “extension” are
estimated at £38,000 a year, and outgoings over
£9l,ooo—say £40,000 for interest and £51,000 for
working expenses, leaving a deficit of £13,000 on
expenses alone. As an example of how not to do
it this is unique.
4.
“V. R. -man ” writes :
The Vic. railway-man has been fooled again, the classi-
fication so Jong promised, so often postponed, having
been put off indefinitely. The men feel the injustice of
it all the more strongly after reading of the success of
their N.S.W. brethren in obtaining the eight-hours-a-day
and other privileges. The Vic. men will soon begin to do
as the N.S.W. men did—talk strike. They would now,
only that the various associations are largely bossed by
the old “compensation” men, who don’t want to run
risks with their accrued pension-rights. On Sunday (17th
Nov.), meetings were held all over the State, and the talk
was pretty plain. Hundreds, thousands of men have
been hanging on to the hope that their long-deferred in ■
crements would come when the Classification Bill was
passed. Present writer has been in the service over 13
years. His yearly wage is £l4O Bs. It has been the same
for 10 years (when not less, as it was during the depres-
sion). The feeling amongst the men now is that, if the
Bill is dropped, it may he years ere anything is done.
The new Commissioners (there are to he three again) will
want a year or two in office “ to get the hang of things.”
Then the men will have all their battling over again—
conferences, deputations, etc., without end. Human
nature —-even railway human nature, which is very
patient—can only endure up to a certain limit, and that
limit has been nearly reached.
4
Warden Gibbon, of Adelaide Marine Board,
proposes to add to the local shipping by-laws a
provision making it a criminal offence for any
shipmaster to take his vessel out of any port in
the State without having her hatches properly
secured. A step in the right direction, which
should be taken also by the other Australian
States. But in matters concerning the safety of
the “shellback” (mostly regarded as a mere
money-making machine), Australian legislatures
are slow to move. The number of instances in
which insecure hatches have brought about total
loss will never be known, for the simple reason
that usually nobody is left to tell the tale. In the
rush and scurry of the present-day shipping-
industry, the securing of hatches is never thought
of by the shore people intent upon getting a
vessel away to advertised time. That’s the mate’s
business when the ship gets to sea. But, before a
vessel is allowed to leave, the Marine Board cer-
tifies her seaworthy, and “Jack” ships on that
understanding. Now, no ship is seaworthy with
unsecured hatches. Yet vessels are continually
allowed to leave our ports (even in heavy weather)
in this condition. The same (Adelaide) Marine
Board, by the way, reports, as a result of its
inquiry into the loss of the barge Gwydir, that
“the evidence given showed a direct lack of
systematic and efficient inspection of ships, and
their equipment generally at the larger South
Australian ports.” And it’s the same in the other
States if only the local Boards were honest enough
to admit it.

The extraordinary statement recently made by the
Times on the subject of train wrecking in S’Africa
is one of the most remarkable eye-openers in the
history of modern warfare. If the Times is to be
credited, it is clear that, even with 250,000 men in
the field in Britain cannot guard the
railways. There is only one obstacle, says the
Times, which prevents the Boers from absolutely
destroying the communications. That obstacle
consists in the fact that the stoppage of supplies
by destroying the railways would mean starvation
to the women and children in the concentration
camps, and the Times declares that of late train-
wrecking commandos, while deliberately destroy-
ing the line in order to secure supplies of arms and
ammunition for themselves, have allowed the sup-
ply trains for the concentration camps to pass un-
molested. The British public is asked to believe
that certain sections of the line have been kept
open by arrangement with the train-wrecking
commandos. “It has been an understood con-
dition between drivers and guards and the train-
wreckers that as long as certain supplies—such as
coffee, sugar, and flour—were dropped at a certain
convenient spot on the line, that particular section
of the line should not be tampered with, and these
burghers have never once broken faith when the
conditions have been complied with.” If this
statement is true, it proves that the Boers are
simply levying tribute upon the army of occupa-
tion, and that, so far from the war being over,
even the main lines of communication are at the
mercy of the commandos.

N.S.W. Education Minister Perry is credited
with blocking B. R. Wise’s announced Bill deal-
ing with the scandalous death-rate among ille-
gitimate children. The Bill is a comprehensive
one, placing all dependent children under the
State Children’s Board, including those on the
training-ship Sobraon, the Industrial Schools,
and other institutions now controlled by
Minister Perry. It provides also for wider
powers in rescuing the poor illegitimate
from an untimely grave, and comes close
—perilously close, in Minister Perry’s eyes—to
establishing State foundling hospitals. Whether
he objects to parting with institutions under his
supervision, or whether, representing the “ God-
fearing” element in the Cabinet, he objects to any
sympathy for the child of “sin,” the fact remains
that he has blocked the Bill, arguing that the
Govt, has no time for the measure. Surely a
glance at the statistics recently published in these
columns would convince the Minister that few
measures are calling more urgently for instant
consideration than this one. At present the mor-
tality amo ig illegitimates in N.S.W. would force
the superintendent of a Boer concentration camp
into a green-paint fabrication.
The Gold-Buyers Bill has been an interesting
item in the Vic. Assembly pow-wow. Clause 19
decrees that a person offering unwrought gold or
amalgam for sale may —not necessarily must —be
required to declare the locality and the nature of
the ground where he got the stuff. Ex-Minister
Foster, also Bailes, of Bendigo, affected to
regard this clause as a great injustice to the
patient fossicker who, having struck a patch, is
asked to give the show away to a crafty buyer
with myrmidons waiting round the corner to rush
the find. But, as there didn’t seem to be any
danger of anybody rushing a district whose pros-
pector couldn’t arrange to get a £6 lease of
his Tom Tiddler’s ground ere divulging the
locality, the plaintive cries of Foster and Bailes
failed to melt Clause 19’s heart. Another
matter for Bailes’ lament was a new clause intro-
duced by his colleague Lazarus to prohibit the sale
of gold by children under 14. The youngster who
“specks” gold in the creek must sell the dust
through his father or any other person of over 14
if he wants to turn it into cash. Bailes protested
against this injustice to fossickers of tender years.
He himself specked gold before he was 10, he
said—overlooking the fact that there is nothing in
the present Bill to prevent babes from doing like-
wise. If Bailes meant the House to infer that
he used to “doin’’ his own gold-dust before he
was 10, it explains why he eventually sank to be
a member of Parliament.

majority of the aldermen returned being in favor
of jobbing away the trams to Snow’s syndi-

lienee, the syndicate’s supporters will probably be
crying out for State purchase at a high figure, and,
if the deal comes off, the division of the profit will
be suggestive of 20 crabs reaching out for a
drowned passenger on the shore.
