Wilson Barrett is closing his season at
Her Majesty’s (Sydney) with a series of revivals,
whereof the “Silver King” and the “Manxman”
have been the most notable up to date. Barrett’s
“ Silver King ” drives somewhat heavily, like
Pharaoh’s chariot with the wheels off struggling
through the damp places in Exodus ; but his Pete
—which seems to be rather an improvement
on the % Pete which used to be—has much to
recommend it. And the “Manxman” is a play
which always fills the present writer with cynical
enjoyment. In that drama Philip Christian
refuses to marry Kirry, daughter of Mr. Publican
Cregeen, and is sorry afterwards. Kirry marries
Pete, and is sorry afterwards. She leaves that
noble, but ignorant and badly-dressed person, and
flies to Philip— and is darned sorry she fled.
Philip gets the woman he has longed for—and is
wretched. The parties concerned being all miser-
able, Kirry drifts back to Pete, but is too depressed
to remain. Finally she wanders to the house of
her father, old Publican Cregeen, where she was
never happy, and settles down in the capacity of
a beautiful woman who is miserable everywhere,
and who might as well be wretched in her father’s
public-house as any other place. The writer tries
to admire hero Pete, and fails dismally. Pete
has every good quality save grammar, a collar, a
white shirt, a pair of boots that won’t make him
sound like an iron-shod elephant, and a knowledge
of when not to bellow up the stair as if it was a
speaking-trumpet. A boiled shirt, a collar, and a
good ten-and-sixpenny hat, applied at the right
place, might be the three hairs in the
scale that would turn Kirry’s admiration
into love; but Pete doesn’t know. Wilson
Barrett’s Pete is perfect in this sense, that the
writer can’t imagine any way in which he could
be a kinder husband, a truer gentleman, or a more
weary individual to live with. Ambrose Manning’s
Publican Creegan is the same excellent character-
sketch as aforetime ; and Kirry, the girl with the
unlimited oapacity for being wretched, takes well.
The moral of “ The Manxman ” appears to be that
the wages of sin are death, and that these wages
are just as hard to collect as any other salary.

** * *
“Honour Thy Father,” now doing its
second week at Sydney Lyceum, is a play wherein
Miss Ada Guildford is the erring daughter in a
sinful green dress, and is accompanied occasion-
ally by a nicely-got-up infant, for which the hero
is supposed to be responsible. All through the
drama Ada is in a chronic state of being cast
somewhere or other. She is cast adrift in act 1.,
and later on is cast into the Thames by the villain
in the presence of half-a-dozen persons. No par-
ticular notice is taken of the villain’s conduct in
this respect, though he is severely reprobated
when, later on, he casts her beneath the horses’
feet. (The programme here tells the audience to
“ Lift her up tenderly.”) The scenery behind the
horses’ heads then breaks out into an apotheosis
of Ada on angel wings. She has been cast down
all through the drama, and is now going up
for a change. Anderson’s drama is evidently
written with a painful attempt to inculcate virtue
in young women, but it misses its mark, because,
if Ada had been less inclined towards morality,
she would not have troubled the villain to make
her an honest woman, and thereby tempted him
to cast her down so often. She would have been
perfectly satisfied with being a dishonest woman
in easy circumstances. Some mention must be
made of Broadmead Asylum, where a lunatic
invents a vicious chair that puts people’s eyes out,
and is allowed to leave it in the waiting-
room. The lunatic is also permitted to wander in
and out of the room and entice persons to this
chair at will, and to carry about a supply of acids
necessary for the blinding process. The chair is
afterwards removed to the villain’s drawing-room,
where it is further equipped with a bronze mask,
that falls over a sitter’s head and puts out his eyes
by another process. It is taken very much as a
matter of course that titled persons should have
this sort of furniture in their baronial halls.
* » * *
H. R. Willard takes the part of the villain
with the casting-down propensities, and loses his
eyesight in a graceful and aristocratic fashion.
Sterling Whyte makes the best of that
dreariest of characters, a lower-class parent. Miss
Ivy Gorrick, as the virtuous heroine, acts more
naturally than she speaks. She takes the unsatis-
factory role of the heiress who laments that she
does not see Ada in the simple village garb of old
days instead of in the bright-green dress of sin.
The heiress wears a very nice dress herself, and
does not reflect that she ought to be grateful she
can do so without having to sin. Temple Harri-
son manages the trying dialect of an English
peasant well enough, and Ethel Buckley and Bert
Bailey fill the other comic parts. Nobody is
absolutely natural, for it is impossible to be so in
a drama of this kind, but the above-mentioned
are as little like confused wax figures as the case
will admit.

In the Sydney Tivoli “first part,” where
the chorus wanders about the stage, Chas.
Walker’s foghorn voice is heard in “Give Me
Them Clothes,” and Miss Florrie Ranger sings
a deadly uninteresting patriotic song, “Pattern
to the World,” in a dress that seems as if
it were trying to be a vivandi&re costume,
but couldn’t get there. Harry Gray ends
the programme with humour which, like Moody
and Sankey’s Hymn-book, is ancient and modern.
Mademoiselle Nadine, the “celebrated Parisian
Chansonette,” does some clever toe-dancing in
the limelight, but strikes the audience most when
she appears, like Joseph, in a garment of many
colors, and sings a ditto re her willingness to dance
with “M’sieurif M’sieur will not tell his wife.”
Lizzie Collins, who appears with the still cleverer
Albert Athas, is a bright, large-voiced songstress
without any of Lottie’s unique charm, but with a
certain amount of her own. The re-appearance of
Miss Lizzie Kirk, however, pleased this authority
as much as any larger-lettered item.
m* * *
Oscar Asche, son of the proprietress of the
Imperial Hotel, Sydney, has “struck it” in the
London theatrical world. His sudden celebrity
as the ‘ ‘ heavy lead ” in Pinero’s new play has led
to a newspaper discovery that the new man
acquired all his acting with the Benson
travelling co., of which his clever wife, Miss Lily
Brayton, was also a member. But it wasn’t at
the Benson “school,” as it is called, that the
handsome Oscar Asche began to learn how to turn
his brains and fine physique to dramatic account.
He went from Australia to Norway, in the first
place, and studied stage business on the plain,
straightforward lines of Ibsen, whose characters
do the most unnatural things in a matter-of-fact
way. After that, Asche went to England and
took instruction from Walter Lacy, a master of
elocution and robust, romantic style. With the
Benson co. he got practical experience, whilst
developing his own ideas of dramatic technique.
This seems to be the explanation of Oscar Asche’s
success—a success that promises to grow into fame.
He started with a strong personality, set himself
to acquire the art of naturalness on the stage,
and then became an actor.

At Her Majesty’s (Melb.), the demand for
Italian opera seems wayward and uncertain.
“Otello,” billed for three nights only, drew a
great house to the third performance, and is being
played again this week—on Thursday. But
Ponchielli’s “La Gioconda,” a more frankly
melodious work with a delightful ballet to com-
mend it to people of moderately musical taste,
was no tiling of a success compared with Verdi’s
majestic masterpiece. They were knocked by
“Otello,” whereas “ Gioconda” merely tickled
them. Perhaps the libretto of the latter opera
is too awful for any public that nowadays
meets it for the first time. “La Gioconda”
surpasses “II Tro.” and “ Ernani ” in tragic
lunacy. The heroine is a sort of Maritana
afflicted with a blind mother in a dingy black
dress, and the tenor’s other girl is a married lady
who gets lured to his full-rigged lugger by a
champion villain, after which the said villain
brings her husband along to catch her in flagrante
delicto, as the programme narrative expresses it.
Also, the Mantana spinster starts to murder her
rival, but is checked at the outset of her crime by
perceiving a rosary which her blind parent had
given to the other lady as a preservative. So the
jealous husband takes over the task of killing his
wife, but when he thinks he has got her dead, and
summons the famous ballet and the admirable
chorus to inspect her corpse during some
Government House festivities, the supposed
victim is not defunct at all. Her spinster rival
has saved her by exchanging her dose of poison
for a sleeping-draught, in order to spare the
tenor’s feelings and leave him with two girls
instead of one. Finally the spinster donna (Bene-
detto) takes the poison herself, after promising to
marry the champion villain in consideration of his
sending her loved Larizza away with the other
woman. In an Italian way of speaking, the “La
Gioconda ” libretto is a little bit too thick.
* * * *
“La Traviata,” which transpired on
Saturday, is a sweet old opera wherein the leading
airs sound like quotations from a volume of concert
music and the orchestration often runs on the lines
of a banjo accompaniment. Those airs are probably
immortal. They were.composed in the “II Trova-
tore ” time, when Verdi was full of song and senti-
ment, and each new “mash” suggested a new
tune to the man of naughty forty. But it is
doubtful whether “ La Traviata ” gains or loses in
effect by its slavish fidelity to the tale of
“ Camille.” One likes a grand opera to be backed
up by pale moon and painted foliage in some of the
scenes. All the action of “La Traviata ” happens
within three stage walls. It is a small-sized opera
in every respect, and, although the declamatory
section of Williamson’s co.—Larizza, Benedetto,
and CatadOri —would perhaps be too furious to be
trusted to carry on amidst such fragile furniture,
it is certain that the unemotional Dani, Sapio, and
Bozzoli err in the opposite direction. They sing
the music very fetchingly, nevertheless. The
sweet airs that fall to the baritone in the second
act, and the hackneyed duet for tenor and soprano
in the final are things to be heard on Friday for
the last time. “ Fedora ”is due on Saturday, and
then comes the last week of the brightest and most
fruitful operatic season that Melbourne has
known.
*# * *
At the Royal there was a change of bill
on Saturday, when the “War of Wealth’’came
along to fill in the yawning gulf before Xmas.
This is the drama which begins and finishes on
North Shore, Sydney, and spends the rest of its
time at Redfern Railway Station and a bank in
George-st. Most of the dialogue relates to in-
vestments and securities, and the principal situa-
tion introduces a crowd of sordid depositors who
want their money back from Walter Baker because
they have heard on good authority that they can’t
get it. And, just as Baker’s bank is about to burst,
Bland Holt arrives with £lOO,OOO concealed about
his waggon, and Albert Norman calls upon the
excited customers to present their cheques, what
time Manager Baker lies insensible on the floor.
Then they rush to convert their paper into coin,
and the widow and the fatherless sit upon Baker’s
body to count their money, and the wicked
director, who had compassed the bank’s ruin on
account of Mrs. Bland Holt, is handed over to
justice in the last act. Brunton’s scene paintings
would be ample apology for a more improbable
story than “The War of Wealth.” Also the per-
formance is strong and hearty, except in the case
of Miss Ross, who has dropped back into her
languishing flop-hat style.
** * *
The Brough season, at Melb. Princess’s,
was to end with a revival of “ Sowing the Wind ”
on Wednesday of this week, and to-night (Thurs-
day) the theatre will be,gin to be closed for the
preparation of George Musgrove’s big Xmas show.
The Broughs gave “Mrs. Dane’s Defence” for
seven nights. H. A. Jones’s story is a
powerful warning against the foolishness of
taking one’s Past to the wrong locality for inter-
ment. Likewise it goes to show that marriage is
apt to prove a dismal failure whether the lady be
quite respectable or otherwise. In fact, it would
be a very useful play if it offered any reasonable
suggestion for making both sexes happy without
making them regret it afterwards. Mrs. Brough
(as Mrs. Dane) and the young man who worships
her in a labored monotone are the least satisfac-
tory members of the cast, Miss Susie Vaughan,
Miss Temple, and the somewhat rigid Garry
being the most satisfactory, whilst Brough, Miss
Chatteris, and Leslie Victor follow the leaders
round the track.
m *• * #
The World’s Entertainers are doing well
at the Bijou. Charles Sweet’s humorous intrusion
appears to be more popular than even the two
men who bounce themselves on a billiard-table,
although the sweetness of Charles, which is linked
by a pair of baggy pants, seems rather long
drawn out. Henry Lee impersonates the same
half-dozen characters as before, and his eminently
unlikely Shakespeare is received with confiding
applause, but the fact that he hasn’t attempted
Barton or George Turner, or any other local sub-
ject, throws a shadow of doubt on his claim to talk
like Kipling, Chamberlain, and Lord Roberts.
** m *
At the Opera House nothing has occurred
to weaken the general opinion that the Craggs
amount to a full half of an entertainment in them-
selves. For which matter Louis Lohr bears
witness that in days of yore he ran the Craggs as
a whole show at Adelaide Exhibition-building, and
scored an enormous success. Eight Craggs, two
Allisons and a Peggy who stands in her Pryde
alone, are chief items of the expensive bill at
Rickards’s, and they ought to be a strong enough
crowd without reinforcements. But on Saturday
somebody else will arrive from America.
** # *
All this while, Fitzgerald Bros.’ Circus
remains an old-established fact at the Fitzes’
township, on the south side of Princes’ Bridge.
The “Arab wonder,” whose name begins with
Hadji, the Dunbars, the slim and pleasing Lady
Juggler, and the hurricane acts that don’t act in
the least like a hurricane, are produced eight
times a week. Also, a Beautiful White Lady
on a stately black horse is said to be “of
Dusseldorf,” and is an elegant vision in any case.
Likewise, a Clowns’ Ballet was lately added
to the show to make it more various. The Clowns’
Ballet is the prettiest thing the Fitzgeralds have
put into their big ring thusfar. It comes from
Germany, has a quaintness altogether different
from the humor of a French clown ballet, and a
feeling of circus fairy-tale in its happenings. The
Ballet, which takes 50 women and men to put it
through, will be continued over Xmas.
A sign of the times—a sign of Sydney
Daily Telegraph, anyhow. “G. A. Wood ”is
permitted by the D.T. to make a few observa-
tions :
Your Capetown correspondent writes about the “ con-
centration camps.” He has just “run across” the
statistics of mortality for the month of Sept. They were
published in the D.T. several weeks ago under the head-
ing, “ The Horrors of War. Terrible Infant Mortality.”
You pointed out that the figures meant “an
average death-rate of about 433 per 1000 per
annum.” Your correspondent remarks on these figures
that “ the low death-rate is a fresh testimonial to the
extreme care that has been taken to entertain the
nation’s by no means grateful guests.” The notion seems
to be that Lord Kitchener will be acting with quite exces-
sive humanity if, in the course of a year, he exterminates
more than one-third of the whole child population. On
the same sheet you print “ a graphic story ” from Mr.
D. J. Amos, of the Imperial Bushmen. Mr. Amos tells
how he and his comrades attacked a small party
of Boers, who were defeated, and ran away. The story
then proceeds as follows :—“ Ah ! here’s open country at
last. Johnny Boer won’t follow us here, for we are
superior to him in point of numbers, so we halt for half-
an hour, and burn a stray farmhouse as some slight relief
to our feelings. It is now raining steadily—a rain that is
like melted snow—and the unfortunate women and
children, whom we found in the farmhouse, are left
behind in the wet, without blankets or shelter of any
kind. Forward once again into the rain and darkness;
it’s much too cold for us to ride, so we sling our rifles to
our saddles and trudge forward on foot.”
I make no remark on this “ graphic story.” I have no
language at all equal to the occasion ; and if I had I am
quite sure that you, sir, would not print it. I only beg
permission to ask one question. Is there not in Sydney
enough English patriotism to protest against a policy
that is bringing everlasting infamy upon the English
name ?
Just think of the change in public feeling which
permits the Randal-handle organ giving circula-
tion to instead of throttling such foul blasphemy !

Vanning Tables.—The very newest and what
gives exceedingly fair promise of superseding all other
tables is the machine known as the Wesley-Baird Vanning
Table. The table is of South Australian invention, and
has given excellent results on Broken Hill ores and ores
containing pyrites, carbonate of copper and tin. Like all
tables, the best work is done on “ fines,” and more espe-
cially when the product is properly classified. The
Central Mining and Tramway Co., of 424 Little Collins-
street, Melbourne, and 40 Hunter-street, Sydney, are the
sole agents in the Eastern States. The table has a capa-
city up to 30 tons per day.*

* • • *
A very successful trial of the Gardner Electric
Drill took place at the works of the Austral Otis Com-
pany, South Melbourne, a few days ago. The drill,
although only temporarily fixed, bored inches in three
minutes through hard basalt. A number of prominent
mining men witnessed the test, and expressed themselves
as greatly pleased with the machine. The Austral Otis
Company have been appointed Sole Agents in Australasia
for the Gardner Electric Drill. *

* • * *
The State Treasurers continue to issue their
monthly financial returns in a way that makes comparison
practically worthless. Queensland, N.S.W. and South
Australia follow the sensible course of showing only the
purely State revenue— i.e., the revenue from State
sources and the amount of customs, excise and postal
revenue returned to the State by the Commonwealth
Treasurer. Victoria and Westralia mix State and Com-
monwealth revenues together, and the State Treasurer
therefore includes in his accounts income which is no
longer his. Tasmania shows its accounts both ways. All
the States issue monthly returns save S.A. which gives
its figures quarterly. N.S.W., Queensland and Westralia
issue monthly expenditure returns ; the other States
don’t. N.S.W. and Queensland are the only States which
issue monthly statements of loan expenditure. If a
Federal Statistician were only appointed, and he could
persuade all these confused Treasurers to supply him
monthly with full particulars of State revenue, and State
ordinary and loan expenditure, and publish them along
with the corresponding Federal statistics, he would be
an important blessing. Meanwhile available figures
are
State revenues only, as compared with total revenues
for corresponding period last year.

Queensland has still the whole year’s interest on its

£528,188, and things are already £39,819 worse than they
were at the corresponding date of 1900. On the other
hand loan expenditure for the five months is £462,882
against £519,532 up to 30th Nov., 1900, a decrease of
£56 650 The net result is that revenue has decreased
£304 102, and expenditure £320,933, which means a
general improvement of £16,831 in the financial position.

As regards the other States, the one that
presents the most lamentable aspect is Tasmania.
Even if there had been no Federation, and it had
retained the whole of its excise, customs and postal
revenue, it would have shown a decline of over £68,000,
and with its share of the Federal expenditure to provide
for, and a reduced tariff and the loss of its inter-State
customs ahead, the position could hardly be much worse.
Under these circumstances the Government had a hazy
idea about doing something extra in the way of direct
taxation, but promptly thought worse of it, and decided
to let the State finances slide.

South Australia is starting to sell 3J per cent.
Treasury bills on the local market, with a view to raising
£850,000. Every State except Victoria has now floated a
good-sized loan this financial year, and Victoria will
probably be heard from before long.

The earnings represent £l2 6s. Id. per cent, per annum
on the shareholders’ funds as at commencement of half-
year, as compared with £7 6s. Bd. per cent, for half-year
to March, 1901. Paid-up capital is £lOO,OOO, so, in pro-
portion to capital, the VVestralian Bank has the largest
reserves in Australasia. It is also the largest dividen
payer, and has been so for the past seven years or more.
The Westralian Bank holds £1,794,944 of de-
posits, over £1,000,000 of which bear no interest, which
probably accounts, in part, for its extraordinary profits.
Its cash reserves are enormous (£1,097,888, equal to 54
per cent, of all liabilities to the public). It also holds
£49,400 in State securities—an item which, judging by
the appearance of things, it could well afford to increase
to £200,000 at least. The deposits have increased by
nearly £60,000 during the half-year, and the note-issue by
over £20,000. Taking one thing with another, the
Westralian Bank is a remarkably fortunate institution.

* * * •
It is reported that the Colonial Mutual Eire In-
surance Go., of Melbourne, one of the most prosperous of
the Australian offices, will probably take over the United
Australian Mutual Fire 00. of the same city. Latter
hasn’t paid a dividend since 1896, it has only £293 of re-
serves even on paper, and it created them by writing up
the paper value of its properties by £3672 in 1899. It has
no reinsurance reserve, and altogether its prospects look
very blank. Yet it has a respectable little business—
there are three smaller cos. in Australasia which are
prospering handsomely. In the matter of losses its luck
was good enough ; according to the Year Book, in 1895 its
proportion of losses to net premiums was the third lowest
among 20 offices enumerated ; in 1896, fifth lowest; in
1897, third lowest among 17 offices ; in 1899-1900 it was
again third lowest. But its expenses, as reported in
the Year Book, look appalling. In 1895 it had the
heaviest expense rate of all; in 1896, the highest save two
offices; in 1897, the highest save one office ; for the 18
months to June, 1899, and again in 1900 it had the
highest expense rate —about twice the Australasian
average. Much the same thing happened again in 1901.
The Colonial Mutual Firs is an excellently managed office,
whereof Mr. Hague Smith is the secretary in Sydney, and
its balance-sheet, published in this issue, is a striking
example of economical working. It could probably make
a steady £4OOO a year profit out of the United Australian
Mutual’s business, but the business appears to be of little
use to the U.A.M. itself.

The Universal Permanent Building and Investment
Society has a paid-up capital of £128,648, so, on paper at
all events, about £105,000 is still intact. But for two
serious troubles the position would look very favorable.
In the first place, £171,699 worth of securities are
still put in at book value, and though a very strong
revival has set in there may still be a good deal of depre-
ciation to face. This wouldn’t matter so much if the
business profits could be set against it, but the co.
owes £38,000 on 4 per cent. B debentures—interest on
which is paj'able out of profits. Profits not having
hitherto been available no interest has been paid, but as
soon as the necessary earnings are realised this liability
must be faced. The A debentures at 5 per cent, and B
debentures at 4 per cent, are a heavy load to
carry. The most promising part of the show is that
earnings are increasing somewhat, and the load of
liabilities is being steadily reduced. The net liabilities to
debenture-holders, depositors, mortgagees and bank, less
cash on hand, have been as follows :
1895 £156,907 .. 1899 £106,223
1896 143,843 .. 1900 96,925
1897 119,645 .. 1901 86,906
1898 110,108
This is a very material shifting of the dead weight of
liabilities, and with reasonable luck the Universal should
have a fair prospect in a few years (perhaps a good many
years) of getting on a dividend-paying basis again in a
small way.
* * • *
A small lawsuit, in which the Australian Metro-
politan Life Assurance Co. was lately concerned, led to an
interesting cross-examination of the managing director,
Terence John Harrold. It transpired, among other
things, that the manager and a director, Dr. Ure, owned
more than half the shares between them. An annual
meeting was attended by two shareholders, both em-
ployees of the co., and the manager and directors. Ap-
parently the co. is mainly the manager, directors, and
these two employees, who elect themselves, and meet
annually to report to themselves what they have done,
and to decide unanimously and with enthusiasm that
their own management is Al. This is the co. which
bought an office building in Brisbane for about £13,000,
and created (on paper, anyhow) a large and pros-
perous assurance fund by putting it in the
balance-sheet as worth £30,000. Most of the
little proprietary life offices which have lately swarmed
into existence in these States appear to be owned almost
entirely by the directors and staff, who meet once a year
in one capacity to rigidly overhaul their own doings in
another capacity and remorselessly sack themselves if
they have done wrong. Also, three such offices have
greatly improved their position (on paper) by writing
up the office building.

The reserves are a miscellaneous collection : reserve fund,
£15,500 ; dividend equalisation fund, £3500; forfeited
share capital, £2,310; and undivided profit, £2367. It
would be a vast amount more business-like if these were
all combined to make one respectable reserve ; especially
the policy of leaving forfeited capital lying around loose
in the balance-sheet, instead of appropriating it to any
fund, is somewhat remarkable. The Intercolonial Invest-
ment, Land, and Building (Jo. has a paid-up capital of
£35,802, so the shareholders’ funds total £59,479, and its
position looks solid. At the same time it has a growing
overdraft—£2o76 in 1899 ; £5914 in 1900 ; and £6531 in
1901, which is not a desirable feature in the case.
* * * *
Significant of the “depression” in the lead
market is the diminution in the number of men at work
in the Broken Hill mines. At end of 1900 there were
7260 men at work on the surface and underground ; but a
census taken the other day'showed only 4860—a decrease
of 2400. The mines temporarily shut-down, of course,
show the biggest difference. The British staff has fallen
from 622 to 4, the North from 234 to 14, and the Junction
North from 250 to 15. The Sulphide Corporation, how-
ever, has increased its hands from 870 to 950 and has
added 200 men to its Cockle Creek staff. Directly
dependent on the B.H. mines, and in addition to the
4860, are the Proprietary’s 1250 men at Port Pirie, the
Sulphide Corporation’s 600 at Cockle Creek, and Block
14’s 350 at Port Adelaide, swelling the gross to 7060. Not
since ’9s—when the Barrier was in the throes of the
sulphide difficulty and it was a toss-up whether the field
would live or die—have so few men as at present earned
their living in the mines.
* * * *
It is not likely that there will be any strike at
Broken Hill, but there will be much grumbling ; and no
wonder. The cos. that seek to cut wages are working the
poorest mines left open : and the clearest-seeing of the
men realise that most, if not all, of these cos. would just
as soon shut down altogether, as the Junction, British,
and others have already done. Many of the shareholders
would, as a matter of fact, rather lock up and wait for the
metal recovery that is bound to come some day. And the
men are helpless also in another way : except as to the
mechanics they are wretchedly organised, and have no
fighting funds. The surface laborers—the poorest paid of
all, and the men who do the most cruel work in the open-
cuts —are scarcely touched by the unions. Even the
miners are only moderately organised ; and though some
of their officers are fire-spitters, the rank-and-file includes
the largest proportion of old hands—the men who battled
so magnificently through the great strike of ’92, and
drank so much bitterness then that they hate the very
word “ strike.” That ’92 strike .narked the failure
of unionism under modern industrial conditions, and the
men, thinking the unions of less value than they really
are, turned to politics for salvation through compulsory
arbitration and otherwise. So the unions weakened ; and,
compulsory arbitration having not even yet quite arrived,
the workmen go down between the two stooh. Yet an
arbitration court, if there were one, would most likely,
in view of the smashing falls in lead prices, award the
B.H. co’s the relief they seek ; though much of the work
is so hard, the cost of living so high, and the conditions
of life so wretched that a court with even a lawyer at its
head might well blush as it pronounced this sentence—
7s. for working in the open-cuts, and Bs. for risking
everything underground.
* * • •
“Sessil”: Emmaville (N.S.W.) tin-field has
fallen on rather evil days. Some months ago, the “ Y
Waterhole ” syndicate informed its 50 to 60 tributors
that it required 15 per cent., instead of 10 per cent, as
hitherto exacted. This, of course, is the God-given
mission of syndicates. Tributors bucked, and in two
months syndicate surrendered. The property of the
celebrated Gulf Lode Tin-Mining Co. —the richest, for a
time, in Australia —is now quite deserted. It is a mining
c.p., and its Sydney owners neither work nor let it. Close
by is old-hand M. W. Carr’s “ Red Cross.” Carr has been
18 years on the lode. His stope is 30ft. ; his vein aver-
ages 3in., and his returns 74 or 75 per cent, from lode tin.
The country is granite, surrounded by quartzite, and
carries some galena and wolfram. Saw some splendid tin
specimens, similar to those accepted by the “ Dock ” from
same claim. Tent Hill battery is at work yet. At “ Nine-
Mile,” co.—fortified with Govt, aid—sunk 159 ft. on true
bottom (others say on dip-sidling of old bed). Found
very little wash, so hastily humped its machinery and
silently stole away. Developments awaited with despair
by local people. Latter are also getting up petition
against further extension of three months idleness craved
by the Little Plant Silver-mining Co. This nervy cor-
poration gutted its property for a time, and spent at the
rate of £12,000 a year in the district. Then it was in
such a hurry that the Govt, inspector shut it down, and
gave it a month to make its mine secure. Co. seems to
have turned sulky—has received further extensions, con-
sisting of 12 following months, and is yelling now for
three more.
» * * *
North Nymagee (N.S.W.): The latest: “ Coun-
try passed through composed principally of mineralised
slate with small veinlets of quarts and yellow sulphide
ore through it. The water is highly mineralised and is
very strong in copper. This proves that we are in the
vicinity of good copper ore, which may be met with at
any time.” It does nothing of the kind. It is one thing
to have water impregnated with copper in solution, and
quite another to find a good copper-lode. For instance,
Blayney mine, if the copper in the water was any proof of
the richness of the ore body, would be ten times as big a
show. There are some good copper-mines with very little
copper in their water, and vice versa.
» » » *
Yambulla Goldfield, N.S.W.: “ Three practical
miners from Westralia have arrived here. Many miners
are of opinion that Yambulla will rival Wyalong, but
capital is required for the further development of the
resources of the field.” Quite a number of small crush-
ings of Ito 3oz. ore have taken place. Reefs small but
consistent. If several of the blocks were to amalgamate,
they might make one co. with working capital enough to
deal with the sulphide zone which, sooner or later, the
mines there will have to face.
* * » *
N.S.W. dredges have bumped terribly the last
few months. The pity of it is that those who suffer
are chiefly poor people whose hard-earned pounds mostly
went to fill the pockets of the dredging expert and pro-
moter. Particularly on Shoalhaven River and its tribu-
taries the inevitable (as here pointed out 18 months
ago) has come with a vengeance. The Terraces
closed and sold to Araluen Consolidated. Shoal-
baven Gold Dredging Co. closed. Braidwood Pro-
prietary getting a little gold and has a big law-suit
pending. The Colombo’s recent for a week
is not much more successful than its sister dredge higher
up. The Federal has closed down because it could"not
get gold to pay expenses. The Araluen dredges are
getting more gold than any of the others, but a dreadful
lot goes in repairs and keeping offices in Svdney. Araluen
Junction, which is not in Araluen proper but some miles
on the Muruiya side of the valley, will soon have the
dredge laid up, as there is not enough water, but “ will
have splendid returns as soon as such and such a
face is cut out,” and so on. And so with many other
N. S. W. dredges. The successes will probably not average
one in ten. It is bad enough in M.L., the declared home
of the dredging industry, where to-day there are more
dredges silent than working—where the boom has faded,
thousands of people have lost money in it, hundreds
of families have been ruined by it, and where
at last foundries are looking for other work than
dredge-building. The public rushed into a thing they did
not understand. Because it was new it could not fail.
Most, if not all the money, could have been saved by
patience, caution, and a little discrimination ; for the
ground, whether in a river, beside it, or on an alluvial
flat, could be tested as to its gold contents as well as its
suitability for dredging for less shillings than it takes
pounds to build a dredge.
* * * •
Those who are sticking to the Araluen Junction
Gold Dredging Co. (N.S.W.), are going to make another
bid for fortune, but whether they will reach the goal this
time remains to be seen. A fresh co. has been formed
out of the sludge of the old concern, and has acquired the
dredge and assets fo» £llOO. The new co. is in 30,000
shares of 2s. each, Is. per share payable on application.
Shares were first offered to the original shareholders, and,
failing them, to the public. The dredge will be moved at
once on to the Araluen Creek, where good and consistent
returns are, in the usual way, anticipated. When weren’t
they?
• • * *
Chillagoe Railway and Mines: Since writer’s
last reference to this gigantic fiasco, meetings of share-
holders have taken place, directors’ proposals have been
rejected, and they have felt themselves so offended that
they promise all to resign next year and to submit them-
selves for re-election. They have made other promises,
none of them very substantial ; all, barring John
Moffatt, agreed to find £50,000 to help the co. out of
its difficulties. Good, old man Moffatt declined
on the ground that he had held his shares all along and
was therefore not in a position to put up more than the
liability represented by his holding. There is no way out
but reconstruction—which probably means making
all the shares contributing to the extent of 4s. or
ss. each. Many fools are now engaged in buying
Chillagoes because they are cheap. Some started
with that idea when they were 10s., others when they
were down to 55., and now they have the opportunity of
continuing the operation at 2s. If they like to wait a
little longer, they will probably get them for a shilling,
and, if they exercise much patience, they will probably
receive thousands at a gift. How many of these men
ever realise, in their blind faith in Stewart, that the
debenture-issue already represents a liability of some-
thing like 10s. a share, for this money must some day
either be paid or the property lost! The security is not
regarded as too good, or the £lOO debentures carry-
ing 6 per cent, interest would not be selling at £7O
to £BO each. Imagine anybody buying Chillagoes
with such an anomaly staring them in the face, with
the copper-market rapidly falling, and with little hope
of any substantial help from the men who sold their
shares at a big profit. Herbert J. Daley, one of the
few well-known mining men who condemned this scheme
in its infancy and several times since it started, recalls
the fact per medium of the Argus. He said of the group,
some three years back, that, although some rich ore
had been extracted, and was still obtainable in
limited quantities, its occurrence was confined to
short shutes and irregular deposits, and in many
cases the veins were too small for profitable mining.
“ Bob Moore ” unhesitatingly condemned the Chillagoe
mines every time he went there. G. A. Stockfeld said
of them ten years ago that the deposits were irregular
and erratic, that they did not live in depth, that their
treacherous character was accounted for by the formation
surrounding them, that the mines were too widely
separated to be of any value, and if the ore of the various
groups were all in one, then it would not be a huge mine.
Over three years ago, when the Chillagoe boom started,
he revisited the camp, and reiterated his opinion after
another exhaustive examination, but was laughed at for
his pains.

SOCIETY
As Australians will know, the Duke of
York was accompanied on his tour by a literary
man whose business it was to act as “ghost ” and
put words into the mouth of his harmless, in-
sipilicaiit little master. But now that the
utterly -commonplace Duke has got back to
England, the Australian press actually has the
gall to print cablegrams setting forth the Duke’s
opinions concerning the political and social con-
ditions of this country ! Just as if the Australian
people really believed the Duke aforesaid capable
ot forming conclusions upon any subject more
profound than whether it was time to light
another cigarette or uncork another whisky-and-
* * * »
Moore, who shot solicitor Abigail’s man-
aging clerk in Sydney, last week, and then
smcided was apparently a nervous man driven
mad by the sloth and costliness of the law. And
He was an inefficient man as well as a nervous
one, for in a city that is full of solicitors he only
succeeded in damaging a clerk who was, appa-
Tik 7 ’ .. ,n n° way responsible for his troubles.
Alter all, though, it is a foolish person who goes
shooting at lawyers these times. A dead lawyer
is out of his misery, anyhow ; alive one lingers on
to note the increasing scarcity of litigation and
the growing tendency to arbitrate or settle the
case out of court, to curse Torrens’ Act, which
cheapened and simplified conveyancing*, and to
see the constant inrush of new solicitors in a pro-
fession where there is less and less to solicit, and
new barristers who have no bar to go to except the
threepenny one. And now that it is proposed to
a olish the free lunch, even the threepenny bar
may soon cease to be what it was.

Since Dutton raged some years ago among
the fat cavalry officers who couldn’t ride and the
fatuous infantry officers who couldn’t swear, the
comc-opera eiement has gradually crept back into
JN.b.W. military forces. In a Sydney police
court case last week, before a plain-clothes magis-
trate named Snnthers, one gallant officer was fined
40s. for calling another gallant officer “a d-
liar and ad oad.” But it was explained that
the words were spoken in a quiet way ” ; other-
wise, doubtless, the stain of them would have had
to be wiped out in gore.
* * * *
During the plague which seriously
threatens to recur, in view of the many bubonic
rats lately found dead—the filthy practice of keep-
mg poultry in upstairs rooms in Sydney was for-
badell - Now, within a stone’s throw of the Town
Hall, poultry may be seen fluttering and roosting
at a wire-netted window on the third floor of
certain premises. The old-world peasant, with
his pig under his bed, has an equal in the Austra-
lian who keeps hens in .a bedroom.
* * * *
The returned N.S. W. cohentingenter is still
demanding an extra Is. 2d. per day, and is
resolved to make Premier See’s life a misery till
he gets it. The question is whether he was to
get 4s. 6d. a day, which was how most people
understood the matter, or 4s. 6d. together with
Is. 2d. a day of Imperial pay, making ss. Bd. a
day in all; also any trifle of loot that came in his
way. The cohentmgenter wants to get his ss. Bd.,
and to pose as a self-sacrificing patriot at the
same time, hence the deafening row. The spec-
tacle of the Cornstalk troops— the best-paid army
in the world—demanding Is. 2d. a day over and
above their bargain throws an interesting side-
light on Australian discipline, and may possibly
convey a valuable hint to foreign nations. They
will probably arrange to invade Australia at some
period of scarcity when the Government can’t
afford to pay more than 3s. 6d., and thus take
Australia’s five-and-eightpenny army at a disad-
vantage.
* * * •
At Newington, Parramatta River, N.S.W.
Govt, treats its aged citizens as a gentleman
should. There are cottages for old married
couples, where, amid comfortable surroundings,
life’s sun may set in brightness. Many people
who go over Newington Asylum are moved to
hope that when their twilight comes they will
be able to claim and share one of the cot-
tages with “the missus.” But a missus is
essential; the cottages are for married
couples only. This was made pathetically
clear to one old man, on the wrong side of 70,
who, with his wife, had long shared a Newington
cottage. The wife died, and the widower was
told that he must be removed to Parramatta
asylum. After sorrowfully thinking over the
prospect, he asked for leave of absence for three
days, and at the end of the time presented himself
at the gates—smiling, confident, debonair —
accompanied by a bride about 80, whom he
had wooed and won, expecting as a dowry
renewed right of tenure of a Newington cottage.
But callous Authority interfered with this little
plan, and the couple were separated—the bride-
groom being sent to Parramatta, and the bride
remaining at Newington.

As here surmised, the death-sentence on
Kuhnell, the supposed Red fern poisoner, has been
commuted. Capital punishment will, however,
be inflicted on Campbell, the sex - maniac,
as by hanging him the Law hopes to make some
sort of illogical atonement to society for its crime
in ever allowing the miserable wretch his freedom
after a first offence had shown his dangerous in-
sanity. But no matter—while Australians are
burning the farmhouses of men and women
nghtly struggling to be free, ” there is no room
for any fuss about such a little thing as the
hanging of a madman.

By an error in the recent Act consolidat-
ing the N.S.W. vagrancy laws it would seem that
a conviction in certain cases is to be followed by a
surgical operation.
* * * *
As the Criminal Court-house at Darling-
hurst, which has done duty since 1842, is now
considered unsafe, it is being partially demolished
and re-built, the original stone and decorations
being preserved and used again. The interior
will be much altered. Among other changes, the
dock from which so many eminent criminals, from
John Knatchbull to Prank Butler, have gone to
their doom will be modernised.

Near Henty (N.S.W.) a baby girl, six
weeks old, was found lying close to a gate by two
farmers, Who were attracted by her feeble cries.
She was carefully wrapped in shawls, with a
bundle of baby clothes and a feeding-bottle along-
side ; also a note stating that mother was in diffi-
culties, and forced to part with baby, that baby’s
name was Dolly, a,nd she hoped some kind person
would send the little one to Albury orphanage.

• * * *
A recent case in Vic. Supreme Court cried
shame on society. The mother of an illegitimate
was discovered perambulating Collingwood with
the dead infant in her arms. It was in a dirty
state, and had been accidentally burned. The
mother had taken it to the hospital, where its
wounds had been dressed. But society makes no
provision for the keep of such children, and the
mother friendless, homeless, moneyless—wan-
dered about till a policeman arrested her for being
illegally m possession of a dead baby, or some-
thmg of that sort. At her trial, the woman—
though charged with manslaughter—was unde-
fended. Mr. Justice Holroyd told the jury that
the mother of every child was bound to bestow
due care upon it, and to feed and clothe it pro-
perly. And when she can’t do that she is a
guilty woman ! Por less than was wasted on the
ducal shivoos a well-equipped Foundling Hospital
might have been established. Society has the
power to do its duty and neglects it; but it
punishes those who offend from sheer impotence.
One Howell, fairly well-known in Syd-
ney, has been sentenced at Nowra (N.S.W.)
to nine months gaol for obtaining sums of money
from local magnates by representing himself as a
London Times man seeking biographical records
of the great men of Australia. This ancient
swindle is always worked on the strength of the
London Times. The weird improbability of the
JLimes sending out a man to beg guineas from
Alderman Blank, of Blankyville, as the price of
printing his biography side by side with that of
the last dead statesman, never seems to strike the
bucolic mind.

The churches which go in for the hysterical
business, so marked during the recent simul-
taneous mission in Sydney, are really cutting their
own throats by preparing the way for big defec-
tions at the call of fakirs like Elijah Dowie,
Theocratic Unity Horos, and other adepts in
humbug. Much better the old, solemn way, with
no jokes and no hysteria. It is safer and surer for
the Plate in the long run.
“FIGHT ON!”
Jones reads
that the tailor-
esses have forced
the manufactur-
ers to pay 4½d.
more for making pants, and, determined
not to be a party to his surrender to the
beastly working classes, decides not to wear ’em.
One incident of the Sydney simultaneous
mission suggests a valuable clerical reform. Too
long has tyrant custom ordained that a clergyman
must be allowed to inflict his sermons for so long
as he pleases on a suffering congregation. In
future every pastor, ere holding forth, should take
a vote of his audience as to whether he should
“ Shut Up,” or “Let Her Go, Gallagher.”

What the ingenuous clergyman said to the
ladies at a big feminine meeting in Melbourne is
the best smoke-room story of the recent Spring
season. And everybody sees the joke bar the
clergyman;
At a recent Sydney “Parliamen-
tary picnic ” — hie —the Govt, launch
managed to run down the cable of th.i
Spit punt and dislocate the ferry service.
Accidents at these “picnics” are very
common. Many months ago a Govt,
launch with a gallant Minister, some ladies,
and some gold-top aboard, ran down a very
prominent labor member who was fishing
(without ladies or gold-top) and nearly
killed him. That gave somebody a fright,
as press publicity was narrowly escaped, and
the Govt, launch picnic temporarily lost some
of its frequency—also some of its hilarious-
ness. But it seems to be breaking out again
badly.
Telling (and probably true) statement by
a mission parson:
At the time of the boot-box tragedy, in Melbourne, no
less than 600 mothers viewed the head of the victim in
the morgue to see if it would throw any light upon the
whereabouts of their lost daughters!
The number of disappeared daughters (or sons) in
Australia is one of the symptoms of the “ casual-
ness ” of the national character.

Another tram - accident verdict against
N.S.W. Railway Commissioners—damages this
time £l2OO and costs. By the time the Com-
missioners reckon up costs and damages for the
year they will be rather pleased that fair hours for
the tram running-staff are to be enforced for Iho
future. With shorter hours, decent provision for
meals, and more time for rest, a decrease in
casualties may reasonably be expected.

In Kalgoorlie (W.A.), there is one street,
the lower part of which is a row of small, detached
cottages, mostly built on the same plan
—a door, with a window on each
side. The inhabitants of
these cots are perfect ladies,
of various nationalities,
who are to be seen lol-
ling about in bright-
colored raiment of
more-or-lessreveal-
ing type. At
nig n t, the
whole row is
brilliantly
lit up ; a
“scarlet
stain” occu-
pies each
window, and
the passer-by
is addressed
sotto voce, in
foreign ac-
cents —if a
policeman
doesn’t hap-
pen to be in
sight.
Visitors to
Kalgoor-
1i e are
taken along
to see this
street as one of the
sights of the town. The
ladies keep to their premises,
and are, to the eye of the law,
simply “looking out de vindow.”
So they go unmolested, and have
done so for years. A syndicate of
foreign gentlemen—perfect gentlemen
—run the show and import the ladies.
Coolgardie had them in the
days of its champagne and
four-in-hands, but when
the gold left Coolgardie
the ladies left also. Kal-
-goorlie doesn’t somehow
seem to be equal to the
task of throwing them out. An
effort was lately made to transfer
the lot to an outside quarter of the
town —like a Japanese yoshiwarra,
but it failed.

A Bulletin correspondent:—
Recently saw in Liverpool-street, Sydney, a policeman
and two other men carrying a drunken woman to the
lock-up. They had her heels in the air, with her head
dangling about anyhow. And when they wanted a
spell” they deposited her, face-downwards, in the dirt.
She was apparently insensible, so that her carriers had
not even the excuse of resistance.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I have heard that a lover, seized
With ardour for his mistress dear,
Is like one parched who, water near,
Would fain risk all to be appeased.
And sure a maiden ought, I think,
To pity such a grievous drouth,
And try at least if with her mouth—
(Art thirsty, Sweetheart? Come and drink !)

South Australia is the only State that has
done anything serious in the way of cutting down
the local Legislature, and the Upper House has
undone a good part of the work. The Assembly’s
proposal was to reduce its membership from 54 to
42 and the Council from 24 to 18 (the Council
altered this to 21). The Assembly’s proposal for
a double dissolution next year, both Houses to
start fresh, disappears. The deadlock clauses,
copied from the Federal Constitution, disappear
also. When all is finished, there is no visible
reform, except a reduction of membership and a
paring-down of the miserably insufficient Minis-
terial salaries. There is a chance now for the
Premier to make a rousing fight; but, alas,
Jenkins isn’t a Kingston.
Not content with expelling Tocsin pub-
lisher, Edward Findley, Vic. State Ministry has
resurrected for that print’s special benefit an obso-
lete law which compels every newspaper proprietor
to find £3OO security that he will publish no
sedition. No other paper is asked to provide
security. Several State Labor members offered
to act as sureties, but Magistrate Panton wouldn’t
accept them. Federal members were thereupon
secured in King O’Malley and Fowler. The
P.M. somewhat aggressively asked Fowler what
he was worth. “ About £18,000,” replied Fowler.
“ About £lB,OOO ?” repeated the Magistrate with
some awe. “Yes, about that,” sharply returned
Fowler, “but I can’t say to within a few
thousands.” The sureties were then accepted.

and Troubles.
“ Jeff.”: Apropos the additions to Sydney’s
Prince Alfred Hospital. The people who run
that institution contend that it is for the training
of medical students first, and the benefit of the
sick poor second. They claim to use the body of
every patient who receives medical assistance
there—whether such assistance be paid for or not
—as a subject for the training of the students,
unless the health of the patient forbids it. The
argument is that the original bequest out of which
the hospital grew was for that purpose, therefore
all the moneys paid by Govt, and subscribed by
the public are for the same purpose; and the
patient who refuses to be used as a subject is told
to leave the hospital—unless he or she is too ill to
be removed. But the rule is—No student, no
treatment. Parliament ought to make it clear
that it is absolutely optional on the part of the
patient whether he or she be used as a subject for
the training of students.
• * * *
At one church in Carlton, Melb., the
parson and the elders, to show their great faith,
insisted upon leaving the place of worship un-
locked at all times. Once it was robbed, but still
the brethren persisted in keeping open house.
But finally .the church was cleaned out again, seats
and all, and now the doors of the House of Prayer
are fitted with formidable locks. Alas! only
the church which has absolutely nothing worth
stealing can afford to have complete faith !

One S.A. contingent came back from
Africa calling itself “the Fighting Fifth,” and
has been much paragraphed and pictured under
and over that title. Hobson deolares this war
has killed the sense of humor in England.
* * * *
Why does Sydney go to great expense to
put iron railings around her parks ? Absolutely
inartistic, without being ugly enough to be inter-
esting, they serve no useful purpose. Hyde Park,
for example, is never closed, and it would not be
likely to suffer from straying cattle ; so, why the
railings ? If some sacrifice must be made to the
god Terminus, the low wall that supports the
rails would surely answer all necessary purposes.

The population of Adelaide and its suburbs
has become so permeated by teetotalism that
brewers’ profits have fallen greatly, and the
adverse influence of the analysis of local beer pub-
lished by the Govt, analyst has still further
injured the Bung interest.
The usual series of jokes on the obtuse-
ness of customs officials and the new tariff has
started. Sydney ’Varsity received a collection of
Egyptian antiquities for the Museum, and the
customs charged on them, as “ fancy goods, n.e.i.
25 per cent, advalorem. It is said that the JJeps.
maintains that equally good articles are made in
Melbourne. But on the Uni. officials declaring
that the relics of the past were absolutely value-
less, the collection escaped taxation.

“Yarrum” writes :
If, in some Australian farming communities, the breed-
ing of children got a quarter as much attention as that of
stock, it would he well for the future of the State. I
know a little spot, once noted for its strapping men and
women, now peopled by a race of pigmies, runts, and
imbeciles. Talking to “ leading man ” Pother week, he
explained how he was related, by marriage and other-
wise, to every family of the hundred or so in the district.
Also, he was related to the blacks, and had no hesitation
about saying so. In Tas., N.S.W., Queensland, Vic. and
S. A. the same evil is observable—too close inter-marriage,
and consequent racial deterioration.

The fact that the Carrie Moore-Tyson
breach-of-promise case didn’t go on was the worst
disappointment Sydney has met with for years.
And, with a refinement of cruelty, the public was
allowed to come down to the court, and see the
curtain go about half-way up—and then find that
there was to be no show, after all. Plaintiff didn’t
even offer to pay the disappointed audience’s
tram-fare. The community is justly indignant at
the owner who scratches his horse on the morning
of the race, but when there is only one horse, and
it scratches itself after the race is started, the
position justifies the crowd in breaking something.
0 * * *
“Justice”—good old “ Justice ’’—writes
to Melb. Age re the command of the Victorian
men in buckram, which seemingly is going to pro-
vide a fat salary for somebody other than Tom
Price. “Justice” is peremptory in his request
for a solution of this horrible mystery. “I
now write,” says “Justice,” “to ask what
Colonel Price’s crime is that he has not been
given the command ?” Somehow, this demand for
information reminds the present scribe of an
elderly uncle of his, with a red face and a ban-
daged toe, who used to lie upon a couch, crying to
Heaven and people in general—“ What have I
done that I should have the gout?” Nobody
could think of a better reply than “Nothing.”
As a matter of fact, there were worse men than
uncle who lived and died without suffering any
gout whatever.

The latest caper of the unspeakable Vic-
torian J.P. is to beseech Govt, to label him with
a gold badge on his watch-chain. He would pre-
fer the country to foot the bill, but if that can’t
be worked he is willing to give a guinea apiece for
the pretties.

Broken Hill has given birth to a print
styled Freediscussion (one word), conducted in
the interests of Socialism. “Hy,” “hys,” and
“hym” are used for “he or she,” “his or hers,”
and “him or her”; and the editor recklessly
offers prizes of 25., Is. 6d., Is. and 6d. for 1000-
word essays on “Sport: its advantages and evils,
physically, economically, morally and spiritually.”
Also of 35., 2s. and Is. for “ not more than 32
lines of poetry, in either English, Irish, Scotch or
Cornish,” on the subject —ye gods !—of the
“ Mines Equity Cases, or Equitable Assessment
of Mining Property.”

“Rypo”: Re that subscription-list and
bazaar-tickets-grievance of railway men. A still
worse feature of the case is the “send-off” or
“presentation” subscription-list in the case of
transferred or retired officers of the various Govt.
Depts. A “boss” retires, or is transferred,
whereupon the “toady” section of the men get
up a testimonial, and the others are afraid to stand
out. Thus the boss, who doesn’t care a hang for
the men, gets a present from them, while the men
—most of whom are glad to see the back of the
“boss ’’—give him “ a token of regard.” Ugh !
A peculiar mental aberration ia that of a
Melbourne man, whose wife died over a year ago.
He goes every night to a large box in which he
has stored her belongings, dresses him*
self in his wife’s clothes and main*
tains an imaginary conversation
with her for an hour or more.
Occasionally he goes for a walk,
k but is promptly pounced on by
* the local policeman and led back.
In every other way he is quite
sane, and successfully conducts
a large business.
The “ necessary and just ” policy of farm-
burning in S’Africa is illustrated by a N.S.W.
cohentingenter’s recent letter. After describing
the hunt of a Boer convoy, wherein the hunters
found themselves the hunted, and had to retreat:
We halt for half-an-hour, and hum a stray farmhouse
as some slight relief to our feelings.
Which recalls the “ man ” who, having had a bad
day, goes home and kicks his wife. What is
likely to be the effect on Australia’s criminal
statistics when these easily annoyed gentry come
home to “ relieve their feelings ” at our expense ?
A REBOURS!
Gray grief and sin shall burden man,
And souls be bitter hard to save,
So long as we shall live our span
From out the cradle to the grave ;
But we may lose our galling weight,
And cheat Remorse and still Regret,
When man can grapple with his Fate
Backward—from bier to bassinette !
O.K.
W.H.O.
CHRISTMAS EVE IN THE FIELDS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Here ’neath the trees, while all the world seems sleeping,
My spirit to an ancient influence yields,
I muse upon some shepherds vigil keeping
’Mid the strange mystery of the star-lit fields.
And though lik.e them to kneel in adoration
Perchance our doubting hearts forbid us, still
We all can echo the sweet salutation ;
“ Peace ever be to men of kindly will! ”
ALICE J. MUSKETT.

The Bulletin’s Christmas number has
been a great success. Copies can still be obtained
from the office at 6d. each, accompanied by the
necessary postage.
Sanitary enthusiasts advocate shorter
skirts for women, as they—the skirts, not the
ladies—would not collect the dust and “detri
menta” like the long trains, which sweep the
dirty pavements. In regard to grace of outline,
neither long nor short frocks matter if the P.D;
corset be worn. To the queen in her regal robes,
and the comic-opera girl in her gauzy sauciness,
the P.D. corsets are equally essential.*
To be fragrant and daintily perfumed with
Parma Violets is the latest town-scent fad.
Fashionable women use Westall Guest’s Violet
Bath Salts in their baths, for shampooing, and
as a perfumed sachet for their clothing. This
preparation is quite the latest and choicest luxury.
