A WOMANS LETTER
_ Sydney, December 9, 1901.
My Dear Arini,—
As Xmas draws near shop-windows are filled
with things to eat and things for children to play
with. Windows which before showed all that was
fair and fine in muslin, lace, and chiffon, now hold
golden-maned dolls and white-maned rocking-
horses. Windows that held eatables in puri-
tanical labels now flaunt “nicies” in gold and
blue dress, with portraits of lovely women and tags
of tinsel and loops of ribbon, and red lions
rampant. Canned fruits from Columbia show the
eagle with his beak wide apart, a Xmas lustre on
his plumage, and a peace-on-earth-good-will-to-
men label in his claw. Then there’s the giant
strawberry, tomato, and pine-apple from our State
in gold frames, so to speak, or accessories that
show them to 25th of Dec. advantage. Also the
mothers of girls in service are falling sick with
strange unanimity, and are writing for their off-
spring to come home till after Xmas.
The streets and theatres are gladly suffering an
invasion of bronzed men in new grey clothes. Of
one of these, it is told that he is most suspicious
of townsfolk, and, before he goes into a crowd,
leaves everything in the way of money and
jewellery at his hotel office. Well, he went to
the first night of “ Florodora’s ” revival with abso-
lutely nothing in his pockets save a potato, carried
for rheumatism. Now he rejoices in his fore-
thought, for even his anti-rheumatic potato was
filched in the crush.
Although every woman who calls herself a lady
is getting ready to go to the mountains, there are
plenty of texts for talk between packing and the
making of sunbonnets—Amy Castles, the collapse
of the Moore-Tyson case, the Gov.-General on
trailing skirts, the epidemic of women’s clubs.
Also,Melba has written a nice letter to Mr. J.
r'-i WiUiamaon, ' n "which she regrets that she
failed to come to terms with him. “I cabled to
my brother-in-law,” she writes, “to get from you
your best and final offer, and am much disap-
pointed that the reply did not meet my views.”
The terms on which she comes to Mr. Musgrove
are most satisfactory— to Melba. She takes all
but a crumb of the profits. Melba won’t sing
during Cup-week, as she wants that space to
go Cupping “with the boys and girls,” and hold
receptions under the gums while the billy boils,
and someone reads Ogilvie’s “The Bush, My
Dover, to her. Melba’s tenor, Arens, is a nice
man with a fine voice, but already people who
will jump at conclusions are saying that the
tenor who is to support Amy Castles is
A dense, wet heat hung over the face of the
land (Kipling) the morning that the case, Moore
V-Tyson, was called before Justice Owen. “Is
there more than one defendant ? ” asked the suave
Judge (with that innocence that only Judges and
fair-haired, dimpled ingenues may assume success-
fully). A big crowd sweltered in the Court, and
was staggered to hear Mr. Want announce “a
friendly termination on a basis highly satisfactory
to both parties.” Mr. Justice Owen said “I am
ver y glad to hear it,” but that did not represent
popular opinion by a long way.
At one of the side doors Miss Carrie, very pale,
but looking eager and expectant, stood and
watched the crowd pour out into the street. She
was in white-silk muslin frilled at the back right
up to the waist, and big white hat. A good-
looking young solicitor, and a group of fair
maidens, regular jury-quellers, attended her. So
far, the terms of the settlement have been kept
secret, but, for one thing, Carrie retains the
diamonds and gets the trousseau which has lain in
limbo at Farmer’s. The defendant did not show
u P i 1 < n Court, but the same night he occupied a box
at “ Florodora,” and seemed to quite enjoy the
importance of being Ernest.
All the principals in “Florodora” are the
plumper for their absence from Sydney. Miss
Palotta and Miss. Moore are markedly so, and to
the latter’s great improvement. Her shoulders are
no longer show-bones, and she has discarded wigs
and wears her own hair ; it is parted on one side
and just hangs curly to the shoulders. One of the
six pretty maidens wears a costume, in the land-
mg-on-the-island act, that is most freakish and
fascinating. It is pink, with black points.
Those black-gloved arms and hands suggest
black magic. The black-barred elbow-cuff
(“elbow-cuff” is good) and big, black hat
and something about the corsage—well, if some of
my friends, the impressionable ones, wore that
toilet it would tincture their morality; they would
play ping-pong with the Ten Commandments and
come to a bad end.
Concerning “ Florodora ” merely as a picture. If
I saw it for six consecutive nights, as some persons
have done, I’d require to go for recovery (from a
color orgie) to the dead monotony of the Old Man
Plain The new lighting apparatus, lately
brought from America, has done wonders. Rain-
bows and sunsets have been materialised and laid
down for flooring; green and gold, and crimson
and rose flutter and wax and wane in glorious play.
I saw a miscellaneous collection of Simultaneous
Missioners (parsons and their women-folk) land
at the French wharf, after their trip round the
harbor in the Govt, yacht, t’other day. Four
cabs stood at the gate as the procession filed out:
they are probably standing there still, for there
was not a fare among the whole cargo. The mis-
sioners straggled away along the quarter-mile of
wharf as cheerful-looking as milkingcows, and made
for the tram. Never saw so many married parsons
in any sort of procession before; and never got such
a good look at so many parsons’ wives either
before or behind. Result: Disillusioned. Con-
olusions : Can nevermore admire the parsons’ taste
in~ women—nor the women’s taste in parsons.
Reflection : Not one “ squeezable ” woman (per-
sonal opinion only) in the whole half-hundred.
The men are most to blame; for consider their
opportunities!
Forty fair Hobart ladies are just now indulging
in a simultaneous mission, the object being to
establish a Tas. Ladies’ Club on latest London
lines. At the first and only meeting held to dis-
cuss the matter, Lady Havelock presided, and
three motions were carried in a hurry : 1. That
no male ever be admitted to the club. 2. That
the entrance fee be one guinea, yearly subscription
two guineas. 3. That membership be rendered
as exclusive as possible—an extremely low per-
centage of blackballs to disqualify. Then the
enthusiasm, which had been fervid, suddenly
slumped, and an unaccountable shadow of regret,
or something, seemed to hover over subsequent
proceedings. With a less awe-inspiring chair-
woman than Lady Havelock, it is probable that
either the man resolution, or the money one, or
the blackballing one would have been modified,
but none was brave enough to face the glittering
eyes of the new Governoress. The meeting
stands adjourned.
Three women’s clubs have been born in Sydney
within the last month. In London, Mrs. Hirst
Alexander has schemed a scheme for a female
Imperial club. Mr. Percy Spence, Miss Theo.
Cowan, and Mr. Bailey (who, by the way, claims
to have introduced the woman-journalist into
Australia) are assisting enthusiastically. Can this
be the (W. H. L.) Bailey who, after he left here,
introduced himself to Stead as the founder of the
Sunday paper in Australia, and got the reply—
“ Sir, you are an enemy of the human race ! ”
“ The Manxman ” is a dolorous play, but in it
Wilson Barrett is at his best. Portraying the
domestic virtues suits him, and at the end of the
last act real case-hardened old theatre-goers grow
tearful and choke somewhat. It is wise of him to
leave his enemies for Providence to pay out. We
should all do that (at the same time, if one sees a
chance of giving Providence a helping hand one
shouldn’t hesitate). Basil Gill, as Philip Chris-
tian, has a terrible deal of silent waiting, watch-
ing and staring, but he contrives to let you see
what long sharp teeth the fox has that gnaws at
his vitals. Ambrose Manning, as Caesar Creegan,
evolves a splendid Pharisaic mouth, wide, hard,
smug and crescent-shaped. The men who wrote
“Hell open to Christians” and the “Mustard-
Pot-of-Charifcy-that-Maketh-the-Soul-Water-with-
Devotion ” had mouths like that. Miss M'Carthy,
in blue, and the white pigeons with pink legs that
fly into the farm-scene are charming.
Society of Artists will open its Exhibition for
a while longer than it originally intended, in
order to show a big historical picture now being
unloaded from the Orizaba. It is the work of the
portrait-painter, H. J. Thaddeus, and represents
Pope Leo XIII. receiving the oath of allegiance
from the Cardinals. The figure of the pontiff
dominates the canvas, which holds, also, portraits
of Prince Colonna and Count Pecci. When the pic-
ture was exhibited in Rome and London persons
whose opinions count greatly admired it. Invita-
tions are now going forth for a private view. The
painter himself, very big, all in white drill and
an explorer s helmet, looking like a man-’o-war in
mufti, accompanies his picture. He painted the
picture of Gladstone that the Reform Club
secured, and various Royalties. He has the least
taste in life of a Cork brogue, though Florentine
by domicile ; is 38 and unmarried.
Mrs. J. C. Williamson’s “At Home” at the
Society’s Rooms was the most successful in the
Society’s annals. The “ Florodora ” and Barrett
co. s attended. - Amongst the crush were Mrs.
Justice Simpson, Mrs. “Jack” An till, Mrs.
Burdekin, Miss Darley, and Mrs. Want.
A plump young lady in the front stalls at “ East
Lynne,” the other night, performed a smart bit of
lightning-change in full view of the audience. In
deference to the satisfactorily-growing custom in
front-stall society, she had removed her big, new
hat, and, woman-like, put up her hand to feel if
i? r ™* r . was right. She felt something more
than hair and hair-pins— a housemaid’s cap, no
less, which, in her hurry to rush away and weep
with Lady Isabel, she had forgotten to take off
before pinning on her Sunday hat. You should
have seen that bit of lace disappear ! But you
would have needed quick eyes. It vanished much
more quickly than the large human smile the
audience wore.
Irresponsible Australian chroniclers of Ada
Crossley say that she shares contralto honors with
Clara Butt. Australian musicians visiting London
find that Clara’s share is—about 98 per cent. To
Ada are left the rest of the honors.
It is noticed that a certain Im Pay, an indefatig-
ablejsquire of dames, invariably wears a bouttoniere
coming into town of a morning, but he is never
seen going home with a bouttoniere.
You remember “Bushie,” the fine collie that
went away with the first Bushmen’s Contingent.
Like his fellow travellers who fell, or who are
waiting for their pay, the dog was soon forgotten.
A letter from England tells that Bushie was
handed over to an attendant of the late Queen’s.
Perhaps one of the Hindus. Possibly the little
Hindus, or the young John Browns, ride him ;
and mayhap he has the mange, and perchance as
he lies dreaming of sheep and rabbits and a sun-
burnt land, one will kick him, saying “ Dingo ! ”
Edwin Geach of the straw hat, drowsy voice and
sleepy blue eyes, was in town for a few davs
arranging for the Arnold co. to open at the
Palace. Whilst in London with war-lecturer
Macdonald, the pair went to lodge at a house in
Bedford-street in one of a mile of terrace houses
owned by the Duke of Bedford. In that street
Australians fairly swarm. Well, the landlord re-
ceived them warmly: “ You are not the first dis-
tinguished Australians I have had,” quoth he to
the lecturer. “De Rougemont and Mr. Mark
Foy stopped with me when they were in London.
Do you know them ? ”
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ What struck me most forcibly at Flemington
during the recent races was the shocking increase
of donkeyfied British affectations amongst the
swarm of pretty Australian girls. A certain
fatuousness of expression familiar oil the face of
the Bwitish peer, who habitually says ‘ Haw,’
and dazzles the democracy by hanging two large,
glittering front teeth out for public inspection, is
making itself visible amongst the otherwise pre-
possessing features of the Australian girl whose pa
has got money to burn. You might have detected
the expression all over the Lawn on any of the
big race-day*. It is secured by dropping the chin
slightly, drawing down the lines of the mouth, and
half-veiling the eyes, and the effect produced
implies that the wearer detects a bad
smell, and has a rather unpleasant taste
in her mouth as a result of coming in
contact witli so many nasty, common people
whose.. existence is not ‘ weckognised anywah ’
worth mentioning The accent that goes with
this expression is simply ghastly. For instance :
‘ Deah me-ah I had noo ideah they weserved seats
foah ennybody heyah.’ This kind of thing is not
produced without an effort; the smallest remark
is accompanied by painful contortions, and I
should say the performer would be quite prostrated
after a five minutes’ conversation.
“The Australian johnnie boy, too, becomes more
and more like the comic artist’s ideal ‘Bwitish’
dude, with blue blood in his veins, or else there was
an enormous number of imported bounders in
Melbourne for the horse-parades. The herein-
above alluded to tombstone incisors were plentiful
enough to suggest that fashions in dentistry areas
arbitrary amongst the ‘ naicest ’ Percies as
fashions in lies. Hitherto I had regarded those
particular teeth as characteristic only of the Aus-
tralian rabbit and the British ‘ heir.’
“ The latest dodge in a Melbourne tea-room is
to take your drop of hard-stuff in temperance dis-
guise. The innocent tea apparatus comes along,
and you pour your spirit out of the teapot in the
most innocent fashion in the world. The fact
that you take neither sugar nor milk passes with-
out comment, and a perfumed handkerchief does
the work of overcoming the pungent odour of the
intoxicant. Neat, isn’t it ? Only in one Collins-st.
cafe does womankind take her nip of brandy in the
straight and full view of the world.
“ At the recent races, many men wore Panamas
and kindred hot-weather hats, bent up in front, or
picturesquely slouched. Broad-brims, white or
Tuscan, are the tiles for our boys. f Young Aus-
tralia, with his dark hair, fairly regular features,
and tallish figure, should be a handsome fellow,
take him altogether; though he doesn’t get full
credit for his looks because he’s sallow and slight.
Ugly brown or black boxers and hard, rigid sailor-
hats are very trying to the most prevalent thin
brown face, but top that same face by a picturesque
Panama or creamy sombrero to show up its dark
coloring, and you’ll see that the Australian
brownie, less vivid and tropical than Southern
Frenchman or Italian, has nevertheless a certain
neatness of feature and daintiness of texture all in
his favor.
“At the Henry Bracy matinee, Mrs. G. B. W.
Lewis and her bright pupil, Miss Unie Russell
(who, by the way, is said to have had offers from
both Wilson Barrett and Tyrone Power), gave the
Juliet and Nurse scene. The Russell novice—a
slim blonde, with rippling, flaxen floss hair, blue
eyes and dainty stage-gait—has been trained from
youth up in elaborate dancing as well as dramatic
elocution. At present, Miss Russell, pretty,
dainty and winsome, with her Dresden china
coloring, impresses one as an ideal stage figure
for light comedy. To me her Juliet seemed
disappointingly shallow and trivial, especially
when lamenting the supposed death of Romeo,
but the young thing looked like a spray of blue-
bells in a cornfield, her crinkly maize hair falling
over her pale-blue robe and dainty blue satin feet
peeping from under her skirt. Robust, slap-dash
Mrs. Lewis did ‘Nurse’ capitally.
“Among the big crowd at the Bracy matinee
were Austral Salonites, beaming on the debut of
their member and protege, Miss Russell. Susie
Vaughan, in black, leaned over the back of
the d.c., looking exactly like her middle-aged
stage self as she chatted to slim, mild Leslie
Victor. Bessie Thompson (Mrs. Dartrey),
who did mature matrons for the Broughs last
time they came, was perhaps too polished,
caustic and cultured in her dowager methods.
Miss Vaughan, on the other hand, is ever ready
to assume virago attitudes and termagant tones.
Having it out with hubby in ‘The Magistrate,’
her gestures were those of a suburbau shrew
rather than of His Worship’s lady with baronets
and colonels on her visiting list. Mrs. Bracy did
a little comedietta, mostly monologue, crisply,
albeit in the hollow, superficial style which
sooner or later mars the acting of comic opera
people ; and Mr. Bracy made a nice little speech
for his wife who prompted him in wifely fashion—
a natural, unaffected little interlude. The
‘ Musical Burglar ’ was on hand, also ‘ Josephine
Gassman ’ —such a big, dark woman, and so little
skirt and corsage ! In the daytime female
shoulders and legs are unromantic. Her pican-
ninies are darlings, especially ‘lrene,’ with comic
whites to her eyes. She and ‘ Bill ’ fought and
squalled like cats ’cause they both wanted the
presentation box of sweets.
“Melbourne has come to the conclusion that
the freezing Countess looks her best riding.
In a white linen Beaufort coat, a smart tie, a
block habit skirt, and a white Panama hat, she
sits her hack proudly to perfection. Her genial
Lord rides by her side in all-grey, with a pincbed-
up face that is never without its amiable smile.
Even people sitting on the tram-dummy and
being rattled along pretty smartly, are recog-
nised by Lord Hopetoun, and get a quick glance
and smile of recognition. Her Ex., on the other
hand, never seems to see anybody.
“At the Mills-Price wedding, which is quite
ancient history by now, the dowager of ‘ Clive-
den ’ had the place of honor beside the bride’s
warrior papa. The colonel got through his part
of the business promptly and cheerfully. A bevy
of five bridal nymphs wore white, and bore high
black sticks garlanded like may-poles. An air of
festivity prevailed, and the leading lady having
duly signed the register, which converted her
from Lorna Price into Mrs. Mills, waved her
bouquet in the air every time she caught a guest’s
eye.
“ The other day a fashionable bride-elect issued
invitations for her coming sacrifice to Hymen, aud
put the usual penny stamp on the envelope. But
the swagger cards were over-weight, and all the
guests were, therefore, charged excess. The bride
and her parents heard of this with deep regret,
and actually went to the trouble of penning
apologies and enclosing stamps to all the invited.
What a task ! And what a strange form of penny
wisdom!
“Signorina Marchi, the tail, shapely beauty of
the Italian Opera Co., is believed to have become
engaged to an officer of the Puglia, whom she met
here first, and then came across again in Brisbane.
There were some congratulations up North over
something or other —more than likely this. By
the time the news filters through Italian, half-
and-half Italian, and plain English, the freshness
is about gone. It leaks out through the same slow
channel that Signorina Bassicli is a great student,
and the most serious person of the company off
the boards. She certainly looks as though she
were.
“ Madame Slapoffski is often seen in the circle
at the Opera these nights, wearing a magnificent
cloak, and generally an Empire kind of dress
hanging slack from the corsage. Herr Slap.’s
forelock in repose pretty well conceals the con-
ductor’s identity. We knew him best, when in
the ‘Princess’ sphere, by that madly-swinging
forelock, that danced from side to side, and bobbed
and waved as frantically as a gum-leaf in a high
wind.
“There is much fun to be got out of watching
the two Conservatorium cliques at the Opera.
Peterson and co. barrack right bravely for the
Italian school, but the Marshall-Hall clan swear
by the German and English makers of music, and
only attend regularly to profit by the Italians’
faults.
“Prof. Peterson seems to be running Melb.
University Conservatorium of Music on ‘Kirk’-ish
lines. The recent programme of the annual
students’ concert was headed ‘Sixth Session,’ and
the incidental circular, without making direct
reference to the unsaintly policy of Peterson’s
predecessor, Marshall Hall, stated that the
Session’s record ‘ encourages the hope that the
Conservatorium is about to enter on a new period
of usefulness and success.’ Some of Marshall
Hall’s old pupils who appeared at the ‘ sessional ’
concert must have, read that circular with grave
interest.
“ Polo, the game of sweating ponies, collisions
and swears, begins its course on Wednesday (11th)
at Caulfield, for the summer season. Why the
hottest of all games should be played in the
steamiest period of the year, is, and ever will be,
a mystery to hard-thinkers. But since the Gov.-
General is going to take an interest in it, and
also going to play, and afternoon-tea is to be
laid on, the fashionable will rally despite heat-
waves and dust-storms.
“Now that one can look back on a gay and
festive season and calculate things, it is home
upon the mind that the only two Federal Ministers
in no way represented at any of the social shivoos
are Alfred Deakin, the nimble-minded, and
Charles Kingston, the Sphinx. Mrs. Deakin still
eschews the limelight of Social Life, and Mrs.
Kingston hugs a retired existence. Once or twice
only has Alfred, who behaves in Parliament like
the terrier of the Ministerial benches, barking and
biting at any trespasser or intruder who threatens
the peace of the heavy-weights alongside him,
shown up at a function. Then he was completely
out of his element, and disappeared at his first
chance.
“ Talking of Federal feasts, no one tnroughout
the States has entertained so lavishly as the
Malcolm McEacharns. Unlike the Sargoods,
who scatter their shivoo invites broadcast in
sassiety and the world of political pests, the
Mac’s have lorded it at smaller but extremely
swagger banquets, where their hospitality has
been of a particular and highly discriminative
quality. The Bartons are placed above “the pale
spectrum of the salt ” every time; in fact, the
devotion of the shipping king and political infant
to the Prime Minister has been for the last few
months singularly touching.
_ “ Melbourne artists are beginning to push their
pictures under the notice of the public, which
steadfastly declines to go as far as Eastern Hill
and pay a shilling admission to the Vic. Artists’
Society’s shows. The foyer at the Princess’s
Theatre has been converted into something of an
art gallery since Brough’s season started. A
number of purchasable little works by .T. A.
Mather and others hang around the walls, and a
numbered ticket on the corner of each suggests
that a list of prices is available in the vicinity—
probably at the refreshment-bar. Not a bad idea
to put paintings in the way of the capitalist whose
artistic sense has been stimulated by a love-scene
on the stage and by seeing a man between the
acts.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The Bulletin regrets to hear that Tom
Durkin is lying seriously ill, of jaundice, in Mel-
bourne Hospital.
N.S.W. Assembly will have to make Mr.
J. C. L. Fitzpatrick Chairman of Committees, or
be talked to death.
Having left the State Legislature, Mr.
G. H. Reid, K.C., now gets a share of the N.S.W.
Government briefs.
There are in the air a number of small
signs, showing that Cardinal Moran may not
return to Sydney after all.
The Dutch royal row in a nutshell : The
Queen’s consort was hired as an animal. Now he
is accused of being a beast.
Editor Doidge, of Cootamundra (N.S.W.)
Liberal, is mentioned as a probable chairman of
the Western Lands Commission.
Minister Kingston, mildly disapproving of
an Argus report:—
Your report reeks with recklessness and is foul with
malice.
Possible successors to the N.S.W. Chief
.Justiceship: B. R. Wise, Jack Want, Justice
3tephen, Justice Owen, C. E. Pilcher, and R. E.
O’Connor. '
“ They run in all shapes. ” Some of the
published photos, of a “ distinguished visitor ”
severely recall those of Frank. Butler, of Blue
kopje fame.
There is at last open talk of the Pope’s
ill-health —a serious symptom. Eminent per-
sonages are always “ quite well ” to the public
until they are on the point of death.
According to Henniker Heaton, Marconi
is so wrapped up in his work that he won’t marry
his over-the-water (U.S.) fiancee “until he can
convince her by wireless telegraph.”
That hard-up confraternity, the lawyers
of Sydney, are just now praying that there may
be shortly another clergyman named as co-
respondent, “ for the good of business.”
General Hutton coming back again. O,
for the fun of it! The General (to obese N.S.W.
officer): “ Good God, man! you can’t ride, and
you are too blanky blanky fat to walk ! ”
There have been four deaths in N.S.W.
Leg. Counoil in the session of 1901 —Garran,
Dalton, Hyam and Thornton —and five members
have resigned mostly to take seats in the Federal
Parliament.
Federal. Customs Minister Kingston has
entered his 52nd year. Twelve years ago it was
prophesied by Cockburn that at 50 he would be
tame and docile. Cockburn’s prediction is mani-
festly a vain thing.
The late George Thornton, of N.S.W. Leg.
Council, lived whilst four monarchs reigned in
England, and saw N.S.W. in turn convict settle-
ment, Crown colony, self-governing colony, and a
State of the Commonwealth. Four monarchs and
four Constitutions!
If it is proposed to name the Federal
capital after a member of the first Federal
Ministry why not submit the names to Federal
ballot ? Probable result: Kingston first, the rest
nowhere ; loyalists satisfied, and Australia accept-
ing the inevitable with loyal resignation.
ON THE WAY TO TOPHET.
CHORUS OF WOBBYGONGS: “ Hullo! Another fisherman!”
Hughes (N.S.W.), M.H.R., has passed his
second year Bar examination, and will soon have
the right to wear a wig. Holman, another of the
brilliant “youngmen” of N.S.W. Radicalism, is
also rapidly qualifying for the Bar.
Of the new Westralian Cabinet, selected by
A. E. Morgans, the only native of Australasia is
Col. Secretary Matthew Lewis Moss, barrister,
bom in Dunedin, M.L. And Moss has been badly
defeated on standing for re-election.
There does not seem to be a happy poli-
tical life ahead of Mr. Arthur Lynch, the new
M.P. for Galway, and ex-Melboumeite. He is
threatened on one side by the British Government
with punishment as a traitor for fighting with the
Boers, whilst the extreme Fenian section in Ire-
land refers to his “ pseudo-military harlequinade,”
and denies that he ever commanded an Irish
company in the Transvaal.
Who would believe, nowadays, that
S’James Fairfax, of S.M. Hekald, was once upon
a time an enthusiastic volunteer, an aggressive
amateur military blazer, a warrior pal of George
Dibbs and W. C. Windeyer ? But he was.
G. L. Jessop, the lively English batsman,
was accosted, the other day, in a Sydney street by
a man who wanted somebody denounced ana
“ dealt with.” He was mistaken for John
Norton, to whom he bears some resemblance.
The bones of millionaire James Tyson
were removed to the “ family vault ” at Campbell-
town, N.S.W., last week. Probably the poor old
man is now turning in his new grave at the
thought of how his money is being played up.
The painstaking and capable J. W. Israel,
Tas. Auditor-General, a nervous little lamp-
lighter sort of man in flying coat-tails and a
worried look, is now Federal Auditor-General. As
his name implies, Mr. Israel thoroughly nose his
business.
Henry Gilmour, alleged to be the third
man engaged in the Bridge-st. (Sydney) burglaries
and police assaults, for which Montgomery and
Williams were hanged in ’94, has been sentenced,
at Paris, to life-servitude, for burglary and
attempted murder.
“ Jawbone ” Neild says he offered N.S.W.
Premier Lyne to take the presidency of the Old
Age Pension Board without emolument, but
that economical Minister preferred to pay
a big salary to someone else. Doubtless Lyne
recalled the fate of the Premier who had made the
last contract for gratuitous services with the gal-
lant Colonel. :
William O’Brien, Irish M.P., now on a
visit to Australia for his health’s sake, is at once
one of the most brilliant and most disinterested of
a Party, whose prominent men are, as a rule, too
fond of playing a lone hand. As journalist and as
orator, Mr. O’Brien has won a reputation, and he
did no damage to his name when he refused to
join in the howl against Parnell in that leader s
dark days.
Obit. : The Hon. Henry Isidor Joachim
Raphael Rooke, M.L.C. (Tas.). Though born in
Spain 60 years ago Mr. Rooke was no Spaniard,
either in speech or demeanor, for dignity knew
him not. He was considered the funny man of
Tas. Parliament, and delighted in shocking the
Tory respectability of the Grubbs and Crosbys
and Doderys of the Council.
Members of the family of the late Mr.
Cox, M.L.C., will indeed be much obliged to Mr.
Clarendon Stuart for his devilish well-meant letter
to the Sydney press, explaining how very hard it
was, in the little village of Wimborne—after which
Mr. Cox’s N.S.W. estate is named—to discover
some obscure old folks who dimly remembered
that some Cox or other had gone to Australia some
time or other.
About Mr. Harry Sholl, the W.A. postal
official’s son, who died, a Perth paper solemnly
publishes this:—
As a discreditable and wholly unnecessary allusion has
been made to the possibility of the late Mr. Harry Sholl
being still alive, an allusion which, under the circum-
stances, must cause infinite and unwarranted pain to the
members of his family, we have much satisfaction in pub-
lishing the following, &c., &c.
It is rarely that a paper commits itself to stating
its great satisfaction that a man is dead and not
alive.
Judge Williams’ sarcastic remark to the
Geelong jury that “much valuable time would
have been saved had
they given their verdict
before hearing the evi-
dence,” recalls Judge
Molesworth —first of the
name. He was presiding
at the Supreme Court,
Port Fairy (Vic.), where
juries, at that time,
were notorious for re-
fusing to convict. After
one particularly scan-
dalous “ not guilty ” de-
cision, the Judge, speak-
ing in his gravest and
most severe manner,
said : “ Gentlemen, after
that verdict, I should
not be at all surprised
if the sea should to-
night sweep this town
out of existence.”
Whereupon a barrister,
of the old school, gravely
interrogated him : “But
does your honor really
believe that there is any
danger of such an occur-
rence ; as I had in-
tended to sleep here to-
night?” The Judge
made no reply ; the bar-
rister kept to hisoriginal
intention —and the
Judge also slept in the
town.
Cabled that some gilded Englishmen have
offered to “put up” a yacht for the King if he
will challenge for the America Cup. If Edward
can’t run the race on his own revenues, he had
better keep out of it on pauper lines. The idea
of letting-out the royal stomach as an advt.-
lioarding for such pushing bounders as the
Liptons!
Yet another Scotchman (Dr. David Arthur
Welsh, ye ken) has been appointed to a lucrative
post at Sydney University. Dr. Welsh, appa-
rently a rather distinguished man, is the new
Prof, of Pathology. In the University medical
school, or at the Alfred hospital, no man stands
the faintest chance who has not been raised on
parritch. At the present moment The Bulletin
has in its mind at least one case of gross
favoritism. In the instance alluded to nothing
could be said against the personal character of
either man, but between their qualifications there
was indeed a great gulf fixed.

"SIMPLE SIMON" FRASER.
The pure-merino Sydney Freetrade crowd
in the Reps, mostly batch together under the pre
aidenov of Drydog Reid at a big house at bt.
Kilda. Editor Willoughby, of the Argus, lives
next door, and he and George Reid (when the
latter is in town) may be seen almost any morning
fixing up the policy of the Commonwealth over
the back fence.
A. E. Morgans, Westralia’s new Premier,
forms one of the few recorded instances of a M.P.
becoming head of a Ministry without hawing pre-
viously served in a Cabinet. Morgans, lived half
his life in Central America, and is a mining mag-
nate. He has been in the Assembly for 4£ years,
and enjoys the distinction of having beaten
“ Smiler ” Hales for Coolgardie seat.
Lord Hopetoun on Lord Rosebery :
The most brilliant statesman and the most polished
speaker Scotland has produced in this generation..
There is one thing that a Scotchman is not
niggardly with—praise for another Scotchman.
By-the-way, Hopetoun’s speech on this occasion
was quite in the Rosebery vein. Has he also
learned the value of “ skilled assistance ” ?
It is now stated that the rumor of B. R.
Wise’s appointment as Aoting.N.S.W. Chief Jus-
tice was circulated ‘by request.” An Actmg-
C.J.-ship looks at sight a risky transaction for
Wise, who has a leading place at the Bar, and. a
large assured income. A year’s “Acting” in
such a high and responsible position might mean
disaster on the Bench, and difficulty in ' getting
in ” again at the Bar. It is as hard for a barrister
as for a jockey to get back into inside running
once he loses his place. The fields are always
jostling and changing.
The Argus has something further to say
about a certain well-advertised person who went
up like a rocket and came down like a load
of bricks:
The official view, I believe, is that B.P. has been quite
sufficiently praised and rewarded for the defence of
Mafeking, which was altogether a much less remarkable
episode than it pleased the public and the newspapers to
imagine. It is also hinted that a good deal of the credit,
which the press showered on Baden Powell, for the de-
fence of this frontier post, really belongs to others.
In making S’African reputations the pen is
mightier than the sword.
James Sialk, alleged to be 106 years old,
and a Waterloo veteran, died recently in Bendigo
(Vic.) benevolent asylum. Even if his figures are
correct,' James was a very young soldier of 20 at that
memorable battle. . But only the obscure person
whose age has to be taken at his own valuation
lives to be 106 ; public men, whose date of birth
is known, seldom reach 90, and "when they reach
95 the fact is regarded as a marvel. The peerage
is a pretty tough crowd, yet out of 815 British
peers enumerated by Whitaker at the end of
1900, only 11 dated back before Waterloo. The
granddad of the crowd only reckoned 93 years ;
two were 90, and one 89.
Pretty little Queen Wilhelmina, of Hol-
land, it seems from the cables, has made a big
matrimonial mistake.- - Her recent illness was due
to her husband’s insults, and two of her courtiers.,
have fought duels with him in her championship
(both of them being worsted). A queen-regnant,
as a rule, has a small chance of a happy wedded
life : she must marry someone of “ royal blood,”
and as no man of real dignity will live in a
position of dependence on his wife, it is generally
some poor - spirited scallywag scrap of small
royalty who grabs the billet. In the case of
this particular marriage, for instance, there was a
“saving clause” that in the absence of a male
heir after five years the arrangement should be
“off” without the formality of further divorce
proceedings. English experience, despite the
traditiou maintained with such splendid hypo-
crisy, did not provide any exception to the un-
happiness of this type of marriage.
Coadjutor-Archbishop Kelly, facially
and characteristically, suggests a chastened
W. B. Dailey. The forehead, nose ana
chin are similarly moulded, and there 18
that mesmeric merriment in the eyes wmeh
drew and held .the company of W.B.D.
The Archbishop has the reputation of
being an orator. His first remarks were
not rhetorically articulated —they were,
rather, precise, modest, explanatory and
careful; nevertheless, they gave more
than a hint of a reserve of force and
fluency. He will draw the multitude to
hear him, and his voice has the compass
for a multitude. The Cardinal is read in
the daily press; his coadjutor, it is be-
lieved, will be heard from pulpit and
platform.
Mr. Panton, Melb. P.M., often ex-'
poses a dignified flank to the gibes of the
heathen ; but as a rule the heathen is too
awed to aim straight. Once, though, a
severely-ad monish ed habitual drunkard
got a straight left home. Mr. P. had
prosed for many minutes ere he asked if
the incorrigible had anything to say before
being sentenced. No, he had nothing to
say ; but he’d like to ast a kweschin, if
the dignitary didn’t mind. The P.M.
didn’t mind, and said so ; whereon the
drunk grinned again and waded in :
“Warr I wanted ter arst yer, Mister
Panting, is this : Ain’t Panting a nick-
name or an erlias, or a psoodonim, or
son-ethink like that ? Your real name
is Gawd ; now, straight dinkum, ain’t it ?”
When “ Mister Panting ” recovered him-
self he gave the blasphemer seventeen
thousand years’ solitary, together with all
serial rights and a half-share in the book-
profits.
It is to be hoped that the death of
poor old Sydney native George Thornton,
aged 82, will kill the inane joke about his
being present when Captain Cook
arrived. Hardly a man has figured in N.S.W.
public life but fired that ancient gag at a
grey-haired opponent. It was Thornton’s thorn
in the flesh. If for years back he attempted a
modern thought, he was summarily dismissed
with a reference to Captain Cook. Parkes
plagiarised the idea with regard to all his op-
ponents. Reid played it off on several occasions,
but the death of Thornton should close up this
joke.
Tas. Premier Lewis, front-named Neil
Elliot, has, it appears, now definitely decided
that Neil, the name he is best known by, shall
stand. For years there has been a persistent
family-circle effort to have him styled the Hon.
“Elliot,”but the “Hon. Neil” persists in stick-
ing. “ Sir” Elliot would certainly have sounded
Grander, but, as only a C.M.Gr.-ship arrived at
>uke-time, the “ Sir ” (and presumably the
Elliot) has been indefinitely postponed.
Trenwith, the one-time strong leadei of
Vic. Labor party, seems doomed. He erred in
joining the present Ministry. By nature he is a
lazy man. It was only his passion for debate, and
for the display of his peculiarly powerful logical
faculty, that carried him on to what he was before
his elevation to office. He is wholly devoid of
ambition, and when he found himself in a position
where he could “ take it easy,” physically and
mentally, he “let her rip.” As a result he has
been the worst sort of failure, as a Minister. In
order to preserve what is called the solidarity of
the Cabinet, Trenwith has had to subscribe to
planks whioh have alienated all his_ old labor
friends. So much is this the case that it is doubt-
ful whether he will be returned for Richmond in
future.
Extract from a N.S.W. country paper,
read in connection with a recent libel action. The
gentleman in whose journal these words of mild
reproach were published wanted damages . for
things another editor had been saying about him :
The badly-ballasted dandy who is the happy supposed
possessor of a literary filth distributor, otherwise termed
a newspaper. Since this journalistic gewgaw was
snatched out of obscurity by some kindly-disposed per-
son, and was emptied without any qualification into the
alleged possession of a newspaper plant, he, like other of
his clownish kin, endeavored to improve nature’s work
by making a bigger ass of himself than nature made of
him in the first instance, but he discovered he could not
do impossibilities, like any other skurvy skunk or social
worm. The esteemed innocent of Woolgoolga, friend of
the courtesan, and compeer of the demi-monde. He
must have reverted to the days before Providence
emptied him out of a butcher’s sausage basket
into the possession of a newspaper, and thought
of the days of his goat-raising industry. For a
nincompoop like him, who is merely a blind pimple
on the face of society, to pose as an influencer of public-
feeling is ‘-brass” tq the fullest degree. . . Licentiousness
and obfuscation are net indispensable concomitants
of a newspaper man’s career. Still, persons of this class
first pose as Hercules defending virtue, and, secondly,
they are staggering under a load of liquor, and profaning
all that is good, entirely dead to the knowledge that in
the most degenerate there is such a thing as moderate
decency and a sense of shame. ... In a measure we
could excuse putrid slobberings of a certain journalistic
hobble-de-hoy on the grounds of the completeness and
density of his ignorance, and the fact of his being a per-
fect nonentity. . . . We do not feel that we are acting the
part of sensible citizens by descending to the level of
squelching literary lizards of this class.
The eloquent plaintiff was awarded damages—one
farthing.

The monotony of the fashionable dawdle
on Melbourne Block, the other afternoon, was
relieved by the appearance of a composed old
party sitting on the kerb placidly shaving his
corns. In answer to the policeman’s “Hi, move
on—you mustn’t do that here,” he glared in
amazement, and said : “ Well I’m ! Here’s
off back to Wandin!” and lie sailed awajr with
hi. boots under his arm.
• * • *
The luck of children ! Some kids wt>re
playing round a well at Nelson, Vic., when
one, an 11-year-old boy, got astride the windlass.
Another child turned the handle, and the lad fell
down the well, 90ft. deep. The child ron were
frightened and ran off, and not till two hours later
(lid the boy’s mother know what had happened.
1 here was a rush to the well; and, a faint answer
being received from below, a young man descended
upon a piece of fencing-wire and brought the boy
up—much bruised, but with not a bone broken.
In a few days he was all right.

Mr. C. A. Benbow, of Sydney, who lately
wrote to the Rhodesian authorities re the possi-
bility of stocking N.B.W. with the S’African
eland, has been informed, in reply, that local
hunters would readily undertake, for a sufficient
consideration, to supply as many young elands as
lmght be required. One man, it is stated, “ has
offered to capture and deliver at Salisbury, tame
to handling, young eland and quagga (Burchell’s
zebra) at £4O and £25 per head respectively, and
would doubtless undertake to capture a number
at a less figure.”
W.S.T.” ; Re the proposed acclimatisation of
the eland in Australia. I believe that if, as su°--
gested, these animals were to be turned out on
the Great Western Plains they would prove as big
a nuisance as the marsupials would be if they got
there. In the far west the eland could not exist
—for want of feed ; but in the Central Division
the animal would be a serious trouble. With its
stilt like legs it could simply walk over the wire-
fences round the wheat and crop areas, and as it
has a stomach of “silo’’-capacity, it’s easy to
imagine ■ what havoc would be wrought in a wheat-
held. Besides, who wants the beast—unless it be
the cranks that introduced the rabbit, the
sparrow, and the Scotch thistle ?

The manager of Jirnkee Hydraulic Sluicing
Co. s show at Tongio W. (Vic.) was killed, the
other day, through being struck by a stream of
water from the sluicing nozzle ; he was lifted up,
hurled oOft., and dashed against the face of a
cutting. You have to get in the way of a sluicing-
head, with the pressure all on, to realise what
hard tack water may be. Present writer once
saw a man struck in a somewhat similar wav to
the above, but, fortunately, he happened to be
well away from the nozzle. Nevertheless, the
victim had the few clothes he was wearing at the
time simply swept off him. He was picked up
trouserless and with his flannel stripped over his
head, and spent weeks in bed as a result of that
shower-bath.

“CrippsO.": Re trees lifting things as
t ley grow, “H.P.” (B. 9/11/’01) is right. I have
never seeil an instance of lifting, whereas I have
otten noticed trees which have grown around
foreign substances—such as fence-wires, iron
spikes, &c. Enclosed photo, shows how a “she-
oak wasn’t lifted by another tree in the fork of
which it had fallen, but is apparently growing
through the solid trunk of the latter, the fork in
which the former originally rested being now some
feet higher up.
“Spunps”: Re weight-lifting perform-
ances. In ’75 “Jewboy” (lOst. 71b.), at
Hasiem s store, Molong, carried seven
bags of potatoes (about lOowt.) 12
paces. They were the old bolster-
shaped sack, and the performer
stood erect and rigid while we
packed them—one on each shoul-
der, two across, two parallel, and
one on top. (Haslem and Sandy
iiogers, of Bathurst, still alive to
verify.) Again, the other day, at
store (Bathurst) a teamster
( Burly” Newman) tossed a 36-
gal. cask of beer (over 3cwt.) on to
ins dray. It is mainly knack and
use. I hat is why I backed our
grocer, little Mac. (Bst. 101 b.), to
lift sugar against “Burly” thus:
A 701 b. bag of sugar to be taken
in one hand, shoulder-high, arm
bent, then arm to be extended
perpendicularly to its full length
and drawn back again. The one
who did this oftenest to win. The
struggle was supei b, but the team-
ster succumbed at the tenth lift,
which the other barely managed.
Mac nevertheless then bet the
publican that he would lift the bag
15 times; and, though beaten be-
fore on the 10th lift, he actually
did manage to struggle through
the whole 15 times. Then, concen
trating every remaining ounce of
energy, he hurled the bag with
terrific force at the publican. It
struck him on his “thinking box ”
and he dropped instantaneously—
to the dodge. The bag was stuffed
with kapok and weighed about 20
ounces!

A man, recently out Betoota (Q.) way
buying horses for S’Africa, says that he was
shown a water-hole where it is estimated 30,000
cattle have perished during the present drought.
Nothing is to be seen on most runs below Jundah
but skeletons. One station, which had 24,000
head, now has 300 ; and another has but 2000 ’left
out of 40,000. Five adjoining stations lost a total
of 100,000. Out there the wind piles up dust
against yards and buildings to such an extent that
they become useless. A yard on Mooney Plains,"
which cost £5OO, is entirely buried. At other
places the dust accumulates up to the windows of
houses, and has to be shifted with scoops.

been of enormous size, the teeth being about 2in.
square.

L e “Eller’s” statement (B.
0/11/ 01) that at Burragorang he found two men
who trapped between 400 and 600 parrots a week,
and sold them at an average of 6d. each. The
tacts are that 500 parrots were not sent from both
places together in a year. Hayes Bros., of Burra-
gorang, and Wonsons, of Wilton, send, between
them, at most about 40 parrots a-week during the
trapping-season. And they are experts who feed
the wild parrots during the breeding-season, and
m 6 'Tr,/! aware that there’s no market for anything
like 500 parrots a week.

Snake-story from Hamilton (Vic.) way.
A country station-master was leisurely disrobing,
the wife of his bosom being already between the
sheets, when suddenly a wild yell from under the
bed scared the lady into a faint. The yell was
followed by the unexpected appearance of a total
stranger who rolled from under the bed and
began a frantic fight with himself on the floor in
the middle of the room. The third party proved
to be a gentleman who had hidden under the bed
with the intention of getting awav with the family
plate in the silent watches of the'night. A four-
foot black snake coiled round his leg was the sulii
cient reason of his untimely appearance.

A S.M. Herald writer attributes to a
i^" - Patriot ” the introduction of the European
rabbit. Bunny was introduced by Thomas Austin,
of Barwon Park, Geelong, Vic. Austin emigrated
1832 ’ l 7 ® 114 to Port Phillip in 1836,
• n ,o„, Uoed the rabbit in the early sixties, and died
in 1871.
* * * *
The devouring rabbit goes slowly West,
and is now fairly established in Westralia. Al-
ready advance scouts of the bunny legions have
been captured in the South and N.E. goldfields
districts. It is safe to prophesy that bunny will
spread the length and breadth of Westralia. The
prospector and the miner are pleased at the ap-
proach of opposition to the meat-ring, but the
settlers, who have seen things in the past, are
very uneasy. The Groper does not seem to care
much ; he has been beaten by the t’otherside man,
and supposes that the other evils of the East will
come as a matter of Destiny.

Douglas Park, near Wilton, 45 miles south of
bydney, writer, last month, shot at 25 rabbits in
one hour. I his at a place where the cheap-excur-
sion shooter had almost exterminated everything
from a wallaby to a bull-frog, and where, up till
18 months ago, a bunny was a novelty

THE SWAGMAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Place side by side with johnny-cakes or damper
Tlnee bags containing sugar, flour and tea,
A billy and some “ neck,” and swaggie’s hamper
" ill, with a pannikin, completed be.
Then spread you out a blanket and some sheeting,
And roll within a little diliy-bag,
A change of togs, a coat for Sunday meeting—.
And there you have his ordinary swag.
Build you a fire beside some eucalyptus,
l.y No Han’s Creek, and ’neath the starry dome,
here dingoes wander whence their lonely crypt is-
And there you have his customary home.
Take j'ou a man sunbrowned, with thews of iron,
A cheerful heart, stout as an ironclad,
And dress him like a wheel without a tire on—
And there you have the nomad on the pad.
Then give him but his health’s assets for hawking,
And his condition labels him “ for hire ; ”
Turn him adrift on horny feet f*w walking,
And to complete him, call hin. hong Maguire.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.

There is one place in Sydney where the
business of money-lending is carried on in an
equitable manner and where those in need of
financial accommodation can rely on being
decently treated. The sensible man who wishes
to get rid of his monetary worries at a minimum
of cost and trouble to himself goes to the offices of
the Australian Equitable Investment Co.. Ltd.
The co. has a large capital for investment at low
interest, and is always open to advance money on
risks which the banks would decline; and acts
generally as a medium between the banks and
ordinary money-lenders. Men in business can
obtain assistance for their venture at a figure
which will leave them a fair profit for themselves.
The co. advances money to any extent on deeds of
land, bond warrants, merchandise, stock in trade,
scrip, interests under wills, furniture and all classes
of security. Press notices which have been pub-
lished from time to time express the favorable
opinion which generally exists regarding the co.
Mr. ,T. E. O’Brien is the Managing Director, and
the co.’s offices are at 45Elizabeth-street, between
King and Hunter-streets.*

4 4 Hemi ” : Re inspection of the dairying
industry. Recently toured Taranaki district, the
Maorilandhomeof thebusiness. Ninety per cent, of
the yards are veritable quagmires, the sheds filthy
and extremely primitive. About same proportion
of the workers are women and children, whose
stolid, expressionless faces bear sad tribute to the
soul-wearing nature of their occupation. At the
recent dairy conference at Palmerston N., one of
the members read a paper on the subject, which
described in plain terms the unhappy lot of these
“white slaves,” but little or no notice was taken
of the effort by the spiritless press. Fat Man also
has his grip on the factories. With
of a fairly-paid manager, the hands receive starva-
tion wages, and work about 17 hours per day.
One case : Married man, with family, in receipt
of 255. per week, asked for a rise, and was told
that the directors had refused his request, but
that he would be allowed free butter for his
household.
THE LATE ALFRED FAIRFAX.
Photo, by Creelman, Sydney.
Mr. Alfred Fairfax, who died the other
day at North Shore, Sydney, aged 77 years, was a
cousin of the present head of the S.M. Herald
Fairfaxes, and arrived in N.S.W., from Warwick-
shire, Eng., at the age of If! years. He began life
as a Herald emnloyee, but afterwards went into
commerce for himself, and made a fortune. His
hobbies were cricket and yachting, and the latter
pastime he supported most lavishly until he lost
his all in his bold and historic gold venture at the
Star of Peace mine, Hawkins-hill. But, despite
irreparable disaster after a long and prosperous
career, he bravely held up his head, and to the
day of his physical break-down was ever the same
charming-mannered, cheery, generous soul, and
straight, ardent sportsman. His obsequies were
largely attended.
Sydney Telegraph, in its leading article
of 4th Dec., poured out a fearsome blast of rage
on ex-Premier Lyne for “slandering N.S. Wales.”
Lyne had merel y told the simple and quite obvious
truth—that N.S. W. needs all the revenue that the
Barton Govt.’s tariff is likely to give it, and that
under the old order of things it couldn’t pay its
way. As N.S. W. has had 14 deficits in 21 years,
this doesn’t require much argument. The great
difficulty in the way of reform in N.S.W. is that it
is impossible to put anything right till it is first
admitted to be wrong; and it is impossible to say
that anything is wrong without the Telegraph
having a dark and indignant suspicion that the
speaker is a hired Guy Fawkes from Victoria
trying to dynamite the Calico Jimmy industry.
The Sydney tail'messes’ strike has been
definitely ended, and the strikers may claim a
victory on almost every point : they have enforced
their “log” of. wage rates, and have» secured
official recognition of their union. In the contest
with Mr. Samuel Hordern, on other minor points,
the strikers tried to push the attack too far home,
and it was a certainty from the beginning that
they would be repulsed. When the Labor
Council refused to help them to maintain the
untenable position that.the forewoman, the em-
ployers’ representati v-e in each factory, should be
forced to join the employees’ union, the tailoresses
were obliged to withdraw. But, despite their
want of success on that point, they have every
reason to congratulate themselves on the general
results of the struggle. Throughout, the leading
spirit among the strikers was Mr. H. E. Holland,
editor of the Socialist paper, the People.
He claims, by the way, to have originated
the phrase, “Reid the Wriggler,” and is a
s eaker with a fine power of invective. The
other strike leader, Mrs. Robinson, secretary of
the Tailoresses’ Union, has shown during the
struggle good organising power and quiet reso-
luteness.
The appointment as Federal Commandant
of Major-General Hutton —the man who damned
the man who damned Chicago—has been con-
firmed.
“ A.P.” w rites :
Much trouble is being experienced by Vic. Railway
Dept, this wool-carrying season, owing to unskilful load-
ing and lashing. As many as 15 bales have been known
to fall at one time from a truck. The dept.’s usual good
luck has so far prevented any of the straying bales from
getting under the train wheels. It only needs a change
of the luck once for an awful smash to occur ; and a good
deal of wool is carried by “ mixed ” trains. The want of
skill shown in the loading of wool is said to be the result
of the dept.’s policy of employing a large amount of
“casual” labor at small country stations. Those em-
ployed are not experts, nor do they feel the responsibility
a permanent hand would. There is no danger of future
punishment in the shape of fines and “ records ” for them.
Ex-linen draper Gurr, who believes that
Sunday-school teaching has qualified him for the
position of Education Minister, made a hideous
exhibition of his incapacity in.dealing with the
State School Teachers Bill, in Vic. Leg. Assembly.
Though for weeks he had been closeted with his
officers, having the facts pumped into him, he was
utterly unable to handle the matter before the
House, and so broke down over the very first
question asked him that the Premier had to come
to his assistance. From that out Peacock took
charge of the Bill, and the well-groomed Gurr sat
and smirked. Gurr is the fussiest, silliest, and
best-dressed Minister of the Crown ever seen in
Victoria. Once upon a time, in Australia-, the
fool of the Ministry was always made Post-
master-General ; now-a-days he is invariably to be
found at the Education office.
M‘Millan, cursing wildly about
the horrors of Protection :
There were hat-mills in Victoria, the proprietors of
which did not know what to do with their money.
Duties on such goods as these constituted a public
robbery.
The Victorian hat co., be it noted, pays 10 per
cent. divs. to local shareholders, who spend their
money in the country, and has a tiny reserve of
£1487. And M‘Millan’s bosses, "the bloated
absentee importing firm in London, also pay 10
per cent. —sometimes more —and spend the money
in England, and their big reserves leave the hat
co. quite out of sight. As an example of the
blastiferous insolence of the rich absentee’s agent
and fugleman, who takes Australia’s wages to curse
Australian industries, and apparently regards the
existence of an Australian industry as a personal
injury, the above is a very beautiful sample
indeed.
How the cheap-labor crowd bite ! Laun-
ceston (Tas.) Examiner contemptuously describes
the present movement for a White Australia as—
“thrusting a few thousand Kanakas back into
barbarism so that an Australian worker shall have
an opportunity of taking the ‘ nigger’s ’ place and
doing a ‘nigger’s’ work in a cane-brake.” How
dearly these Britain-bred and Bull-bossed, but
Australian-supported papers seem to love Aus-
tralian workers 1
THE LATE G. H. COX, M.L.C.
Photo, by J. Hubert Newman, Sydney.
William M'Millan, in the Federal Hau.se
of Reps. (Sydney Telegraph report, Dec. 3) :
Victorians were making a mistake in agitating for high
duties, as they would result in N.S. W. becoming a large
manufacturing State, and securing the trade from their
Victorian rivals.
Of course, it wouldn’t pay M’Millan’s class for
N.S.W. to become a large manufacturing State,
for then the importer would be superfluous. So
M’Millan fights against the high duties. There-
fore, by his own showing, he and his N.S.W. fol-
lowers (including a number of Labor men) are
fighting to keep down the industries of their own
State. N.S.W. should note the fact.

stamped. Name and address must appear on JUS.

tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any State) accompany.

TAILORESSES’ STRIKE.
HARDSHIPS OF A WORKER.
The recent strike of tailoresses in Sydney has
brought to light many instances of long hours,
arduous work, and poor pay, and the request of
the workers for higher wages has, upon this occa-
sion, met with widespread public approval. That
troubles, however, which may afflict a tailoress
are not confined to strikes can be gathered from
the following particulars, given by one of the
trade, Miss Jessie Kersop, of Charles-st., West
Benalla, Vic., to a reporter :
“ I have had a good deal of experience in the
business,” she explained, “for I have worked
at it for over four years, but twelve months
ago troubles came upon me in the shape of
fainting fits and weaknesses, the result of anaemia,
and 1 was forced to temporarily abandon my
trade. During my illness several doctors were
called in, but notwithstanding the tonics I took I
gradually declined until 1 was so weak that I had
to take to bed. My cheeks were pale and thin,
my breathing was greatly interfered with ; it was
a trouble to eat any tiling, and attacks of retching
helped to prostrate me.”
“No doubt your absence from business also
tended to distress you ?” said the reporter.
“ Yes, it did, for I wanted to get back to work,
But just at this time a friend strongly advised me
to give Dr. Williams’ pink pills a trial. I did so,
and found they suited me admirably ; every dose
seemed to make blood, and one box strengthened
me greatly. I continued taking them for some
time longer, until quite cured, and now I am able
to work at my trade as actively as ever. My
appearance has greatly improved, and I feel ever
so much brighter and stronger.”
To women workers, growing girls and tired
housewives, Dr. Williams’ pink pills for pale
people bring perfect health, bright spirits and
rosy cheeks; they enrich the blood, tone up the
nerves, strengthen the spine and cure debdity,
weak heart, consumption and decline, liver and
kidney troubles, dyspepsia, rheumatism, neu-
ralgia, skin diseases, blood poisoning, effects of
fevers, etc. ; sold by chemists and storekeepers,
and by the I)r. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s-
place, Sydney, two and nine per box, six boxes
fifteen and three, post free. Beware of cheap
substitutes, colored pink. Prematurely exhausted
men will find that the genuine pills bring a return
of abundant strength and vigor.

In The Golden Fleece, a new wool monthly
published by John Bridge and Co., Sydney, the quality
of the incidental literary matter is much higher than la
usual in such a connection—a fact due, doubtless, to the
editorial supervision of the exuberant “Fred’ Broomfield.
The little sheet is typographically compact and tasteful,
exhibits a good show of ads., and bids fair to keep its head
above water.*
Those who go forth during the holidays to
recuperate and have a good time, shooting in the
country, fishing at the watering-places, or sailing
upon the face of the waters must remember
D.C.L. to keep it handy. Not only will this pure
whisky assist them to recuperate and add to their
enjoyment, but in any sort of emergency, from
snake-bite to hysterical female, it is invaluable.
The man who camps out and is not provided with
a flask of D.C.L. runs a grave risk.*
Christmas morning is usually ushered in with a
blast of penny whistles and the tinniest of tm trumpeto,
and paterfamilias, lying in bed, hears and thln^. c °“:
placently of the difference in price between the whistles
which he has bought and the tricycle which he huited at
a month before. He works it out and feels good ail o\er.
By the time he is dressed, however, the eternal screeching
and blasting has begun to pall; afterbreakfast ltwornes
him so that he can’t bring his mind to contemplate the
benefits of economy ; by dinner-time he is fretful, irritable
and snappy. Dinner soothes him and brings a fitful
peace; but afterwards the infernal, unslackened dm
works him up to a condition bordering on homicidal
mania. Along towards tea-time he is alternately
slamming doors and cramming wads of wool into his ear*.
His punishment troes on through Boxing Day, and next
morning a shattered remnant of a man, who starts-
violently when asked what he wants, orders a boy &
tricvcle at 9s fid. and a girl’s at 275. 6d. from M Lean
Bros, and Rigg, Ltd., 107 Elizabeth-street, Melbourne.-

POLITICAL
POINTS
Kingston is right. N.S. Wales isn’t “ the
Mother State.” In her Federal dealings she has
shown herself to be, not the States’ mother, but
their mother-in-law.
Q. Home Secretary Foxton, thegrizzler of
the crowd, is to be opposed at next election by
Labor-man Turley, ex-wharf-laborer, one of the
brainy men of the Opposition.
The Bulletin being now on Ketchener’s
Index Expurqatorius does not circulate openly in
S’Africa. All the same, it is in more demand,
and “gets there” to a greater extent than any
other Australasian paper.
Says Melb. Argus :
The sooner New Guinea becomes part of the Common-
wealth for purposes of private enterprise, the better.
A very good argument for looking this colored
gift-horse in the mouth. “ O private enterprise !
—or public theft,” as Randy Bedford hath it.
One Harding, another of the Freetrade
hacks, comes forward in N.S.W. press to advocate
a Blaok Australia. The glamor of G. H. Reid’s
humbug is being gradually rubbed off Freetrade
by this constant confession of typical Cobdenites
that they are for cheap labor, black or white, all
the time.
Latest dodge of the Queensland pro-
Kanakas is to form the Tommy Tannas into a
“ union,” which will send a deputation to
the Federal Parliament to beg that things
may be left as they are. In this attempt
to organise the United Niggers the plan-
ters are playing a rather dangerous game.
The theatrical deputation will have no
genuine political effect, and the “ Kanakas
Association ” may devote its organisation to
other uses—say, a strike, accompanied with
violence, for higher wages.
The M.L. Govt, proposes making a
charitable grant of £llO,OOO to the deben-
ture-holders and £20,000 to the share-
holders of the Midland Railway, and i
receives only curses in return.
When the State agreed to give
land worth £1,250,000 to the Mid-
land Railway Co. the co. entered
into certain agreements, and
these provided for the forfeiture
of the whole show if the work
was not completed in a certain
time. It was forfeited, and the
co., by the decision of the Privy
Council, has no claim to any
compensation whatever. It has
also used actionable language for
five years, and, taking one thing
with another, it would be much
wiser lor M.L. Govt, to stick to
its bond and not part with a
copper.
Mrs. Lance Rawson writes to
Brisbane Courier stating that, at the
little suburb of North Rockhampton alone,
she knows of six Kanaka families, includ-
ing 30 children and four grandchildren
—this despite the fact that the census
only mentions one Kanaka woman in
the district, and says nothing of any
children. She adds :
The names given are but a few of tha
many to be found at North Rockhampton,
on the Yaamba-road and Park Avenue,
where there is quite a little colony of
Kanakas, their wives and children.
The lady gives these facts as an argument in favor
of the Polynesian individual, but they seem rather
to show the necessity of getting rid of the un-
desirable nigger before his multiplication makes
Queensland so much spotted that it is liable to be
mistaken for a coach-dog.
Q. Govt. Two-babies-two-votes Bill is, as
seen from the start, a slaughtered innocent. The
Philp gang will now go to the country saying that
the Labor party refused to pass a genuine demo-
cratic measure, and a lot of fatuous voters will
believe it.
The week in South Africa : Sunday, De
Wet is “located”; Monday, he is “closely
watched” ; Tuesday, he is “ cornered” ; Wednes-
day, he is “ surrounded ” ; Thursday, he is “in a
tight place”; Friday, he is “hard pressed”;
Saturday, he “escapes.”
The Commonwealth Minister for Home
Affairs should be pulled up sharply in re his
proposed junketing in search of a site for the
Federal capital. £3OOO is the estimated cost of
the trip. Members who go on such a razzle would
empty the milk-can 3 of an orphan asylum.
Sydney D.T. describes as a “ triumph for
the Freetraders ” the utterly disreputable action
of the Opposition in coalescing with the Labor-
party to destroy the revenue tariff on cotton
goods. Since this revenue impost was essentially
a Freetrade one, its rejection is, if anything, a
triumph for Protection.
Premier Philp’s demand that the Kanaka
Bill now through both Federal Houses, be re-
served for the King’s assent, is a vain hope. For
he will, in England, be confronted with the same
argument which has dogged him in all his tirades
against the measure: Why did the Queensland
representatives in both Houses advocate the Bill.
At the tail-end of the session, when there
is already ten times more work on hand than can
be got through, Queensland Govt, introduces its
Agricultural Bank Bill and Workmen’s Compen-
sation Bill. The Philp Govt, often introduces
reasonably democratic measures, but always when
it is too late for them to have a ghost of a chance
of becoming law.
The Tory Argus says the only thing to
save Victoria is a Coalition State Government.
It is so long since the Argus pets had office that
they have forgotten what a Treasury bench feels
like. They don’t ask for the whole loaf now—only
a crust —iust to show that there are still a few
mouldering Freetraders and stodgy immovables
in State politics.
About the last act of the Maoriland
session was the passing of a Bill authorising the
purchase by the State of the privately-owned
Manawatu railway. The price fixed is £951,845,
equal to £11,331 per mile. So the State, after
paying at the outset a very large share of the cost
of the line in land-grants and other assistance,
proposes to buy it back at £3390 pervmile more
than the average cost of the lines built by the
State ! The private-railway business generally
ends in the State being left like that.
Pastoralists’ Review still breathes
slaughter on the Kanaka question:
If there was not civil war, it would not be for want of
the will, but because the forces on the side of the Com-
monwealth are overwhelming. The Queensland planters
resent the way in which they are being treated so
strongly that it would he a relief to their feelings to
fight.
On the whole, it is a very good thing—for the
blatantly Jingo Review— that its friends did not
manage to carry, in the Senate, a postal prohibi-
tion of seditious papers.
THE SPECIAL
CREATION.
“ They would end by making
him feel that either he had been
specially created to occupy the position of Governor-
General of Australia, or that the position of Governor-
General of Australia had been specially created to be
occupied by him.”
Australia: “ Great Scott! has it come to this?”
Though Queensland’s Ministers can pass
and get consent to syndicate or other private-
benefit bills to take immediate effect, yet with four
months before them they say it is impossible to
pass and make effective a new franchise. And the
Federal one-vote franchise came into effect with
instantaneous ease, without dislocation of local
rolls or departments. No will, no way.
Chamberlain: “I would quit public life
instantly if I believed my so doing would enable
the country to honorably and speedily terminate
the war.I'’ 1 '’
A patriot he yet may be.
The land that lost him would be blessed.
And plainly Joe begins to see
How he may serve his country best.
A great joke in the House of Reps, was to
see Sudden Solomon, forgetting all about his
alleged Freetrade principles, rush to vote with
the Protectionists on the salt duty. S.A., you
see, is more largely interested in salt-production
than any other State; hence the Sudden One’s
eagerness to forget he is a Freetrader when salt is
on top. As with Reid re N.S.W. sugar, so with
Solomon re S.A. salt.
"ART IN ITS INFANCY"
“Mr. G. H. Reid, in opening? a Victorian horticultural show, said he was sorry
the president had referred to the fact that he was probably we. I-known to them
through the illustrated newspapers, but he was glad of the opportunity of coming
out and showing them how different he was from those caricatures. (Laughter.)
He did not, however, put them down to any malice. Art was still in its
infancy. ” —AGE.
“ Keep the limelight steady , Billy, arid give him a show.
As might have been expected, it was re-
served for one of the 48 Fat Landlords to offer the
buffeted Vie. old-age pensioners the most brutal
insult yet flung at them. Old Nat. Levi, who so
often vainly wooed the Assembly electors, moved
the addition to the Pensions Bill of a clause
depriving pensioners of their voting rights, thus
stigmatising them as paupers. The insufferable
impudence of wealth !
Premier See, upbraided for enforcing the
Calvinistio law forbidding the sale of fruit and
gingerbeer on Sundays, impudently replies that
the law is there, and must be respected. But the
law equally prohibits the sale of Sunday news-
papers, and yet, in that regard, it is never en-
forced. For the reason that John Norton and
the poor, speechless, Sabbath banana are very
different propositions.
Our poor little sister, Tasmania, proposes
to allow Lloyd’s the concession of establishing a
wireless-telegraph monopoly between herself and
the mainland. The V.D.L. Co. and railway
syndicates have the land ; cable-extension co’s
have the water; and now it is proposed to
yet another syndicate a lien on the air ! Tas-
mania should at once be arrested by John Forrest
and placed under medical observation.
It will always be possible to get men of
some sort as co-hen tin gen ters. Out of four or five
millions of people there will always be plenty of
fools—men without any sense of justice, honor,
or humanity, who would prefer to ride to hell
rather than walk to Heaven, and who would elect
any day to loot and kill rather than to work.
You will always, also, have on hand the title-
hunting political snob to engineer them.
The worst that the bitterest enemy of Q.
Labor Opposition could wish it is that the
general election will place it in power. Con-
tinuous Ministerialism has disorganised the
finances, dodged taxation, depleted the Treasury,
and accumulated railway and other promises
aggregating millions. If Labor gets into office it
will have to face heavy taxation and severe
retrenchment, which will ruin its prospects for a
decade to come.
Senator Dobson, of Tasmania, almost wept
in his admiration of the people of India, and pro-
tested to the last against their exclusion from
Australia. Yet, if anyone proposed to give these
Hindu races self-governing powers, Dobson would
probably call him a traitor ; and if anyone sug-
gested that they should be granted the first right
of free human beings—the right to decide their
own political destiny, instead of being conquered
and owned by Britain—Dobson would certainly
call him a wrecker of the Empire. If Dobson’s
colored friends are not tit to govern themselves,
then Australia has every right to regard them as
an inferior race and to shut them out.
N.S.W. Industrial Arbitration Bill, to the
surprise of many people, has passed into law.
Trouble for a long while threatened over clause
36, giving an Industrial Court power to order that
preference ip employment .should be given to
trades-unionists! The. Upper House threw this
clause out with great apparent determination.
Then Premier See.consulted the Labor party and
suggested to them that the clause was not worth
fighting about, and the majority of the caucus
seemed to agree with him. But the Trades and
Labor Council demanded that the caucus should
make a stand. Without clause 36, it was urged,
trades-unionists would prefer not to have the Bill
passed at all. So the Laborites, with some re-
luctance, told John See that clause 36 must be
won ; and John See, with some reluctance, told
the Upper House that it must give way ; and very
much to John See’s surprise the Upper House did
give way.
The See Govern-
ment in N.S.W., like
the Reid Government,
is introducing an Audit
Bill, providing for a
new method of keep-
ing the public accounts
—not for keeping them
honest, but just for
keeping them. But
what N.S.W., like all
the other states, needs
is a Bill to prohibit
the spending of loan-
money on unproduc-
tive works, to establish
a sinking-fund for the
repayment of the pub-
lic debt, to prohibit
the use of trust-funds
by the Treasurer, to
abolish foreign borrow-
ing, to put all the
miscellaneous sho r t-
ages into one big de-
ficit, and then set aside
the proceeds of certain
special taxes for its
extinction, and to pro-
vide that the country
shall raise enough re-
venue to pay its way.
It doesn’t need new
devices for ruling-off
the old fraudulent
accounts more beauti-
fully than ever with
red iuk. It wants
honest accounts, even
though they may be
dog - eared and have
blots on them.
The cheap nigger-labor party made a big
fight in the Senate for its beloved Kanaka.
Walker (N.S.W.), Ferguson (Q.) and Symon
(S.A.) tried hard to get the Kanaka made a per-
manent institution north of the tropic of Capricorn,
and were beaten by 16 to 10. Charleston (S.A.)
and Sargood (Vic.) then demanded that the exist-
ing Kanaka should be allowed to stay here and
multiply'indefinitely ; proposal lost by 27 to 2.
Finally Ferguson (Q.), Jawbone Neild and Walker
(N.S.W.), and a few sundries, endeavored to get
the Kanaka trade extended for seven years, and
went down heavily with a wooden sound, ilk®
Neild’s poems falling from a high roof into the
street.
Irvine’s uniquely-impotent no-confidence
motion on the Peacock Ministry having failed, it.
is likely that Outtrim, who held the portfolio of
Railways in the M'Lean Govt., will attempt to
oust the Govt, before the session ends. If the job
is taken in hand by Outtrim, one of the solidesfc
intriguers in Vic. Parliament, the Peacock crowd
is liable to go under. Should the Peacock Minis-
try manage to creep into recess a reconstruction
will have to take place. Trenwith, Morrissey,
Gurr, M'CuJloch, Attorney-General Gillott,
and, possibly, Burton will have to quit.
Trenwith has proved altogether too indolent;
Morrissey is too weak to carry out what he
knows ought to be done; Gurr is silly to
the last degree ; M Culloch is indifferent, and
wants to resign; Gillott seems incapable of
handling the work of legal adviser to the House ;
and Burton is a much-misunderstood man. dur-
ing the recent crisis M'Culloch hustled round and
got each of the Ministers to sign a document
expressing his willingness to resign if Peacock
thought he could reconstruct. So far, 1 eacock
has steadily declined to throw his colleagues over-
board, but, whether he likes it or not, the feeling
of the House is in that direction.

The Gentlewoman, September 9, 1899: —
Ammonia.— l am afraid you have been taken in. There
are several imitations. Unless you see the name of
Scrubb and Co. on the bottle it is not genuine. A table-
spoonful or two in the bath, according to the quantity of
water, will cleanse your skin and make the bath more
refreshing.*
In a display so teeming with novelties and
specimens of the finest Art Jewellery as is the Xmas
Show at the Goldsmiths’ and Silversmiths’ Alliance, it is
perhaps invidious to single out for special mention any
particular line, but a leading feature is certainly that
section allotted to December’s Lucky Stone, the Tur-
quoise—significant of prosperity in love. Every con-
ceivable form of novelty for the adornment of the female
form divine is to be found here ; nor have the require-
ments of the sterner sex been overlooked. The firm’s
expert buyers have ransacked the markets of the Old and
New Worlds for seasonable novelties. With their
usual foresight, too, this up-to-date firm suc-
ceeded in completing their purchases prior
to the imposition of the much-talked-of
Tariff, so that this “ bogey ” holds no terrors for those
visiting the white and gold shop at 90 King-street.
Limits of space forbid anything like detailed mention of
the vast selection of novelties in brooches, bangles, muff-
chains, necklets, pendants, chains, rings, scarf-pins,
studs, clocks, watches, solid silver and electro-plate,
toilet and table ware, Parisian goods, leather goods of all
kinds, and a specially fine assortment of the latest
fashion in photo, and miniature pendants. In short,
those in search of Xmas and New Year gifts—novel,
pretty, and last, though not least, at reasonable prices—
cannot do better than call at, or write to, The Gold-
smiths and Silversmiths Alliance, 90 King-st., Sydney.*
A SLUGGISH LIVER IS A LIVING DEATH.
VITADATIO
"WHI Cure Y ou.
FIVE YEARS’ SUFFERING CURED
BY VITADATIO.
FEELS SPLENDID.
Shepparton, July 31, 1899.
Mr. S. A. PALMER (Vitadatio).
Dear Sir, —I have very great pleasure in certifying to
the MERITS of your VITADATIO. I have suffered for
the past five years, off and on, from JAUNDICE, EN-
LARGEMENT OF THE LIVER, and an INTERNAL
GROWTH of some kind, which the doctors did not seem
to understand, and all the medicine I took before
VITADATIO had no more effect upon me than so much
water. My husband and son induced me to try your
VITADATIO, and after the second week I had been
taking it a quantity of blood and lumps of stuff which
looked like pieces of liver came away. I now feel
splendid and am able to get about and do my work.
I am an old resident of Shepparton, and all my
neighbours can tell you how bad I was.
I EARNESTLY RECOMMEND your VITADATIO to
anyone suffering as I did.
Yours gratefully,
0. OURREY.
Witness to signature : W. J. Ford, J.P.
