testimonial.
Wangaratta. D. H. K. H'Guinnbss.

Correspondence Invited. Write for Testimonials
Apropos Cardinal Moran’s remark that, so
far, no R.C. has been appointed to N.S.W. Pub-
lic Service Commission. Australians should
strenuously resist any proposal, no matter what
its source, to have a direct or implied religious
test for Civil Service positions. The cry from
Calithumpians, or any other sect, that they have
only got so many men in the service, and should
have more, implies a demand for certain conces-
sions to men, not because they are able, but
because they are Calithumpian.
The imported Chinese egg found one
friend in the Federal Parliament, after all. News-
paper report of debate :
On the item, “ Eggs per dozen 6d.”
Mr. Conroy (N.S.W.) objected to the duty.
The item was agreed to without further discussion.
And thus was lost the most beautiful opportunity
that the Cobdenites have ever had of showing
their consistency and of taking a grievous burden
off the pore working-man. There is but one real
Freetrader now. His name is Alfred Conroy.

A Japanese matrimonial advt. :
“lam a beautiful woman. My abundant undulating
hair envelopes me as a cloud. Supple as a willow is my
waist. Soft and brilliant is my visage as the satin of the
flowers. lam endowed with wealth sufficient to saunter
through life hand in hand with my beloved. Were I to
meet a gracious lord, kindly, intelligent, well educated,
and of good taste, I would unite myself with him for life,
and later share with him the pleasure of being laid to
rest eternal in a tomb of pink marble.”
A clerical contributor to a Sydney religious
paper:—
It has always seemed a degradation and an insult to
women that their dresses should be described in the
newspapers at all. Should not the woman be so im-
measurably superior tt> her dress, that the latter is un-
noticed in comparison with the former? Would it not be
an insult to an artist if a critic should elaborately describe
the frame of his picture, passing over, as if color-blind,
the beauties of shape and tint on which the artist has ex-
pended his best efforts?
Would, then, the reverend writer have the papers
confine their attention to the shape and tints of
the lady herself, as “ Mrs. Blank, in an aureole of
gold brown hair and pink cheeks, looked charm-
ing. Her bust, &la Medici, was the finest in the
room. Her shapely feet were particularly notice-
able, and ” so on.

while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page

THE COLONIAL MUTUAL FIRE
INSURANCE COMPANY,
ULIIMIITJEID.
TWENTY-THIRD ANNUAL REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS
Presented to the Shareholders at a meeting of the Company held at No. 60
Market-street, Melbourne, on Wednesday, 20th November, 1901, at
12 o’clock noon.
r THE Directors have much pleasure in presenting to the Shareholders the Revenue Account and Balance-sheet for
the year which ended on 30th September last.
The business of the Company for the period embraced in the accounts now submitted has been of a most satisfac-
tory character, and has established a record in its history.
The Balance at the credit of the Revenue Account is .. £30,035 10 3
which has been applied as follows :
To Reserve Fund (making £50,000) .. £12,500 0 0
~ Capital Account (making £50,000) 12,500 0 0
„ Dividend of 8 per cent, per annum 4,000 0 0
„ be carried forward 1,035 10 3
£30,035 10 3
The Directors have deemed it advisable to take advantage of the favorable result of the v-ar’s operations to
increase the Capital of the Company by £12,500 in order that the security afforded to Policyholders may be further
strengthened, and the necessary steps have been taken in terms of the Articles of Association to effect that object.
The Capital paid up will, therefore, now stand at £50,000.
By the addition of a like amount of £12,500 which has been placed to the credit of the Reserve Fund, the total
amount of that fund will also stand at £50,000.
It will be observed on reference to the Balance-sheet that during the year the liquid assets of the Company have
been increased by further additions to the amounts invested in Government and Municipal Debentures and on Fixed
Deposit.
The Directors take this opportunity of congratulating the Shareholders on the sound position of the Company.
The retiring Directors—the Hon. J. F. Levien, M.L.A., and Mr. W. Essington King—were re-elected. The
retiring auditors, Messrs. Thos. Brentnall and W. M. Hyndman, were also re-elected.
By order of the Board, WM. L. JACK, Manager.
Melbourne, 9th November, 1901.

AUDITORS’ CERTIFICATE AND REPORT.
We hereby certify that we have audited the accounts of THE COLONIAL MUTUAL FIRE INSURANCE COM-
PANY, LIMITED, for the year ending- 30th September, 1901, and that the above Balance-sheet is, in our opinion,
correct. The balances at the Branches have been taken on the last Certificates of the respective Resident Secretaries.
We also report that during such audit we have not observed or become acquainted with any breach of the Companies
Acts committed by the Company, or any Director, Manager, Auditor, or Shareholder thereof, and that, so
far as we are in a position to form an opinion, the Balance-sheet and Accounts have been drawn up in accordance
with the provisions of the Companies Acts, and present a correct view of the state of the Company’s affairs, and that
all our requisitions, as auditors, in respect to the above, as well as the private Balance-sheet, have been duly com-
plied with.
THOS. BRENTNALL, F.I.A.V. \ AudUors
Dated this 9th day of November, 1901. W. M. HYNDMAN, F.S.A.A., Eng./ Auaitors -

“ The Bulletin Story Book,” a solid, well-
printed cloth-bound volume of over 300 pages;
containing many of the best stories and literary
sketches which have appeared in The Bulletin
during 20 years, will be ready on Ist. December.
Price 4s. 6d. ; post-free from Bulletin office.
The Dunlop Tyre Co. intends making a big effort
to get “ Tattersall ” to run a consultation on the Warr-
nambool to Melbourne road race next September. This
race is of great interest to all wheelmen throughout
Australasia, and last year attracted an entry of 140 riders,
67 of whom started, despite wet weather. No doubt one
of George Adams’ big sweeps on this classic road-race
would tend to greatly stimulate road riding, which, after
all’s said, is the backbone of cycling. It is to be hoped
that “ Tattersall ” will give this big Warrnambool to
Melbourne road event a trial, for, in writer’s opinion, a
consultation on this genuine long distance road race
would be a great success, as the tickets would have a
ready sale amongst the hundred thousand odd cyclists in
Australasia.*
Mr. C. J. Pittman, ooachbuilder and wood-
bender, of Morehead-st., Redfern, and Young-st., Water-
loo, has his large factories fitted with every latest appli-
ance, and going as busy as beehives. In the repairing
department, every kind of vehicle can be seen in every
stage of repair. In the manufactory, a first-class staff is
hard at it with the most up-to-date machinery. The
smithy is particularly well-fitted. The wood-bending de-
partment is very interesting ; expert workmen being
engaged in bending timber to all shapes and designs.
Pittman is a name synonymous with first-class work in
the building of vehicles.*
The grape crop in South Australia promises to
be very large this season, and many of the wine-makers
of that state are making large additions to their crushing
plant, and cellar accommodation. Thomas Hardy and
Sons, Ltd., the largest growers (and holders of some
million gallons of wine) are increasing the capacity
of their cellars by about 100,000 gallons. This splendid
firm have lately opened a branch office at Grosvenor-st.,
Sydney, where they are showing samples of their choice
ports, their sweet white wines, and their robust clarets
so long famous throughout Australasia.
A farthing a mile ! That is what you can go to
London for by Dalgety’s White Star line. The Suevic,
the Afric, the Medic, the Runic, and the Persic take you
and all your luggage, and feed you on good tucker all the
way, for a farthing a mile 1 The ships are so big that an
ordinary wave is frightened to hit ’em. Their promenade
decks are immense. In fact, the Suevic’s deck is more
like a forty-acre paddock than anything else ; and you
cannot realise what 12,000 tons mean until you stand on
the Suevic’s bridge when she is at Circular Quay, and
look thence over half Sydney.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The first test-match is the one which the
Maclarenites should surely win if they are to make any
show in the other four. It finds them at their fittest for
batting purposes, and their freshest in the bowling de-
partment, whilst the Australians are comparatively in the
dark as to the merits of several “ possibles.” Two months
hence the selection of a best Australian XI. would be a
much easier matter than it is this week, though, at time
of writing, there doesn’t appear to be any need for worry-
ing over questions of precedence. A good Australian XI.
ought to do the trick easily.
Early prophecies of heavy scoring by the
Britishers seem to be based solely on their walloping of
Home-made soft stuff this past year or two. The 1899
record of test matches in England doesn’t suggest big
scores for Maclaren’s crowd. Out of 23 men who batted
against Darling’s XI. in the tests of two years ago only
five men averaged more than 40 runs per innings, and
only two of that quintette—Hayward and Lilley—are in
the present English team. Tyldesley and Quaife played
four innings each for an average of less than 15 runs.
Jessop had a couple of tries to do something wonderful,
and the results were 51 and 4. And, seeing that the ban-
tam smiter’s average for last English season was only 36J,
one is at a loss to understand how he ever came to be
boomed.
* * • *
“Slog”: Australia’s team for the first test-
match consists wholly of the ’99 team. This would
seem to indicate that in two years Australian
cricket has not produced a man fit to represent it in a
test match. Yet all the important fixtures of the season
have been won by the new blood. The main reason for
sticking to the old men is alleged by unkind critics to be
the coming tour of the twelfth Australian XI. Individu-
ally Darling, Gregory, Jones and Howell have not earned
their places. Poidevin, in the match against Maclaren’s
team, made (for once out) almost as many as Noble,
Trumper, and Gregory put together. Gregory has failed
altogether, and Stuckey or Hack has better claims than he.
As regards bowling, Howell has taken one wicket for 242
in club cricket, and was practically a failure in the two
big matches. With Clarke unavailable, Saunders (Vic.),
M'Beth (N.S.W.), and Travers (S.A.) have all done better
than Howell or Jones. Although the team as selected
should win easily, it is disheartening for the rising stars
to find themselves ignored. The selection committee
might easily have included at least one bowler and one
batsmen from the junior brigade men:
• • • •
Generally felt among cricket followers that,
after seven or eight were chosen, selectors of the
Australian team for first test match would have trouble
picking the best from many of the same quality available.
Whatever selection was made, heaps of adverse criticism
were expected, but didn’t eventuate. List published in
Monday's papers never brought a kick ; it is—to a man—
the Australian team that went ’Ome in ’99. Fact that
Britishers' strength is mostly in their batting is provided
for with variety in the Australian bowling, and strength
in the fielding. In batting there'll be no tail, for the
weakest—Jones —has proved himself up to making big
compilations when required. So far N.S. W. reps,
opposed to present English team have lost 31 wickets
for 1401 (average 45 T 9) while the visiting side’s figures
are 60 for 1165 (average 19'41). Darling being away from
first-class cricket so long, absence of top-notch form with
Trumble so far, and Noble's leg make cricket cranks
somewhat anxious.
• * • *
English champion bowler Lohmann’s death in
S’Africa, cetat 36, evidences how long consumption may
be held at bay with helpful surroundings—Lohmann
having taken the disease to S’Africa eight years ago. In
May last he piloted a S’African 11 through England. On
three different occasions he visited Australia with an
English team, and earned the reputation of being among
the finest all-round players ever seen.
* • * *
Sydney local grade cricket resumed Saturday.
Batting had a big day out. Sydney against Glebe were
6 down for 351—spite of the fact that three had gone for
15. Quist and Deery (for fourth wicket) accumulated
265. Deery notched 148, and Quist was there all the
afternoon for 168. Playing his first season for Sydney,
Quist has already made two centuries. Waverley com-
piled 350 against Leichhardt-Balmain, of which brothers
S. and O. Gregory contributed 205—Sid 115 (not out) and
Charlie 91. Prior to this, L.-B. hadn’t been beaten,
while Waverley’s list showed one defeat. North Sydney’s
total, opposed to Central Cumberland, was 345, of which
117 came from Iredale’s bat. Bilowell (picked for the
test) shaped badly, getting no wickets for 99 ; thus far
this season he has only captured one for 242.
• * • *
On Saturday Man-o’-War (late Pearson) romped
away with Warwick Farm “Farm” Stakes and Pace
Welter, heavily backed each time. Betting indicated only
the winner and another tried in the last event. Man-o’-
War was bred by recently-deceased jock. Tommy Hales,
who sold him to Messrs. H. E. A. and V. White, a foal at
foot with Corvette. Corvette owes origin to Martini-
Henry and Lady Vivian (imp.) Trainer (Tom Payten) of
Man-’o-War scored another race same day (three out of
five) with Hoop Iron, who gathered in Warwick Farm
Handicap and a big load of bets. Previously, the nearest
Hoop Iron got to winning was two seconds. This time he
showed vastly improved shape and pace and is among the
most promising 4-year-olds in the Loan State. With
(penalties included) 6.12 in Summer, and 6.10 in Tatt’s.
Cups, he looks worth an investment in one or other,
especially as he finished the journey (lrn. 21 f.) full of
going. Hoop Iron is a gelding by Metal from Nurse Girl.
» * • +
The public ought, to bless cup-winner Revenue
and Leslie Macdonald for having burst up the “ Press ”-
created fiction that preliminary gallops in public are
essential to the preparation of a horse for a big race. The
running of “byes” is nowadays exalted into something
like a virtue. Newspaper reporters, being too often
pimps for owners and Ikeys (owner and Ikey are often-
times one and the same robber), are in the habit of
referring to these precious gallops in public by “un-
wanted” ones as “the sharpening-up” process,
whereas it is a mere process of gulling the uninitiated and
deceiving the handicapper, if possible. As far as the
casual punter is concerned, a “ stiff ” favorite is not more
of a fraud than an outsider “ out for an airing.” He loses
his money for certain, whichever of them he may happen
to back. He stakes his coin on a competitor that carries the
owner’s colors, and represents a starting fee and jockey’s
fee, and when these open professions of genuineness are
delusive, the punter is the victim of a swindle. Horse-
reporters have been trying to blind him to the. truth
about “sharpening-up” tactics, but this victory of
Revenue in Melb. Cup ought to be an eye-opener for
every juggins. Revenue has been trained privately: all
his life. Within 2£ years previous to the Melb. Cup he
had made only six appearances in public, and the last o
those six failures happened 12 months ago. He was
brought to Flemington course from his private training
quarters on the morning of the Cup un-“ aired,” and un-
“ sharpened,” but not, O, not unbacked—and he won his
race all the better for not being a frequently beaten
animal.
* * * •
Impression prevalent that Melbourne suburban
race meetings are only a hand-to-mouth game is
apparently wide of the mark. Sec. Ashe, of Mentone
R.C.—giving evidence against Vic. Railway Commission-
ers recently—told that his club’s gross takings at each of
the year’s ten meetings averaged £577, supplying a net
profit, of £225.
* » • *
Interesting that recent winner, Goldfinder, at
Albion Park (Q.) races, was trained by the old-time jock
“ Paddy ” Pigott, now housed at Goodna. Pat landed
two Melb. Cups—one with Haricot, in ’74, and t’other
with Chester, five years later.
* * * *
Wellington Park (M.L'.) stallion St. Leger died
recently. St. Leger, by Doncaster, first saw light in
England, and was brought oht 21 years ago as a foal with
his dam Atlantis, by the Auckland Stud co. Sir Hercules
Robinson selected him. Though he proved a failure as,a
racer he was a big success at the stud, where he began
early. St. Hippo, St. Paul, and Waiuku —three of the
best M.L. has seen—were sired by St. Leger, who also got
St. Crispin, Nestor, Bluejacket, St. Laura, St. Andrew,
Clanranald, Formosan, Cruciform, Nobility, and numerous
others. At N.Z. Stud co.’s dispersal, 10 years since,
Messrs. L. D. Nathan and Thomas Morrin secured him
cheap for 400 gs. He was always heavily insured.
* * * *
Hard luck. At Taieri (M.L.), Nov. 9, race for
Stewards’ Purse was set going 10 minutes before time.
Transport won, with Wild West second. A protest sent
the starters back to the post, and this time positions of
the two named were reversed. They’d treat it differently
in the Commonwealth. Something similar occurred at
the * Oxboro ” years ago, but the kick of the second nag’s
owner had no effect.
• • * *
M.L. top-notch 3yr.-old Menschikoff’s record so
far will take a heap of wiping out. In 18 starts he has
landed the money 14 times, and pulled up second on four
occasions over all distances from 4 furlongs to lj miles.
* • * *
The pioneer of the Sloan jockey style in Maori-
land, L. Hewitt, scored nine wins at the late C.J.C.
meeting.
• • * •
Carbine stock is looking up in England (writes
a Bulletin correspondent). There are but few young
Carbines in training, yet not a week passes but they win
races. Record for week, Oct. I—7 : At Newmarket,
Semper Vigilans (4 years) won the 52nd Triennial Stakes,
and Pistol (2 years) won the Hopeful Stakes. In the
Boscawen Stakes, Fowlingpiece (2 years) was beaten by a
neck by Glass Jug, finishing lengths in front of Royal
Lancer, the second-best two-year-old colt of the season.
At Nottingham, Carabine won Nottingham Handicap of
£5OO, beating a big field of good-’uns. Mannlicher (Car-
bine—Memoir) is preferred by John Porter to his stable-
mate, Pietermaritzburg, for the Cesarewitch, both having
been handicapped at 6.13. As Pietermaritzburg has just
annexed the £lO,OOO Jockey Club’s Stakes, the punting
public thinks it has a good thing in Mannlicher, and has
made the Duke of Portland’s plodder a strong favorite.
A year ago there were no buyers of the young Carbines,
now there are no sellers. And the old horse’s stud-fee
for next season has been doubled.
• • • •
Inter-State glove mill, “Snowy” Sturgeon
against Melbourne mass of tissue, Preston, at Sydney
Gaiety A.O. last week didn’t reach anticipations as a fight,
but clinched previous opinions of Sturgeon’s capabilities.
Weight difference (in Preston’s favor) was every oz. of
2st. —“Snowy" scaled 10.8. Sturgeon opened cyclonic
like, nearly carrying Preston off his feet with a heavy
left jolt on the jaw, and the thing looked done, but
Preston gathered himself up, and, though running in to
some stiff punches, managed occasionally to drive a left
home hard enough to make “ Snowy ” quake, and once
floored him with a jab on the mouth of mule-kick force
that (’twas afterwards said) shifted a couple of teeth.
Sturgeon recuperated well, and was boss of the show ever
afterwards. The second round r had hardly opened When
the fight was over. A left hook on the side of the face
rattled the Melbournite badly, and coming in quickly
with the right somewhere about the same spot on the
opposite side of the phiz “Snowy” won a battle which
wasn’t as easy as it might have looked to some, and which
brought a lot more friends round him.
* * * *
Vanquishing of podgy Preston has set bruiser
“Snowy” Sturgeon talking. He'll fight any 11.4 man—
particularly Yankee Scanlan or Mick Dunn. Snowy’s
“ castor ” has been “shied” in too late; Scanlan has a
handful ahead With “Starlight,” Monday coming; and
M'Coll (again) in January. When Scanlan came here,
Sturgeon kept out of his reach behind a 10.7 limit, and
Armidale piek-and-shovel man . M‘Ooll was the only
N.S.W. bruiser gritty enough to take the unknown on.
Mick Dunn, ’tis said, has fought his last fight. Oribb’s
death shocked him, besides which a badly damaged hand
wouldn’t let him fight if he wanted to. Possibly
Sturgeon may hitch on to the young comer—ex-waterman
Johns—who is willing and clever enough to make a big
showing, if he doesn’t actually win.
* * * *
Australian light-weight champion, Hegarty, has
been in ’Frisco for months, and has not yet been able to
get a match. Booked for one, he broke a small bone a
few days before the date, and since then has vainly tried
to arrange another fight. In private letters Hegarty
says that a pug can do nothing in America without a
manager, and that the managers of the land want all the
profit to themselves.
• * * *
The Jeffries-Ruhlin fight, at ’Frisco, for the
world’s pug supremacy was a perfect farce. Jeffries
weighed 15st., and Rhulin 14.4. The Teuton appeared
thoroughly scared, and simply raced round the ring, pur-
sued by the man with the shaggy head ; but as he ran he
dealt Jeffries heavy blows on the body and gashed his lip.
This went on till the third round, when a whole cyclone of
swipes sent him reeling to his corner. Round five saw the
rag go upamid a storm of hoots, howls, and dreadful curses
from men who paid as high as 2j dollars to see a fight,
and had seen something like a cur dog-walloped.
♦ * * *
News by late Yankee mail is that the famous
colored bruiser, George Godfrey (“ Old Chocolate”), died
recently. Godfrey was the first man to meet and be
beaten by Peter Jackson in America 13 years ago, in
19 rounds of banging heart punches. Godfrey was
always an honest fighter, and scored many brilliant vic-
tories. He had in Boston a family and a home that he
cared more about than anything else, and would get
down to hard work between fights so as to keep want
from the door.
» * * *
Sydney swimmer Reade last week twice clipped
the world’s best for 1000 yards. In a trial—clocked by
N.S.W. Swimming Association’s official timekeeper, W. T.
Kerr aijd other prominent swimmers—Reade put up
13.38 2-5, beating 13.43, the best previously done.
Unfortunately, through being accomplished in a bath
much smallerthan allowed by English conditions,it wasn’t
accepted as a record. Fastest approved, performance before
Reade’s was Jarvis’ 14.10 in 99. At East Sydney swim-
ming meet on Saturday, Reade had another cut, but
finished slower in 13.511-5, through doing first half easier
on the advice of friends, instead of swimming in his own
way. Reade is unquestionably the speediest swimmer
Australia ever saw, and, on time, has something in hand
against champion-of-the-world Joey Nuttall. But Sydney
doesn’t nowadays grow sports of the old brand who took
up Larry Foley, Bill Trickett, Beach and co. So, as Reade
isn’t in a position to go ’Ome on his own, he’ll probably
blaze locally for a while and then drop out, and Australia
will have lost another possible world’s champion.
*;* * *
Reports that Australian and world’s champion
swimmer, Fred. Lane, is done are much short of fact.
Writer saw him propel faster than ever at Sydney Domain
baths, Saturday, and cover the hundred In 60.4-5 —4-'
better than “ Mud ” Bishop’s Australasian record, and
only 3-ssec. slower than Englishman Derbyshire’s world’s
quickest. Lane has never done as well as this before,
and looks like going- better.
y * * *
Old Timer: Recently deceased Hon. George
Thornton was president of N.S.W. Bowing Association
for the past 23 years. Said to have taken to aquatics in
’33, figuring in whale-boat races. He created the “ Aus-
tralian Subscription Rowing Club,” the first of its kind
in Sydney, and did much for old-time scullers and scull-
ing, having financed Dick Green’s trip to England in 1863,
and offered to provide the wherewithal for a return-
match with Chambers.
* * * »
Final of Austral wheel race in Melbourne this
Saturday absorbs as much attention as a big horse
struggle. Heats last Saturday were exciting and bristled
with surprises. Don. Walker fulfilled anticipations horn
of previous racing present season, and shaped brilliantly ;
his return to form recalls what he did years ago,
and is remarkable. When Zimmerman was top of
the Yankee heap, cycling enthusiasts talked of
financing Don to America to take the flier down,
but, before a move was made, Zimmerman came
here, and went under to the Australian, who fairly para-
lysed the crowd with his phenomenal finishing sprint. It
was a revelation to “ Zimmy,” who didn’t think the
world held a man his equal. Walker subsequently
dropped back, and hasn’t shown a glimmer of that
brilliancj- until the last month or two. The biggest set-
back on Saturday last was the defeat of O’Cal-
laghan, who, as‘ second favorite, carried a great
load of coin. J. W. Parsons was another public “ cert.”
that went under. Great old plugger Bilmartin’s showing
proved a huge improvement on what he did the previous
Saturday, and at time of writing it isn’t possible to get
better than threes about his prospects. Bill has a good
solid bit on for himself, too. Only N.S.W. rider to qualify
was R. W. Lewis who, with 70yds., looks well in. Limit
(300yds.) man Scott had last year’s final all his way but
for falling ; he shapes as well as ever, and won his heat
in convincing style. Megson (five miles champ, of the
continent) tips A. 0. Middleton, and Megson ought not
to be a bad judge.
* • • *
“Plugger” : M. Lewis, on the 250yds. mark, is
said to be the “ Revenue ” of this year’s Austral—and he
looks it. Jack Parsons, who had also been put about as a
“ good thing,” didn’t look it, even before he was required
to make a winning effort in his heat, and couldn’t raise the
necessary steam. But Ken Lewis’s brother is a horse of
very different stamina. If Don Walker, or any other, can
give him his handicap a lot of people who ought to know
will be very much surprised.
'*• * ♦
Mark Foy’s Panhard motor-car experiment to
Melbourne Cup and back was successful. Actual running
time (going over) for 588 miles was 38J hours. Only
previous trip of a similar nature was W. J. C. Elliott's
On a Dion tricycle, the running of which occupied
39 hours. Frequent night travelling materially affected
the Panhard’s rate of progression. Coming back, running
averaged 35 miles an hour, with roads, as a rule, ex-
ceedingly bad.
* * * •
World’s champion chops at recent Tas. Axe-
men’s Carnival were exciting and unusually interesting.
Victorian cracks competed, but M.L. representatives
didn’t. Underhand chop (blocks 6ft. 4in. girth), first day,
looked like being a big struggle between Clark (Tas.) and
Blanc (Vic.). Blow for blow sounded, and chip for chip
flew. Thousands looking on were worked to high pitch.
Blanc got the axe fast in the middle of the second scarf,
but Clark, running out of wood, left the finish soft for
his opponent. Blanc’s time was 4.45. and Clark’s 5.38.
Third man (Buckingham) occupied 5.50. Second day saw
t’other champion chop—2ft. blocks this time. Bryan
opened well, and turned his log in 2.2. Blanc was last to
get the first scarf in, taking 2.30. M'Carthy turned at
2.24. Absence of condition told on Bryan at the end of
4min. ;he was then furthest through. M’Carthy and
Blanc kept going in fine form, with the result that Mac.
won in 5.1, with Blanc second (5.7), Bryan third (5.17),
and Buckingham fourth (5.19); Clark didn’t finish.
M’Carthy, by the way, won the Commonwealth Champion-
ship at Hobart, specially got up for the Jook o’ York’s
edification.
* * * •
Maori footballer, Billy War brick, recently
succumbed to consumption at his brother’s home, Bay of
Plenty (M.L.) Billy was ailing long, and lost tissue
rapidly for some time. He owned a capital physique till
lung trouble compelled him to forsake athletics. He had
a lease of Lavender Bay (Sydney) floating-baths for years,
and only returned to M.L. five or six weeks ago. Was
one of the Maori team that went ’Ome a few years since
and did so well. The whole family were splendid speci-
mens of athletic manhood, first-flight Rugby players.
* * • *
Gunn, winner of Yic. Rifle Association’s King’s
prize t’other day, has, during 12 years’ shooting, got
among the first 30 for the Queen’s prize on nine occasions.
He was fourth in that event last year, with an aggregate
of 292—notching seven bull’s-eyes after missing with the
first three. His weapon is a special Fulton Lee-Metford.
* * • *
W. Martin and D. J. Walker on Red Birds got
home at the Austral meet, Saturday. Bill collared the
ten miles’ scratch race, and Don the one mile Blue
Riband. Both riders won their heats in the Austral
Wheel Race.*
The Bulletin’s Christmas number has
been a great success. Copies can still be obtained
from the office at 6d. each, accompanied by the
necessary postage.
Each Australian State is being given, as
vacancies occur, a military Governor —which, of
course, is a part of the Chamberlain plan for
making Australia a good ground for the Imperial
recruiting-sergeant.
Obit., C. H. Cox, M.L.C. A type of the
successful man on the Australian land, with a
passion for everything that grows or grazes. He
sought to represent N.S.W. landed interests in
the first Federal Senate, but failed, though his
enthusiasm for the farmer and his political services
in the State Parliament earned him the support
of many.
Ex-Mayor James Graham, of Sydney, is
being gradually relieved by kind friends of some
of the burdens of office. A while ago he was
Mayor of Sydney, M.P., University lecturer,
president of half a dozen societies, a practising
doctor, and aspirant for a place in a coalition
Ministry. Belmore electors a few months ago
relieved him of his political work ; now Sydney
Municipal Council has elected another Mayor,
Mr. T. J. Hughes. Dr. Graham doesn’t alto-
gether appreciate his friends’ kindness, but he
should. A thin man can’t do too many things at
once. As to Mr. Hughes, he has brains, leisure,
municipal experience, and enthusiasm, is well-
endowed with the world’s goods, and will please
the city with a charming Mayoress.
From Frank Harris’ new London paper,
The Candid Friend :
The whose heart is not very strong, has been
very much worried about it lately. When he wag at
Homburg last year, he drove over one day to Nauheim to
be examined by Dr. Schott, who is one of the greatest
authorities on all cardiac affections. He has been sum-
moned during the last few days to London, as His Majesty
is anxious to have his opinion and advice again.
The Lancet, by the way, denies a recent state-
ment in the Medical Press and Circular “ that
papillomatous growths have on three occasions
been removed from the King’s left vocal cord,
and that an immediate operation of another
nature has since been rendered necessary. _ A
papillomatous growth is a kind of wart, which
may degenerate and become ‘malignant’— i.e.,
cancerous.”
The Christmas annual supplement to the
N.S.W. Charities Gazette and General Intel-
ligencer this year is a well-got-up appeal for
funds to give the 2216 adults and children, who
receive a weekly grant of food from the Bene-
volent Society of N.S.W., something extra for
Christmas dinner. In addition to this little army
there is another of children, destitute old people,
and women who have had to take refuge in the
midwifery section of the Asylum—all absolutely
dependent on the Society for food and shelter.
The Society appeals for even the smallest contri-
butions to help a good work; and everyone should
strive to give at least one small child a real good
time on Xmas Day.

FAIRFAX « ROBERTS,
Vice-Regal Jewellers, 23 HUNTER STREET, SYDNEY
Of 137 men who went to S’Africa in the
Second Contingent of Australian Bushmen, only
eight are left fighting, (18 having been dolled, and
the rest invalided home. This disastrous state of
affairs is attributed in some quarters to the
“abuse” of our soldiers as scouts for British
regiments. A heavy price to pay for a little glory.
“Mr. McCulloch, Victorian Minister for
Public Works, suggests that the Commonwealth
should immediately offer 5000 more men for ser-
vice in South Africa.” The Bulletin suggests
that the Commonwealth should offer—M'Culloch.
M'Culloch can be very well spared. We can do
without M‘Culloch. If the ardent M'Culloch will
only go and lay his bones on the African veldt
The Bulletin promises him precisely the same
reward as the others get— i.e., a week’s cheap
sympathy from the M'Cullochs who stay warm at
home and shi’iek “loyalty ” to the Other Fellow —
and then oblivion. By all accounts of the
war it is plain that the Australians have
been used shamefully. Instead of being or-
ganised into a battalion, they have been
split up into detachments to scout for English
regiments—served and cockered at the expense of
the “colonials.” To the Australians has
fallen all the meanest, most miserable
work—they have been used as scullions
to the array ; and bumptious English brigadiers
have rubbed in their ignominy by calling them
“ white-livered bastards.” For protesting against
suchtreatmentsomemen were court-martialledand
sentenced to death; the sentence was commuted to
penal servitude ; and it needed the intervention
of King Edward himself to secure their release.
The Bulletin wants the opinion of those men
about sending Australians to this farm-burning,
children-slaughtering war. But, in the meantime,
by all means send a contingent—a contingent
of M'Cullochs. Send all the Fat Men, who
have no objection to seeing others doing dirty
work while they sit at home in safety and
ease. Send all the “ loyalty’’-prating poli-
ticians. Send all the “ loyalty ’’-shrieking
journalists. Send the wealthy subscribers of
hundreds and thousands—who risk money of
which they have plenty, but will not risk a shred
of their blessed skins. Let’s see the Fat Men’s
Contingent go to Africa. Let’s see Boodle
scouting on one biscuit a day, and shivering under
one blanket at night. Let’s keep our bone and
muscle at home, where we badly need it. But
cheers, loud and lusty cheers, for the Fat Men’s
Cohentingent !

Perseverance : 10,743 tons for 14,1470 z., the
record production for a month, and a div. of 10s.,
amounting to £87,500, declared. The mine paid no
other div. this year and none at all last, but turned
out a little div. of Is. in ’99, none at all in ’9B, and
one of 4s. in ’97. Since the machinery started in
August last year it has cleared off a debt of about
£lOO,OOO incurred in building the largest sulphide plant of
the field, The mine, the writer is credibly informed, is
looking remarkably well in the upper levels, i.e., above
the 400 ft. ; the 500 ft. level to date has been disappointing,
and the 600 and 700 ft. no better. Development is being
pushed ahead, but if the lower levels continue poor, and
there is not much encouragement in this direction in the
adjoining mine (Lake View) the Perseverance will not
pay many 10s. divs. before it is worked out. Without
any payable ore so far revealed in its lowest levels, the
Perseverance has to-day a market value equal to the
Great Boulder, which has no poor or barren levels, and is
opening up at 1200 ft. one of the best levels in the mine.
* * * •
Lake View Consols: 10,104 tons, 14,4120 z.
About the usual average, and good enough if it can be
maintained, but of this there is much doubt. Many
months ago it was here printed that to largely increase
the gold output by increasing the plant was next to im-
possible, and that the move was unwarranted,
anyhow. Sometime afterwards consulting engineer
Mackinnon warned the directors in London that
this was the true position ; but manager
Hartman said the proposed increase was “ quite
justified,” and asked for an independent examination
of the mine. The names of Hewitson and Hamilton,
of the Associated and Great Boulder respectively, were
suggested, and the former seems to have undertaken the
task which he expects to complete, it is said, next week.
“ It is evident that his examination of the property will
only be a cursory one and he will have to depend in a
great measure uuon the mines assay-books for his values.
Nevertheless if the directors are to decide between the
very diverse statements of Hartman and Mackinnon as to
the mine’s ore reserves, a thorough sampling of the differ-
ent levels is absolutely necessary.” Meanwhile, the public
has decided whom to follow. Mackinnon, without a
doubt. When writer made the statement, some months
ago, the shares were standing at £9, and within a few
days of Mackinnon’s declaration leaking out they fell to
£7; and they have been down to £5 10s. No doubt in
anticipation of a 10s. div. before the end of the year,
they are now standing at £6, but' even that depreciation
amounts to three-quarters of .a,million ; and, as this has
taken place in the face of an unaltered monthly output of
14,000 odd oz., the inference is very clear.
» * ■» *
W.A. mines in Switzerland : A native of that
country sends along a copy of a Swiss paper in which
Chaffers, Mt. Wagahong and Paringa shares are exten-
sively advertised for sale, and the marvellous prospects
and potentialities of each set forth in eloquent language.
Our correspondent wants to know if they are good
enough for a very near relative to buy. The Bulletin
unhesitatingly replies No. The prices wanted for these
shares in Switzerland were double what they can be bought
at to-day, so those who were caught by this means of
selling worthless paper have already lost half their
money, and have a good chance of losing it all.
m m m •
Great Boulder South: “Judging by surface
improvements which are taking place the management
has great faith in the future of the mine.” Same remark
may be applied to about 50 other co.’s which have for
years past been trying to find gold outside the defined
payable gold-bearing area of Kalgoorlie. They all started
with great faith in the future and have spent in the
aggregate certainly more than a million of money only to
establish a very ugly past. This mine has only its name
to recommend it, and as there are several mines between
it and the Great Boulder, and it is about half-a-mile away
from that mine, even the name is a lie.

The Argent (Zeehan) with £9OOO, and Hercules,
with £5OOO output for Nov. are the chief gleams of hope
amid the gloom that now envelopes the ancient glory of
the Silver West. A new and apparently important find
(Dec. 4) is reported at the Argent.

.- * • *
Tasmania’s Nov. exports of minerals reached
£184,168, an increase of £61,577 over same month last
year. Total for the 11 months of the year, £1,623,143, an
increase of £182,727 over first 11 months of 1900.
Mt. Lvell : Net profit earned last half-year,
£125,000 ; £lO,OOO added to reserve, which now stands at
£50,000, invested in Govt, debentures and in fixed
deposit. A 2s. 6d. div. declared, also Is. 6d. bonus per
share. Why this? Why not say 4s. at once and be done
with it? Anyhow, it makes 10s. 6d. for the year, and if
there was any hope of this being continued for any length
of time, Mt. Lyells would be a magnificent investment at
£3 155., the present price. Chairman Bowes Kelly
told shareholders that there had been a fall of £3
per ton in copper, and that standard brands were
then only worth £62 10s. per ton, but that the
sudden fall was attributed to speculative dealings
of market operators. No doubt the meeting went
away satisfied that copper would soon be at £7O again.
Alas! since that meeting, held but a fortnight ago, other
falls have taken place, and at time of writing copper was
down to £55 per ton, and the quotation for three months
was no higher. A fall of £2 7s. 6d. in a day, over £7
since the time of that meeting, and more than £2O lower
than copper was for some time last year. The situation
is getting mighty serious, although no allusion is made to
it by any of the Australian dailies, and the fact that Mt.
Lyell shares still stand at £3 15s. makes writer wonder
whether it is the div. that kept them steady, or
if the fall has been brought about by the Ameri-
can syndicate to force other copper co.’s into the
“ combine.” Private cables have been received
to the latter effect. Anyhow, it is the biggest fall
that has taken place for years, and it looks as if the
syndicate, big as it is, is not strong enough to keep up
the price of the metal. If copper goes to £5O and stays
there Mt. Lyell cannot be worked to pay, and this can be
said of nearly every copper-mine in Australia, the most
notable exception being Great Cobar.

The Sabbath tipster : “The rise in copper has
helped to strengthen Mt. Lyell, which are worth, say,
755.” Ye gods ! and this in face of a drop of £5 in cop-
per in a few days, £lO in a few weeks, and £2O in a few
months!

Waihi, M.L. : Last month’s return a record,
within the shadow of £40,000, or a total of £385,000 for
the 12 months—bringing the grand total got from the
mine up to £1,905,000. Shares steadily rising the last
two months. They went to over £lO last year, after
which the share capital was increased. They are now
about £6 10s., and it is not unlikely that they will go tc
£lO. when another issue of shares will probably be made.

N.S.W. Public Service Association, having
lost all hope that Parliament will do anything definite
re the insolvent Civil Service Superannuation Fund,
has drafted a little Bill of its own. The Associa-
tion’s solution of the difficulty is the same old Borrow.
Treasury bills are to be “ floated ” (apparently given tc
the Service) for the amount involved, bearing 3 per cent,
interest. Being an easily-negotiable security, the impe-
cunious civil servant can pass on the bills for cash, at, if
necessary, a small discount. The Service argues that
another million or two to the public de t of £75,000,000
won’t matter much, and that the 3 per cent, interest is
already accumulating on the full amount of the Super-
annuation Fund. The idea should he adopted if only to
give a beautiful completeness to the N.S.W. system of
borrowing b.v having the State’s Treasury bills palmed off,
at a discount, on a sniffing and suspicious landlady for
Chawley’s board bill.

Writes a London correspondent :
The London police .deserve credit for their parental
management of the local “ anarchists.” At their precious
‘demonstrations” there is not even an arrest of the
ringleaders ” of these bogus ruffians. The men in blue
treat them as mere bank-holiday “ disorderlies,” and give
the order to “ Be off with you ” in a cheerful, muscular
manner. The life of a professional anarchist in London
is not without its pleasures as longas he can find a secret
silly association to supply an income of £2 or £3 a week.'
He just walks around in a slouch hat and an old brown
velveteen coat, and drops mysteriously into a ch»ap
Italian restaurant for his meals. Lingers long over his
meals, and plays dominoes with other members of other
brotherhoods. Is sometimes, but not often, accompanied
by one or two mild, well-to-do looking men of the
Juggins type ; and on these occasions he has the manner
of a conspirator developed to the fullest extent, and sits
£ with his ears pricked, as though expecting the news of
Somebody’s assassination to arrive at any moment. The
Jugginses are presumably contributors to the secret fund
for wrecking the universe.

The New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency
Co., Limited, report : —Wheat--At the near approach of
the season much interest is naturally evinced now that a
record harvest is assured chiefly in regard to the basis of
values likely to rule. The general opinion is that 2s. 3d.
to 2s. 3&d. will be the millers' opening rates, but these
prices are somewhat disappointing to the growers and
probably large stocks will be held both in the country
and metropolitan warehouses in hopes of higher values
being obtainable later on.*
Tuberculosis.— Mr. A. R. Beck, Easfc-st.,
Brompton, Adelaide, writes to The Bulletin :
“ Re your article (28/9/’01) from British Medical
Journal about the open air treatment for consump-
tion. It does seem a farce to theaverage intelligence
when doctors have come so completely down, from
former drugging processes, as to assert that nothing
is required but fresh air, and expect to be paid for
telling that. Where tuberculosis shows itself in
other forms besides lung trouble, nobody would be
foolish enough to believe that fresh air alone could
cure; why should it be able to cure the disease in the
lungs with a much better chance than in other parts
of the body? Surely a specific is required to cure
such forms of tuberculosis, and such a specific would
thus be a great thing for lung trouble also. Mr.
Beck further states that he himself was cured by a
specific for tuberculosis of both lungs, together with
fistula in anus, and says that he is willing to give any
information wished for, and subject himself to any
medical examination in the interest of humanity. *

HEALTH, STRENGTH, HAPPINESS
HEALTH
Few recognise the BLISS of being in PERFECT
HEALTH, until SUDDENLY certain symptoms are
noticed, slight at first, but gradually RECURRING,
causing annoyance and SUFFERING, the individual
being forced to admit he is unwell, and finally compelled to acknowledge that PERFECT
HEALTH is a PRECIOUS BOON.
STRENGTH
What a blow to a man who discovers he is LOSING
his STRENGTH and VIGOR, and THAT perhaps at
a period when he most NEEDS ALL his physical
and menial capabilities to battle through life. They
are PRICELESS to him. He must by every means endeavor to REGAIN THEM.
HAPPINESS
Does happiness consist in Wealth? No. Who can
be happy without HEALTH and STRENGTH?
Wealth materially assists the endurance of suffering-,
but PERFECT HEALTH, STRENGTH and VIGOR
should be the natural inheritance of EACH individual, and are invaluable gifts, tending to a
happy existence and long life. THE HEALTHY, STRONG MAN CAN contend, and SUCCESS-
FULLY, with the billows of life. BUT HOW • CAN THE WEAKLY AND SICKLY ?
Health, Strength and Happiness If constant study, combined
with intelligence and a
thorough practical knowledge of the many ailments to which the human subject is a victim, THEN I SHOULD
be able to achieve that result AFTER THIRTY YEARS’ PRACTICE, enabling me to bring to bear unlimited
and VARIED EXPERIENCE in all ACUTE and CHRONIC CASES of NERVOUS EXHAUSTION, CHEST,
SKIN and SPECIAL DISEASES OF MEN XND WOMEN. PERFECT SYSTEM OF ACCURATE DIAGNOSIS
AND TREATMENT BY MAIL FOR COUNTRY PATIENTS A SPECIAL FEATURE.
Dr. WATSON’S Fee is Strictly Moderate. He recognises the fact that the Public is
prepared to pay a Reasonable Fee for Assured Skill in the treatment of Complaints of
whatever character.
Consultations from 10 a.m. to 9 p.m. Daily. ABSOLUTELY FREE.

From a Lighthouse.
Straight before us starlit waters, stretching far
beyond our sight,
Overhead the white clouds sailing through a sky
of misty light;
And we idly watch the ripple 3 where the star-
reflections quiver,
Watch the shadows on the sandbanks where th
mangrove hushes shiver,
Listen to the weird-voiced curlews calling from the
scrub and plain,—
And our hearts are filled with longing just to
greet a friend again.
Oh ! the life that lies behind us! Oh ! the years
that stretch before !
In our manhood we are exiled —driftwood on an
alien shore,
Dreaming of the far-off cities and the friends we
used to know,
While we watch in saddened silence by the
lantern’s steady glow.
Comrades gone beyond our greeting, do you ever
give a thought,
In your careless, jovial lifetime, to the friends
whom once you sought?
Years that you have half forgotten, days you never
thought to mark,
By our hearts are garnered safely ; in the silerce
and the dark
We have made them our companions, tinged the'r
sweetness with our pain;
For we know that youth is over, and that man-
hood ’s on the wane,
And the best years we have wasted, squandered
with a spendthrift hand
Till we drifted, wrecked and hopeless, to an un-
familiar land.
And throughout the long night watches, when the
white stars blaze above,
Visions come to us and haunt us of an unforgotten
love, —
Loyal hearts that loved us dearly in those joy >u -
years of old,
Hands that helped us, lips that blessed us, now,
alas ! grown still and cold :
And the mourning waters mock us, and the star-
lit midnight falls,
And across the barren sand-wastes ring the hope-
less curlew-calls.
N.T.
M.S.

The W.A. Boodle Party.
The queer, coil into which Westralian politics
have developed during the last few weeks has not
been altogether unforeseen by the readers of signs
and omens. Right through the piece, the
machine god has been A. E. Morgans, capitalist,
investor, exploiter of mining properties, and
master of finance. Suave, polished, much-travelled
and hospitable, he has seldom made mistakes —till
now. In South America he earned the
title of “ The Friend of the President ”;
presidents change very often over there, but
Morgans usually managed to be the friend of the
man who had concessions to giveaway. Morgans
has long been ambitious to become W. A. Premier
and, deeming the psychological moment had
arrived—the Coronation will take place next June
gently worked up Pi esse, as Leader of the
Opposition, to move a vote of censure on the
Leake Government. The result—a majority of
one in a House of which 46 of the 50 members voted
—left PinssE with the task of forming a Ministry.
Personally, Piesse is unpopular, and politically
he was, when sent for, rather more unsavoury
than usual, for the report of the Board
of Inquiry into charges levelled against John
Davies, the suspended Manager of Railways, had
splashed a lump of mud about the size of an eagle
on Piesse’s political coat-tail. He offered
portfolios to men whose seats were far from
secure, and they were afraid to face their con-
stituents, and didn’t expect a Piesse Ministry to
last a month, anyway. Failing to form a Ministry
Piesse suggested that Morgans be sent for. The
suave Welshman's schemes had worked out as he
anticipated, but he overlooked the weakest
spot in the web he had been weaving.
Morgans is the apostle of private enter-
prise for public works. Now, Westralians
have had more than enough of the system.
The Great kSouthern railway purchased from the
company by the Govt., and a white elephant at
that—the Midland railway, under offer to the State
for over a million and in doubtful repair along its
whole course—the Kurrawang Syndicate tram-
way, the promoters of which bought material from
Govt, and partly paid for it with Govt, water and
owed the rest —the Canning-Jarrah line, with a
ruling gradient of somewhere about 1 in 15, also
purchased by Govt.—these matters have made
private enterprise smell witli a very lofty perfume
in Westralia. Therefore, when Morgans, who
owns large interests on the Pilbarra goldfield,
and is known to be anxious to construct a
private railway from Port Hedland to Marble
Bar, sought to form a Government, the elec-
tors began to think things out loud. Morgans
proposed a coalition, but the Leake crowd,
only beaten by one vote in the House, and
certain that the vast majority of the community
was on its side, refused with emphasis. The new
Premier formed his Ministry with some difficulty,
mostly of men with shaky seats and no political
record. Out of three Ministers who have, up to
time of writing, been opposed at re-election, two
have lost their seats which seems to end
Morgans’ alleged majority. Forrest’s Ministry
lasted 10 years and 46 days ; Throssell’s 95
days: Leake’s 178 days. Morgans’ looks the
most hopeless of the lot.
At Poverty Point.
In the first days of the Amy Castles boom, that lucky
young- lady was described as a rising Melba, with a great
operatic career before her. The one point on which all
the Melbourne boomsters agreed was the desirability of
sending her “ dramatic soprano ” voice to Europe to be
trained for operatic triumphs, and on the allegation that
she would in the not far distant future become a Queen
of the lyric stage, 14,000 people fought for the privilege
of hearing her warble at Melbourne Exhibition Building.
That was 2£ years ago. And now, we are told, Rev.
Father Robinson, the boss of the original boom, has
arranged with manager Williamson for an Australian
concert tour, which Miss Amy is to commence next
autumn.
Asa managerial spec., The Bulletin would back Amy
Castles against Melba for a ton of money. Amy will
likely enough cost J. O. Williamson not much more per
week than Musgrove will pay Melba per night. Already
we hear the voice of the smiling padre “ Tickets for
the Amy Castles’ concert are to be had at the dhure.”
“ D.R.U.G.” : The drug used by the hero in “ Man and
his Makers ” is not morphia, but hasheesh, which acts
differently on different individuals, always accentuating
the natural tendencies. In Joh.i Radleigh, it brings out
especially his underlying poetical vein. Wilson Barrett
is the last man to write about a drug without first
inquiring fully into its nature and effect. [The drug is
not stated in the play, but opium is inferred. No known
drug would give the effects portrayed by Mr. Barrett, nor
is it possible to give any scientific explanation of a
fortnight’s “horrors” in the case of a drug-taker who
has only been indulging for a few weeks. If the poem
so frequently quoted in “Man and his Makers” is the
result of a drug, the pharmacopoeia has anew terror.]
A certain foreign tenor, who, years ago, “knocked”
Australia with his high note, was so afflicted with the
native modesty of the tenor that he had his letters from
Italy addressed (rough translation) : To the Most Magnifi-
cent Tenor, Signor Chief Singer of Mr. ’s
Grand Opera Cos., Melbourne, Australia.”
About the most interesting item in the programme of
Mrs. Bracy’s benefit performance at Melb. Princess’s last
week was a song by her evergreen husband. Henry
Bracy, in his fifty-somethingth year, might have been
mistaken, and probably was mistaken by some people,
for a 35-year-old stranger introducing a fresh young
sugary tenor voice to public notice, He sang “When
Other Lips ” and “ Take a Pair of Sparkling Eyes ” quite
charmingly. During his long absence from the operatic
stage the “old man ” must have been nursing his voice
back to its early prime.
Apropos Phil May’s approaching appearance on the
English stage, it is printed that he only aspires to minor
parts. ‘ He will take the rdle of the apothecary in “Romeo
and Juliet,” of Pistol in “Henry V.,” and should
“Richard III.” be represented he will have the somewhat
insignificant character of the Lord Mayor.
A considerable percentage of the Melbourne audiences
that heard Dalia Bassich as Desdemona, in “ Otello,”
judged her identity from the programme, and thought
the Bulgarian lady was De Vere Sapio.
Dan Barry is still going strong. His advt. in Kadina
and Wallaroo Times announced a grand production of
“ Soldiers of the Queen ” in honor of “ British courage ”
and the Battle of Waterloo. This stirring drama had
been played, said the advt., for six consecutive months
in Melbourne. Also, the Kadina and Wallaroo critic
stated that “ Soldiers of the Queen ” had so worked on
Lord Hopetoun’s feelings that»he sent an autograph
letter to Dan thanking him for the production. Dan’s
tongue is as honeyed as ever, seemingly. “We must not
forget,” said the local critic, “ to give a word of praise to
the efficient orchestra ; and last, but riot least, we must
remember to thank the genial Dan himself for the grace-
ful compliment he paid us, as a community, in his
pleasant oration.” Happy Wallaroo !
Said that Musgrove is “coining money” at London
Shaftesbury Theatre with a farcical comedy called “ Are
You a Mason ? ”
Musgrove’s tremendous baritone, Max Eugene, who is
taller and broader than any other singer in Australia, has
a passion for canaries, and carries a whole aviary of them
wherever he goes. His chief distress, as a consequence
of the breaking-down of the s.s. Monowai, is stated in
the pathetic plaint: “ Ah, most sad, I lost two of my
canaries! ”
“ Trial By Jury,” which cropped up again at a Melb.
benefit matinee t’other week, is always interesting as
showing the source of Arthur Sullivan’s musical humor.
The music in “ T.8.T.” is churchy-churchy. It could
hardlv have been written by a man who had not graduated
as a chorister, and studied anthems with his tongue in
his cheek.
The Solidarity-Labor Municipal Council at
Broken Hill is now declared by the local
Solidarity-Labor paper to be an absolute failure.
The fight for the Queensland Premiership
grows interesting. Rutledge, father-in-law to the
Kanaka Courier, is first favorite. Foxton, a
colleague, is fighting tooth and nail to down him ;
while Works Minister Leahy seeks, by various
means, to bind the rank and file to his elevation.
Thynne, the Tom Slattery of Q. politics—T.S. is
very thin—may decide the issue. As to Philp,
nobody seems to take him into account.
The Senate first struck out the educational
test from the Aliens Immigration Bill, then re-
jected M‘Gregor’s proposal to put in an absolute
prohibition clause ; and finally, after further con-
versation and consideration, restored the original
clause and passed the Bill. There were two
stupendous holes in M‘Gregor’s proposition. It
provided for the exclusion of the person born in
Asia or Africa, but it left free access for the North
American nigger and the South American half-
breed. And (what was still worse) Australia has
had the broadest possible intimation that the in-
sertion of M‘Gregor’s clause would wreck the Bill
utterly in England and leave Australia open for
an indefinite period to niggers of all kinds. The
classes who wanted the Bill wrecked worked man-
fully for the adoption of the absolute-prohibition
idea, and it found friends in many strange quar-
ters ; and Sydney Telegraph gave way to bitter
rage when all was over, and headed its leading
article “The White Australia Bubble.” Thus
does an unwise paper get mad in large print and
give itself away.
A brand of spirits which has received the com-
mendation of practically every other market in the world
may, from some accident of fashion, get less than its due
credit in someone country. Martell’s Brandy is a case in
point. Yielding to none in excellence, it is a special
favorite in the Mother country, and in N.S. W. people are
now beginning to realise that its extraordinary popu-
larity elsewhere is due to sheer merit. According to the
leading Government Analyst of Great Britain it is im-
possible to produce a purer or more genuine brandy than
“ MARTELL'S."*
Mr. Justice Cohen has been appointed
President of N.S. W. Industrial Arbitration Court.
Judge Backhouse’s name was suggested in many
quarters, but was not favorably canvassed in
either labor or employing circles. When the
Broken Hill strike leaders were put on trial it was
at the special request of labor representatives that
Judge Backhouse was sent up to try the cause, as
he was understood to have strong democratic
leanings. After the trial some of the Labor
leaders complained that it was the Judge’s
summing-up, and not the evidence, which secured
a conviction. All things considered, the appoint-
ment of Mr. Cohen was a wise one. If this
involves, as it probably will, an addition to the
Supreme Court Bench, Murray, Backhouse, or
one other of the District Court Judges should go
up.

An Influenza’s Legacy.
WEAK & HELPLESS.
THE BIGHT THING AT THE RIGHT TIME.
The Case of
Mrs. I- BLENKINSOPP. *
(A IAMBTON NARRATIVE.)
While most people in the Commonwealth
are employing their brains in mathematical
efforts to ascertain to what degree their per-
sona' interests will be affected by the new
tariff which has been submitted, it is grati-
fy mg to note that Mrs. Isabella Blenkin-
sepp of Church-street, Lambton, was will-
ing to devote a little time to our reporter
on another matter.
“ I think,” said Mrs. Blenkinsopp, “ that
it is a duty I should perform for the bene-
fit of everybody around me to let them know
how I got over the physical difficulties
which once beset me. When I left my
native town of Durham I did not know what
sickness was, but after being out here
awhile I found that the hot climate took a
lot of the energy out of me, besides making
me weaker, and more liable to contract any
disease that happened to be about. Four
years ago I fell a victim to an epidemic of
influenza which was laying low many people
in these parts but after being reduced to a
perfect wreck the influenza left me.
“ Did it take long to recover from the
effects of that complaint?” queried the
writer.
“ I was so long about it that I thought I
was never going to get better, and most
of my neighbours were of opinion that I
would not live. It was evident that my
system was thoroughly upset, for whenever
I took a little nourishment I felt dreadfully
ill for hours afterwards. My food, instead
of digesting in the ordinary way, seemed to
ferment in the stomach, an opinion which I
formed through the manner in which I
swelled out with wind, and I met with
severe pains at times which almost bent me
double. These stomach pains were supple-
mented by a feeling in my chest as if I had
swallowed something heavy and indigesti-
ble, and the effect of this affliction was to
hinder my breath from being easily ob-
tained. Indeed, the choking sensation
which came over me at times was so bad
that I have actually had to gasp for breath,
and the exertion which w'as involved in this
way had the effect of bringing on a violent
palpitation of the heart. I made sure that
s nnething very serious was wrong with that
organ, though for many a night 1 could not
sleep for the painful throbbing which was
going on, and after beating in this way for
smne time the beating of my heart died
away till its pulsation could hardly be felt,
and there I lay in dreadful anguish wander-
ing if it was going to stop altogether. Now
and again I dozed off into a restless slumber
which I would rather have been without, be-
cause I dreamt about such awful things, and
when I woke up again I could not decide
in my mind whether I had been dreaming
or whether the horrible thoughts were real.
I sank into an unbearable state of nervous-
ness, as I was too timid to stay in the house
alone for fear of something happening, while
the slamming of a gate, or the sound of
voices, gave me such a start tha,. I could not
keep myself from tremb’ing. A miserable
life like this was surely not worth living, I
thought, e>pe ially as I was tortured with
frightful agonies across the kidneys, which
almost paralysed me when I walked about
a little, besi >es having a perpetual twitch-
ing between the shoulders. My aching head
felt too heavy to hold up, while
giddiness came upon me so suddenly that I
was always careful to be within reach of
something to hold on to in case I might fall.
First thing in the mornings I was disgusted
with the taste in my mouth, and I never
looked at my tongue but what it was covered
w.th a nasty fur. How monotonously the
time dragged a ! ong only myself can tell as
I was too drowsy and feeble to interest my-
self in anything, altho igh there was nothing
I could think of doing, as even an hour’s
reading was put out of the question by the
swollen and hazy condition of my eyes. The
colour of my skin became almost yellow,
but what distressed me more than anything
else was to find myself getting weaker aud
weaker, till the last drain of vitality had
ebbed away. If I was sitting on a chair I
had to catch hold of something to raise my-
self up, and for two weeks I was helpless in
bed, and with a very remote chance of ever
getting up again. A doctor was in daily
attendance, but the medicines which be
prescribed for me were not helpful in the
least, and I really thought that nothing
would cure me.”
“ Your present robust appearance proves
that you were wrong in that belief.”
“Of course it does,” was the hearty re-
sponse, “ but I have to thank Clements
Tonic —not any other medicine for being so
happy and healthy for the past few years.

Invalids
. AND
THE AGED.
“P.A.,” discoursing on Barmaids, takes
a very extreme view :—
Half a lifetime passed in hotels convinces me that an in-
tending'barmaid might as well, asarule, take to the “town ”
at once. Let a woman be ever so virtuous physically, the
degrading influence of a bar sullies the purest minds. No
woman should be exposed to the filth and profanity of a
bar-room conversation. The smell of smoke and spirits
have a terrible effect on women’s nerves and they awake
after a late, “ busy ” night, so shattered that brandy-and-
soda before breakfast is absolutely necessary. And the
brandy course once begun, the downward road is easy.
The girl who spends her youth and beauty in such a
service is, in 10 years, either on the town or
taking in washing. Bung has probably used her
as his mistress and worked her 15 hours a day.
Then, when drink has a heavy hold on her, and
her good looks go, she is cast aside like an empty bottle.
I have known girls of 20 or so to take a daily bottle of
spirits, with occasional wines and beers as well. Others
I have known who drank, with customers, as much
as 25s. worth per day. They have to vary the drinks to
keep reasonably sober, so the mixture by the end of the
day would mean whisky, benedictine, all kinds of wines,
sarsaparilla, quinine, soda, lemonade, absinthe, gin, beer
and stout. They must drink with customers, for some
men have such a hoggish desire to show off that they
will be offended if the barmaid refuses, and com-
plain to the “boss” that she is stuck-up; so the girl’s
very living depends upon making a slush-tub of her-
self Of course, the life is often looked upon as one of
pleasure, gaiety and ease, but even the greatest fool of a
girl soon gets tired of the brilliant conversational efforts
of the boozy imbeciles who call her “ my darling ” and
tell her azure stories. No one but a contemptible coward
would take advantage of a girl’s helplessness to insult her
thus, but the hotel world is full of such dastards. No
one but “ mugs and outsiders call barmaids “ darling."
The real “ boy ” is always the quietest of the gang. He
gets his work'in after the show, so does not need to make
a fool of himself before people. However, apart from the
degradation, sweating, and ..often starvation of our
women, the sale of drink should not be made any more
attractive than necessar}'. The law forbids saloons to be
made merry by music, therefore they should not be made
attractive by thg suggestion of licentiousness which
women’s presence offers. Fifty per cent, of drunken-
ness is due to this attraction. If whisky were sold
down in a damp,, mouldy,, candle-lit cellar, in tin
pannikins, served by niggers or blear-eyed boozers, its
consumption would go down about half. The barmaid is
a lure and a snare, who goes down to destruction with
her prey. A man who dared to put a girl in a bar in
U.S.A. would have his saloon torn down about his ears,
and would be considered as low as if he took money for
her prostitution. Of course, it may be said that a woman
who is degraded enough to serve drinks in a saloon, listen
to the filthy talk, find inhale its second-hand cigar smoke,
is not fit for anything else, but it is the duty of the law
to protect her from the consequences of her own folly.
Robert Brothers writes of Port Augusta
The smelters started work at Port Augusta on Sept. 27.
I am waiting to see whether this will lift the blighted
town to its feet and put a little stiffening * nto
column. Geographically, Port Augusta should be t
capital of South Australia, and so it would have been, i
think but for the sandy desert to the west. Things were
promising here once, but local enterprise was like a
foolish man who built his house on the sand. Port
Augusta built too many houses on that shifting founda-
tion. Yet the people have not ceased to hope, relying on
the chance of a transcontinental railway passing througn
th In one-fourth the houses are empty, and
in various stages of collapse. For protection, some are
boarded up at the windows and doors, and some are
nearly falling ; some are down. As the houses, so are the
fences. Apathy has fastened on the place, and you can
almost see a Presence which broods and broods, rort
Augusta nourishes unswerving hatred to Adelaide and its
centralising influence. On every hand you see tokens of
retrogression—from the man who discourses to you ot
what has-been to the falling cement on some of the best
buildings. The country roads stretch out, spread deeply
with sand ; and no vehicle seems to make use of them.
Sand, sand everywhere, and not a blade of grass. There
are sparse green bushes, and sparse wattles, and the sand
spreads away to the feet of the black and ragged Flinders
Ranges. Wandering among those sand heaps I discovered
the future of Port Augusta. I was in what seemed a
park or paddock, when my foot kicked something hard,
and I found that it was the top of a post of a wire fence!
A few paces further on I saw the top of a sculptured
stone, and I read “ Sacred to the memory I needed a
spade to read further. There were many buried head-
stones there —some more exposed than others ; and some-
times I found the spikes of railings showing an inch above
the sand. It was a buried burial-ground—with no
flowers, no cultivated trees—only sand, and native shrubs
that had sprung up after the burying-ground had been
buried. In that dreary spot methought I could see the
insidious sand-heap creeping over the whole of Port
Augusta ; and I trembled for the Port Augustans, whom I
knew were too slow to get out of its way.

The Quick and Lively Pill
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
John Strang ways and Jerry, his mate, left
Bandaloora with big cheques, the results of three
months’ fencing, and pitched a camp between two
billabongs, about three miles off an unfrequented
road.
John was making pills. “ When we get fairly
goin’, we’ll get a lot o’ circulars printed,” he said,
mixing up the bulk-pill on a sheet of bark. By
his side was the prescription—“ known only to
Dr. Strangways ”: —
One dish flour,
2 tablespoons quinine,
2oz. croton oil.
Mix up with the water of boiled gum leaves.
“By’n’bye we’ll be able to take a hoffis, Jerry,
an’ ’ave a brass plate on the door: * Dr. Strang-
ways may be ensulted at all hours.’ We’ll adver-
tise in the noos-papers then, an’ print cerimonyals
from grateful suff’rers.”
“ S’pose we don’t get any ? ” asked Jerry, poking
some boiling gumleaves down in a kerosene tin.
“ Yer ain’t got no more intellect nor a goanna,
Jerry. Why, there’s scores o’ blokes wot’d swear
ceremonyals to order jes’ for a chew o’ terbacco ! ”
John cut the pill-stuff into segments, and Jerry
made them round by rolling them between his
palms. Then he dipped them into a tin of cochi-
neal, to color them, and finally laid them on a
sheet of bark to dry.
It took them a week to make a thousand.
“Even that’s five quid,” said John, hopefully.
“When we get machinery, we’ll do much better.”
“They ain’t sold yet,” Jerry said.
“No trouble to sell ’em out-back,” said the
doctor, confidently. “You’ll go ahead o’me to
the stations, an’ camp at the huts. Incident’ly
yer’ll mention how you was suff’rin’ somethin’
awful from inderjeschen, lumbaygo, rooinatics,
hydattis, backache —an’, above all that tired
feelin’. By accident, yer runs ag’in a trav’lin’
dootor. You could scarcely crawl at the time,
you was that bad. His name was Strangways—
Dootor Strangways, from Shercargo—an’ he give
you some of nis Patent Quick and Lively Pills.
Wal, says you, after talcin’ two boxes you felt a
new man; an’ after you’d swallered two more you
was better’n you was afore you took bad. You
was quite stunned by the magic work o’ them
pills. You b’lieve, says you, that this Dr. Strang-
ways once saved the life of an old Indian chief
while trav’lin’ with troops in the Punjab, an’, by
way of gratitude, he gave the doctor the formuliar
for them pills.”
Meanwhile John was writing on the boxes.
“Dose, 2 pils after a feed, an’ the rest o’ the
box when yer turn-in.”
“If them pills don’t’ave some effect on the
cove wot takes ’em I’ll be surprised. You only
want these fellers out ’ere to feel some effect after
talcin’, and the pills’ reppertations made. ”
“’Sposin’ that effect’s injurious?” Jerry sug-
gested.
“Don’t matter if it’s murd’rous. S’long’s they
feel some effect, they’ll reckon they’re bein’ cured.
Some real gen-u-ine pills is that mild # in their
effect they can’t be felt. Consequence is, they’re
put down as hogwash. Now, the principle o’ my
patent pills is that they’ll be felt. That, I say, is
the mainspring o’ the pill-trade. The next thing
is to make yer client b’lieve he’s real bad, an’ if he
don’t take pills he’ll be a blanlcy sight worse afore
he’s better. Size him up on the quiet, an’ if he’s
only gorra toe-ache or weak mem’ry, give a lecture
on giniril parralalusis, or something, an’ ring in
the toe-ache an’ weak mem’ry as symptoms.
That’ll make him find more symptoms, an’ he’ll
soon be dead sure he’s got sev’ral diseases that he
hasn’t. Thinkin’ of ’em will make him melon-
colly, which’ll be proof positive he wants tonin’
up. Then he’ll start physickin’ —first one thing,
an’ then with another, till he really is bad. We
mus’ get some books printed, Jerry. Fine thing
to make fellers sick.
“There’s some people wot thinks they can’t
keep ’ealthy ’thout bein’ a sort o’ walkin’ drug-
store. It’s not natch’rel for their bodily functions
to keep right if they don’t take something. Them
Patent Pills o’ mine’ll supply a long-felt want.
Takin’ of ’em will ease their minds. Remember,
when givin’ yer testermony, that they’re a splen-
did thing for that tired feelin’, an’ for melon-
lcolyer and bashfulness ; and so on. Spots afore
the eyes vanish after takin’ one box—etcettra an’
so forth.
“ Dagnose their cases, Jerry, an’ wotever’s the
matter with ’em, them pills is the very spesserfick
for it. Always tell ’em to beware of imitations.
None gen-u-ine without Doctor Strangways’
written signature on the lid.”
* * * *
Next morning Jerry was gathering gum-leaves
up the creek, when loud voices were heard at the
camp. He rushed out of the gully, and then
stood with his mouth open, peering from behind a
tree. About 20 shearers—cutting across country
from one shed to another —had struck the pill-
factory. They had overhauled the business, ap-
parently, and read the advertisements, for they
called John a low-down dog-poisoner, played
football with the pills, and dabbed the dough on
the doctor’s head ; and while some abused and
jostled him, others wrecked the camp. Jerry
bolted up the creek and hid in a hollow log.
At last the shearers went away—laughing
boisterously. Jerry hurried to the camp. He
found the doctor tied to a tree, stark naked, and
painted from head to foot with cochineal. He
walked round him, and looked him up and down.
Then he shook his head sadly. ‘ ‘ I’m afraid yer
in a bad way, John. Better take a pill.”
John glared savagely, and ground his teeth.
“ How’s yer pulse ? ” said Jerry, taking hold of
John’s ear. “ See any spots afore yer eyes ? Feel
tired-like, or melon-kolly ? ’Ave bad dreams, or
anything like that ? ”
“ Lemme go, blast yer ! ” John roared,
“ Mos’ chronic case I ever see,” Jerry con-
tinued, unperturbed ; “yer’ll want a hextra
pow’rful course o’ Quick and Lively pills ”
With a great effort, John burst the straps that
held him, and the next instant Jerry was lying
on his back with several teeth loose. John
rushed down to the waterhole to “transmo-
graphy” himself. He felt better afterwards, and
came back to apologise to Jerry.
But Jerry was gone—swag and all.
